THE BED PAGE.
THE NUN.
If you become a nun, dear,
A friar I will be ;
In any cell you run, dear,
Pray look behind for me.
The roses all turn pale, too;
The doves all take the veil, too ;
The blind will see the show:
What! you become a nun, my dear!
I’ll not believe it —no.
If you become a nun, dear,
The bishop Love will be ;
The Cupids every one, dear,
Will chaunt “ We trust in thee.”
The incense will go sighing,
The candles fall a-dying,
The water turn to wine :
What! you go take the vows, my dear I
You may—but they’ll be mine.
Leigh Hunt.
4
" Homer was the greater genius : Virgil the
better artist.” The phrase is of eternal applica-
tion. This man appeals to the intellect—that to
the emotions : rare indeed is he who feels deeply,
yet not more deeply than he thinks, and at the
height of his Art is able to communicate the
living touch that warms it into an ideal Nature.
* * * * *
In Alex. Montgomery’s stories the appeal is
almost wholly intellectual. He rarely excites
passion ; always interest and admiration of his
skill. He builds his tales as he might build a
chess problem, and the pleasure they bring is very
much akin to the pleasure attending a chess pro-
blem’s solution. He is the scieneed story-teller—
making infallible point after point, and clinching
all in a dexterous climax—as opposed to a
natural story-teller, like Becke, with his
transcripts of South Sea Island life in
which Nature is everything, and the narra-
tor’s art is confined to the recital of his vivid
emotional impressions. Montgomery knows
more : Becke has felt more. With Montgomery
it is the telling that most excites admiration :
with Becke it is the tale. Both men are good ; and
each is the better in his own way. Becke has no
style; his vocabulary is purely colloquial; he is
lost among irrelevancies and verbal excrescences ;
his ore continually needs purging from the dross.
Montgomery’s styleiscleanand finished ; of itskind
it is all but perfect; to cut a word, a line, would
be to lose an effect; he edits himself too severely,
perhaps, for there is a certain hardness in his
work, a want of atmosphere, of shade and contrast.
And the supremacy of his intellect has dulled his
emotions. Becke makes three-fourths of his capi-
tal from the simplicities and complexities of sex-
passion : the Woman is surprisingly wanting in
Montgomery. His humor, too, is rare and satur-
nine : Becke bubbles over when he has a good
thing. Nor is Montgomery ever carried away by
his story, as Becke is. Becke’s best yarns tell
themselves—the points are made by instinct—so
keenly responsive is the teller to comedy or
pathos. But Montgomery is less nervous and
more Northern, less natural and more cynical,
and remains always master of himself and his
subject—too much master, which is not good.
The essence of good fiction, as of good poetry, is
“ emotion remembered in tranquillity ’’—but even
in the tranquillity there should be 75 per cent, of
emotion. And Becke gets nearer this ratio than
Montgomery.
* * * *
“Five-Skull Island and Other Stories’’—which
is the title of Montgomery’s little book (posted
from this office, Is. 9d.) —gives hardly more than
a taste of his quality; and though his level is
always good,, it is not here always at his
highest. Nevertheless these fifteen lightning-
flashes on the men and manners of the Malay
Archipelago are well worth treasuring. Mont-
gomery does not, indeed, get the broadest
foundation for his work. His stories are still too
sketchy, too detached and objective, to be classed
with the best examples of the conte. No matter how
brilliantly scenes and phases are pictured, if they
be isolated the picture lacks permanence. To be
enduring, literary work must be brought into con-
tact with some primal fact of humanity : there
must be a universal “ moral ” in it. Not the
“moral” of the Sunday-school tract, dragged in
brutally in violation of perspective and pro-
bability, but the natural moral which, abstractly
considered, every incident of life has-for all things
living. Australian writers run too much to the
easy, detached, realistic sketch—not too much if
it be considered that they are young and learn-
ing, but too much if they are judged with-
out reference to local considerations, as artists
merely. Looking back over Bulletin contri-
butors, Mannington Caffyn is perhaps the
one who rose highest as an artistic story-teller.
There are others who have gained as great, or
greater results, but their work will not bear re-
reading so often. Of current writers, the clever
“ Alba Durion ” is one who approaches somewhat
to the ideal. He has something of the under-
current of meaning, the all-human reference, that
our dogma demands.
* * * *
And a consideration of the work of the best
short story-writers brings justification of the
dogma. The French Maupassant is justly con-
sidered a master —perhaps the master-of the
conte; and all his best tales have the asked-for
“moral.” There are scattered throughout his
dozen volumes detached sketches of excep-
tional brilliance— sketches which make, perhaps,
a greater momentary impression ; but it is not
these that one returns to again and again. It is in
the human stories—the stories in which you, the
reader, might be actor, and not the individual
stories, which depend on some peculiarity of
person or circumstances, which you, the reader,
do not possess or do not know —that Maupas-
sant’s power is greatest. Take an example from
scores which present themselves—“ Regret.”
* * * * -
It is an old man, and a wet day—a cold, dull, depress-
ing day in autumn. The old man gets up, presses his
nose to the window, ana shivers. The rain is drip, drip,
dripping down continually. Such a gloomy day 1 And
there are only gloomy days for the old fellow now—he is
sixty-two, and an old bachelor, and the joy has gone out
of his life.
The joy 1 But he has never had any joy. If he could
cherish the memory of something he had done ; if he had
had adventures, great pleasures, success, any sort of
satisfaction I But no ! there was something lacking in his
nature—a certain constitutional timidity had held him
back when his fellows went forward —he had never had to
struggle, but had eaten, and slept, and risen, and eaten,
and slept, and risen—day after day spent in the stagna-
tion of mediocrity ! And now—very soon—he wag going
to die —to die ! —and his life so empty of everything worth
living for 1
He had not even been loved. No woman had
slept on his breast in complete abandonment. He
knew nothing of the delicious anguish of expectation,
the divine thrill when the hapds touch, of
triumphant passion. Outside the rain dripped monoton-
ously : inside he sat by the fire in his dressing-gown—old
and cold. . . . Yea: his life had been a failure—an
utter failure.
Yet ... he had loved! -Secretly, sadly—as was his
nature —but how he had loved! It was his old friend
Mme. Sandres, the wife of his old comrade: she lived
opposite to him. Now —she was , . . yes, she must be fifty-
eight, at least. How pretty she used to be —so tempt-
ing, rosy, smiling, sweet 1 And old Sandres was never
the man for her. “ If she had loved m» long ago—if she
had loved me ! ”
He remembered the walks they used to take, the three
of them, along the Seine—close by. There was one day, one
afternoon . . . they had gone for a picnic . . . and old
Sandres had gone to sleep after lunoh . . . and Mme.
Sandres had taken his arm for a little stroll . . . how
he remembered it! There was something curious
about her that day —she leant on his arm, chattered,
said she was just drunk with the Spring—“just
drunk.” And he looked at her, and longed, and
courage failed him. He could not even speak 1 And
she—she made a garland \yith grass and water-
lilies, and put it on her head, and asked him, “Do
you like me like this?” And he did not answer—he
found nothing to say. And how curiously she had looked
at him when he suggested it was time to go back ! She
was so silent going back : she did not lean on his arm.
Why?
Could it be ... ? The old fellow tortured himself
till he could not rest.
He would go over—yes, he would go over now, and ask
her.
So he dressed himself, and went over the road in the
rain, and asked for Mme. Sandres. She came—disturbed.
Was he ill ?
No, he was not ill; but he wished to ask her some-
thing—he could not rest till he had asked her. Did she
remember that spring-day on the Seine, when Sandres
went to sleep after lunch ?
Oh, yes ; she remembered it well.
“Well . . . that day . . . if I had been . . .
if I had been . . . enterprising . . . what would
you have done?”
Mme. Sandres answered frankly: “ I should have
yielded, moil ami.”
Then the old man, with his head down, went out into
the street, through the pouring rain, and walked, and
walked, and walked. At last he found himself on the
spot where they had been the day of the picnic—the spot
he remembered.
He sat down under the bare, shivering trees and bunt
into tears.
» • * •
Tiiis mutilates Maupassant shockingly ; but that
is not the point. The point is that in every man’s
life there are missed joys- regrets—and conse-
quently this story has a direct, personal, intimate
appeal to every man who reads it. The motive
recurs continually—it recurred in analogous cir-
cumstances in last Bulletin Xmas Number,
where Arthur Watts, in the course of his story
“A Case of Affinity,” remarked that “a lost
opportunity is an awful thing to have on one’s
conscience.” It is just this crude, familiar sum-
mation which Maupassant has expanded into
artistic and permanent form. So in a hundred
other cases he takes a trait common to humanity
and around that twines his story.
* * * *
Take “ Le Bapteme” for another example.
The scene is in a country village, among rough
peasant-farmers. There is a christening, with its
attendant consecration and simple feast in which
the friends of the parents take part. As the
story opens, the men are gathered at the father’s
farm waiting for the women, that they may go in
procession to the church. “Women are never
ready in time.” The nurse comes out, bearing
the two-months child, bedecked with ribbons;
then the mother; and the procession Of rela-
tions and friends is formed, proceeding slowly
to the church, where the child is baptised.
The young priest is its uncle, the brother of
its father.
When the ceremony is over, the proces-
sion returns —all walking faster as they think
of the dinner. The nurse, a little tired, asks
the priest to take his little nephew for a
moment: and he takes the babe awkwardly, not
knowing how to hold it. Familiar peasants ask
jestingly : Isn’t he sorry he can never have one
like that? He does not answer, walking fast,
feeling a strange wish to hug his tiny burden.
When they are seated at dinner, there is a
continual fire of agricultural pleasantries about
marriage and offspring. The priest, used to the
rough manners of his parishioners, takes no
notice. He is seated next to the nurse and child,
and he considers the little human larva with a
reflective attention, feels' a sudden lively tender-
ness, a little sad, for this fragile being, the son of
his brother. The nurse is troubled by the
child, and the young priest again takes it
from her. There seems to him to be in the
helpless infant a mystery of which he has never
thought : he is astonished, profoundly moved
before this incarnation of a new soul, this great
wonder of a life beginning, of love awakening, of
the race of man continued eternally.
The dinner goes on ; and the diners get warm
and noisy. The child commences to cry. A rude
voice tells the priest to give it the breast, and the
witticism is greeted with a storm of peasants
laughter. But the mother comes and takes the
child to an inner room, soothes it to sleep, and
returns.
At last the feast is finished. The priest has
disappeared, but no one remarked his absence.
The young mother rises to see if her little one
still sleeps, going into the room on tip-toe; and
returns alarmed, declaring she has heard some one
moving there. All the men get up, drunk and
-threatening ; and the father takes a lamp and
hurries into the room.
He finds his brother, the priest, op his knees.near
the cradle, sobbing, with his head on the pillow
where rests the tiny head of the baby,
* * » *
And again the moral comes. The scene is no-
thing ; the persons are nothing; they are merely
the vehicles of a forcible appeal to the universal
sentiment of fatherhood.
* * * *
Of course, it needs Maupassant’s genius to get
Maupassant’s effects. Without his art there is
nothing. The subjects lie ail around us—it is the
workmen that are wanting. In the Brady-Phil-
lips murder, for example, there is material than
which Maupassant would demand nothing better.
To imagine the reason for the crime, the circum-
stances surrounding it, and then to make that
reason, those circumstances, so obvious that the
result would seem inevitable; to portray Phillips’
character, the workings of his mind, his actions,
his emotions before and after the crime; and
then ter show the embryo Phillips lurking in all of
us—dormant, dwarfed, or active—but necessarily
present in right of his and our humanity—this
would be a fascinating, an absorbing task for a
great artist. Well : we may grow one yet.

The eelebration of “ Sarah Bernhardt’s Day ”
in Paris on Dec. 8 was memorable indeed.
The great actress was first banqueted in the
largest hall in Paris, which yet proved in-
sufficient to hold the company. Sardou toasted
her as “ not merely the great but also the good
Sarah ” ; and she sobbed her thanks. Then came
a representation at the Renaissance theatre, which
she manages, Sarah taking two rbles to perfec-
tion; and at the close a scene which has fired
the pen of Lond. Times correspondent—
“the unequalled scene which seemed the con-
ception of a poet’s brain —I should perhaps say
some madman’s, if an entire city, one of the most
enlightened in the world, had not been joining
in it.”
Mme. Sarah Bernhardt is seated on a throne of flowers.
She wears a white dress, a light \ eil covering her head
From THE SNETON.
and at mo ents concealing her eyes, wet with tears. The
stage is a mass of flowers. Around her a throng of
Academicians, poets, authors vf oolii sexes, actors and
actresses join their acclamations to those of the public-
Then more flowers are brought. The Society of Student*,
by the mouth of its president, M. Tissier, is the first tc
greet her. The delegations of the schools of law and
medicine, of St. Cyr, of the Polytechnic and the Ecolt
Centrale occupy boxes, and by their acclamations cm
phasise the tribute of M. Tissier. Then come the poeti
Frangois Coppde, Oatuile Mendds, Edmond Haraucourt
Andrd Theuriet, and Edmond Rostand, who, one by one,
stepping forth from the groups, advance towards'Mine
Sarah Bernhardt to read each of them a sonnet in hei
honor. I cannot help quoting ten lines from the sonne 1
of M. Edmond Rostand, the young author of La Pnn
cesse Lointaine, not merely noticeable for their finely-
chiselled form, but interesting to English readers as
indicating the esteem in whioh is held the name of
Shakespeare : _
En ce temps sans beautc, seule encore tu nous restes,
Sachant descendre, pfi-le, un grand escalier clair,
Ceindre un bandeau, porter un lys, brandir un fer,
Reine de l’attitude et princesse des gestes. . . .
Avide de souffrir, tu t’ajoutas des coeurs ;
Nous avous vu couler—car ils ooulent —tes pleura,
Toutes les larmes de nos ames sur tes joues.
Mais aussi tu sais bien, Sarah, que quelquefois
Tu sens furtivement se poser, quand tu joues,
Les lfevres de Shakespeare aux bagues de tes doigts.
And—they know how to honor artists in France
• * * *
Reading “A Humble Enterprise,” one com-
prehends easily “Ada Cambridge’s” fixed femi-
nine vogue. It is a pleasant, unpretentious story
which tells nothing in particular, and tells it very
well. A Melb. millionaire’s clerk dies, leaving
his family ill-provided-for. The family sets up a
refreshment-room, where the attractive daughter
serves tea and scones. The millionaire’s family
patronises the tea-shop, and the millionaire’s son
marries the attractive daughter who serves the
scones. This is all the plot; the rest is clever
embroidery, done naturally, in good English, with
some skill of characterisation. It is not usual, of
course, for Melb. millionaires’ sons to marry tea-
and-soones girls; but the feminine mind still
clings to Romance, and probably this touch of
Romance pleases the author almost as much as it
pleases her readers. As fine Art, as strong Litera-
ture, one cannot praise the book; but in its
humble way, with boarding-school limitations
duly allowed for, “A Humble Enterprise” could
hardly be better.

Little items :
Mallee Cookie: “Re Kipling’s ‘gray Gothav’n speok-
shioner ’ (they pronounce it ‘ speckshoneer ’ in Dundee),
a‘speckshioner ’ is, or was, the name given to the first
harpooner of an Arctic whaler. One of his duties was to
superintend the ‘flinching’ (i.e., getting the blubber
from a captured whale), and the Dutch word for fat or
blubber is speck— hence, probably, the name.” Speck is
still a well-used German word for “fat”—is, in fact,
common Teutonic property. But its English use (good
dies, still give it as a current English word) is now almost
wholly restricted to nautical vernacular, where so
many antiquated words linger longest. “Speck,” says
Webster, is the blubber of whales or other marine
mammals ; also, the fat of the hippopotamus. Thus a
“speckshioner” is a blubberer—a “gray Gothav'n blub-
ber-cutter.” (‘‘Speck falls-” is nautical for the falls or
ropes used in hoisting the blubber, &c., aboard). . . .
R. R. Holmps, librarian at Windsor Castle, is compiling
a sumptuous life of Queen Victoria, to which the good
lady herself will contribute notes and reminiscences.
The volume will include illustrations of the most memor-
able incidents in her 60 years' reign—the frontispiece
b ing a representation of the late John Brown’s un-
paralleled legs. . . . “ A.C.” : To offer a prize for transla-
tion would simply be to invite inundation by stiff, stilted
incompeteuoies—like yours. Good translators can trans-
late for the pleasure and profit of a good translation. . . .
Percy drawings for the Jan. number of Pall Mall
Magazine are in point of draftsmanship no better than
some that, he has previously done, but the composition,
and especially the effort at idealistic treatment, give his
admirers a novel pleasure and his work a keener promise.
It is a pity the accompanying vers s are so flat and stale.
. . . S.M. Herald has lately sparkled with “ Watuba’s ”
conversational transcripts in the manner of W. Pett Ridge.
“ Waniba” is the pen-naine of H. I. Low (“ A.8.C.” of the
Herald s “ Fugitive Notes ”• column), and his work is fre-
quently bright and clever. .. . Capt. Mahan—the “In-
fluence of Sea-Power” man —is writing what he
will try and make the “final” life of Nelson,
whom he has already tackled in articles. . . . J.G.H.:
“Melb. Argus missed the point Id Gay’s “A Se*
Maiden ” as completely as it could. Even if the
poet had compared the maiden’s breath with a gale
‘ Only a very boisterous breath indeed should be brought
into comparison with a gale would not he a reasonable
objection for reason already pointed out. As it is, it is a
blunder.
Her breath in sweets doth more abound
Than fragrant equatorial gales
is plain enough. The idea of fragrance is not only

The Brumby’s Death .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas only last night I was leading them west-
ward
O’er hills bathed in moonlight, thro’ forests of
gloom,
Past reed-beds that sang by the deep water-
courses,
Thro’ thickets of starry-white jasmine in bloom :
My beautiful troop ! with their wind-toss’d manes
flying,
Their hoofs flashing fire as they wheeled on the
plain—
Ah ! never thro’ desert or bird-haunted forest
Shall I lead them in moonlight or shadow again.
It was only last night that we came to the clearing:
The blaze of the camp-fire —our halt in surprise,
And the whirr and the sting of the death-dealing
bullet,
The last maddened gallop, the fast-dimming
eyes.
Then I sank on the reed-beds, they fled in the
darkness
Still westward—their hoof-beats seemed ringing
my knell;
Was there one, do you think, gave a thought of
the leader
Who, stricken and helpless, lay still where he
fell?
I had led them of yore to the hills of grey granite,
I knew where the creepers hung thick o’er the
pass
That led to the vale in the heart of the mountains,
The clear, crystal river—the green slopes of
grass.
Ah, me ! those were days when we met in the
morning
And galloped in glee while the sweet breezes
sang,
And the echoes came up from the hollow, red
ridges
As over the gravel our hoofs lightly rang.
I can follow in fancy their flight thro’ the dark*
ness
Bereft of their leader, still hurried by fear ;
Will they wander till lights of some lonely out-
station
Shine out, or a horse-bell sounds far off, yet
clear ?
Will they turn then and, seeking the swampland
behind them,
Forget their wild panic in longing for me,
And hasten to guard me ?—for bright eyes are
gleaming
And swift shadows hasten past thicket and tree.
How weirdly the dingoes are howling around me!
The wings of a night-hawk brushed lightly my
mane;
The eagles will shriek for their feast on the
morrow,
But my troop will be with me nor leave me
again
Till these dim eyes grow bright, and far to the
westward
I lead them, forgetting this night and its fear.
They are slow—they are late—ah ! I would that
they hasten—
The stealthy night prowlers draw silently near.
Far away in the hills that have guarded so firmly
The granite-ringed pastures the wild horses
know,
They are feeding knee-deep in the grass and the
clover
While red grows the east from the dawn’s tender
glow.
And another as leader looks proudly around him,
Sleek-skinned and fleet-footed, well fit to be
head,
Whilst far in the reed-beds the eagles have
gathered . . .
One might have remembered as westward they
fled !
Queensland.
ETHEL MILLS.
One Australian University assistant in the
bacteriological department has been seized with
pronounced tuberculosis. There was no family
history of consumption, and, as all conditions
pointed to his having picked up the microbes in the
laboratory, he was advised to leave, and a post
found for him elsewhere so that he should suffer
no income loss by leaving. Only the healthiest
should take part in such work, for the microbes
coming there are very fine and large, and only too
anxious for business whenever even a common
cold makes a nice soft nest.
The individual who purveys the “ Literary
Gossip” for S.M. Herald occasionally betrays a
too-intimate knowledge of his native literature,
In his chronicle of the past year’s publications he
observes :
There was a fair amount of work published by Aus-
tralian writers during 1896. Among others we may men-
tion Lawson’s “ While the Billy Boils,” and “ In the Days
when the World was Round.” . . . Lillie Turner’s
“Lights of Sydney,” Louise Hack’s “When the World
was Round.”
The world is round for a good many people just
after the strangled-fowl season.
Australian slang, writes “ T. D.” to The
Bulletin, seems subject to frequent modification.
Four or five years ago the youth of the “ push ”
went down to Chowder and proceeded to “ shot ”
rocks. Now he merely “ slings ” them. Anything
which commended itself to him was “good iron”
—more than one good-natured policeman wore the
sobriquet. The correct term at present is “ all
right.” To “poke borak” is undoubtedly a de-
velopment of “ barrack,” for which some uncon-
vincing Bulletinist has found a far-fetched deri-
vation in a French dictionary.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 13, 1897.
A Great Function.
A few months hence Queen Victoria com-
pletes the sixtieth year of her reign. She
will then have decisively broken the record
of the two imbecile kings who, until lately,
were first and second on the British list in
the matter of longevity, and dreary con-
tinuousness, and protracted remaining in one
situation. They were a solemn pair, those
two old-time monarchs — George 111., the
blind lunatic from Germany, and Henry
111., the weak-minded son of a father who
died from over-eating, combined with over-
drinking and rage and beggary and unmiti-
gated disaster. Victoria, unlike them, pro-
mises to put up her record in peace and
quietness, and great rejoicings are to prevail,
if: they can be arranged, over that empire on
which the sun never sets and the night never
ceases, and from which the drought and the
plague never wholly lift. The greatest pagan
and Mohammedan state on this earth is to
illuminate, if circumstances admit, in honor
of the sovereign whose greatness rests partly
on the Bible, and largely on the Koran, and
partly on other things too numerous to
desci'ibe. The occasion may not amount to
much, and the cause of rejoicing may seem
somewhat inadequate, but these things can
pass. The mere fact that something is
longer than anything else is not so wholly
remarkable as to call for universal joy ; un-
usual length is a circumstance which Vic-
toria’s reign shares equally with the Amazon,
and the principal boa-constrictor, and the
1886 drought, and Speaker Abbott when he
is lying down, and Jawbone Neild’s great
speech, and Judge Windeyer’s ten-hour
charge to the Mount Rennie jury. Also, it
is a characteristic of the S.A. railway to Ood-
nadatta, and of the equator, and the long
threepenny beer and the time it takes to find
the body of the man who went a-missing with
Butler, and many other things.
* * * *
Nevertheless, the “record anniversary”
of Victoria’s accession is to be a great occa-
sion. The cable reports that all the Premiers
of all the British colonies have been invited
to London “ as the guests of the British
nation,” and other celebrations are in course
of arrangement. There is something gro-
tesque about this garish assemblage disturb-
ing any respectable lady in her declining
years, and interfering with her due allowance
of sleep, and reminding her indirectly of her
extreme old age by allusions to the 60 years
which lie between her and her youth.
There is something unpleasant in the idea of
all these varied politicians gathering together
to rejoice because a person who never did
them any harm has lived so long as to have
little chance of living much longer. Even
the clumsiest politician can hardly tell a lady
that he is glad she is so old, nor can he
decently say he is sorry she didn’t die
younger, so the situation is distinctly awk-
ward. Apart from the gathering of Premiers
there will probably be only a cheap and
second-rate rejoicing—in Australia, at all
events. Loyalty is at a comparatively low
ebb just now. It comes in with the loan-
moneys, and the land-boom, and the price of
wool, and the rise in wheat, and the rains.
And it goes out with the burst, and the
drought, and the depression, and the ticks,
and the bottom falling out of the frozen-
meat market. Loyalty is one of the luxuries
of prosperity, like champagne and mid-
night banquets ; the devotion of the com-
munity to its Queen can be measured
by its devotion to burgundy and Flossies
and cigars; and the dribbling of the rich
Rhine wine over its beard and its diamond
shirt-stud is a fair measure of its devotion to
the Throne and Person ; and the difficulty
it has in getting home to its marble hall
with the milk in the morning is in direct
ratio to its theoretical readiness to die for its
beloved sovereign. Australia is very, very
poor just now, therefore it isn’t loyal to any
extent worth mentioning. The tide went
out long ago, and Fink’s arrangement, and
Reid’s deficit, and the downfall of James
Muniio, and the dead-and-gone banks, and
the Men that are Dead are so many ripples
on the sand to mark its subsidence. The
last great evidence of loyalty in Australia
was the disappearance of the Victorian Par-
liamentary mace, the time some festive legis-
lators humped it to a house of ill-fame, and
left it there in a moment of drunk and dis-
organisation. Loyalty is partly indigestion
—partly gin and apoplexy—-mostly stomach,
and consequently pimples. It is a thing
which people get when they have much com-
fortable leisure, and don’t know what else to
do with it, and may as well spend it in being
loyal as in anything else. The community
which suddenly falls from affluence and has
to hustle hard for an existence isil’t loyal.
* * * *
If the seven Premiers of Australasia all
accept the royal invitation one result is cer-
tain—the politics of ’9B will be vastly different
from those of ’97. Individual Premiers have
gone ’Ome before, and fallen from grace, and
accepted titles and distinctions, and damned
Chicago —but never yet in this country’s his-
tory have seven Premiers fallen all together.
It will be a fearsome sight—the downfall of
seven Premiers all in a row—and yet it
is a perfectly possible event. No Australian
Minister that The Bulletin remembers
has gone to England, and been officially
welcomed, and has associated with the British
peerage, without falling from democracy.
There are many reasons for this, especially
in the case of the purely local politician to
whom Britain is a new world. The vast
population —40,000,000 people compressed
into two little islands—is tremendously im-
pressive after the emptiness of this great,
lonesome continent. The old age of the
country, its traditions, its wealth—the visit-
ing politician of any note never sees Spital-
fields and Hoxton and all the other slums of
all the dismal cities, and to those who only
haunt the wealthy regions of England its
wealth looks overwhelming—all these are
startling compared to the gaunt, bare, new-
ness of this country with its squalid tradi-
tions of the convict-days and of King Billy
and his gin. The masterfulness of J. Bull
strikes the stranger with 40 million Bull-
power. This result arises mainly from force
of numbers-—the masterfulness of 40,000,000
Germans would strike him in like manner if
he went under the same circumstances to
Berlin and Hamburg ; it is merely the result
of numbers, and the rush of countless feet,
and the uncountableness of the innumerable
top-hat, and the unspeakable abundance of
the living and of the tombstones of the dead.
Also, Britain is very fresh and green and
novel, after the dust-clouds that rise out of
the camel-tracks away at the back of Bourke,
and by comparison with the droughty plains,
and the deserts, and the miles and miles of
wire fence, and the evening-paper horror
about the prospector who died of thirst, and
the crow that ate his eyes, and the dingo
that gnawed his bones. These are some of
the more respectable things which bring the
wandering politician back with a deep rever-
ence for British institutions. As his rever-
ence makes Australia no less arid and unin-
viting and empty, and doesn’t alleviate the
crow or the dust-storm, it is a very foolish
sentiment, but it is one that has to be
reckoned with none the less.
* * * *
Further, the Australian politician, in the
main, has the same grovelling regard for
titles as any other man or politician, and an
insight into the place where titles are manu-
factured and distributed is to him an impres-
sive spectacle. In many cases the receipt of
the title per medium of a dry official letter is
sufficient in itself to bring about his down-
fall ; to gain personal access to the divinity
Which turns the handle of the mill that grinds
out noblemen, large and small ,
sized, is still more demoralising to his shaky
Democratic instincts. On this occasion, it .
almost certain, the “honors will be more
than usually large and lavish ; and the po -
tician who might hesitate to fa >} , I , nlls ■
is likely to have less scruples about falling in
company, especially with the glory of being
the guest of the British nation and having Ins
liquor-bill defrayed by the British treasury
to make him even more drunk and irrespon-
sible than he otherwise might be. It the
scheme works reasonably well, Australasia
will want seven new Premiers about this
time next year, and want them worse than it
ever did before. And what will happen m
the interim it is difficult to guess. There are
very few things which Britain could ask that
seven Premiers —all the guests of the Britis 1
nation —with their brand-new titles upon
them, and fresh from shaking hands with the
Royal family and from drinking with the
peerage, would not promise on the spot.
If the corpse of Imperial Federation was just
a shade less dead than it is, even that would
be capable of resuscitation at such a moment.
As it is, it should be a grand time for tighten-
ing those silken bonds which have, of late,
showed such lamentable signs of neglect,
and making serious arrangements about atiord-
ing special tariff concessions to the British
exporter, and giving another boost to the
naval subsidy, and laying plans for making
Greater Britain contribute to the land
defence of the empire, and for rendering the
Australian Federation as distinctly “ Feder-
ation under the Crown ’ as possible. And
in the wake of the seven Premiers—if they
all go—there will go a weird procession of
awful poets and majors and mayors and
Fitz Gibbons and small politicians, who will
make speeches and odes and other things,
and horribly advertise this hapless country,
unless it rises to repudiate them and all their
works.
* * * *
It will be something to be an Australasian
Premier in 1897. Therefore, the men who
just missed the billets — Stout, who reckons
that he lost Seddon’s place by a, hair’s-
breadth ; Frank Stuart, who was in train-
ing for the Yic. Premiership when the
unknown Turner sat down hard and im-
movably in the vacant seat; Byrnes and
Tozer, who saw the colorless, commonplace
Nelson put over their heads after they had
borne the heat and burden of the Boodle-
wraith ; M‘Millan, who let Reid have the
New South Wales Premiership more in irony
than earnest and has cursed him in vain ever
since—all of them will gnash their teeth with
a great gnash. And even if any of these
Premiers are gone before the event comes off
some other people will gnash their teeth over
the new ones just the same. It is a unique
opportunity —one which cannot come again
till about 1959 at the earliest, and by that time
the politicians of to-day will all be dead, or in
their dotage, or in the benevolent asylum, or
toothless and out of politics and remem-
brance, so to all intents and purposes it
might as ivell never come again at all. And
when the seven, or whatever other number
it may be, come back ! ! There are
great possibilities about that coming back.
Some may find their Governments vanished
into thin air—wrecked by an incompetent
or unlucky locum tenens in their absence.
Some will probably find their seats in pro-
cess of being jumped by their understudies.
Some will probably find their businesses or
their investments or their female belongings
“gone wrong” in their absence. All will
probably return burdened with promises
which looked very small when they were
given, and which look very large and awk-
ward when they have to be redeemed and
explained away and clumsily apologised for be-
fore a jeering country. And, worst of all, they
may never go at all. Even the most ardent
loyalist must admit that the life on which
the celebration depends is very old and
frail, and may flicker out at any moment.
On the very eve of the great proceeding
the whole jubilee may fall through, and the
seven men who never went be a subject of
mirth for ever afterwards. And the spectacle
of the Premier who didn’t start, sadly hump-
ing back his portmanteau from the mail-boat
where the dreadful cablegram, stating that
he wasn’t to come, overtook him, would be a
whole essay on the futility of earthly hopes
and expectations.
A Study from the Nude.
Undress the Australian race. Are the men
big-boned, broad-chested, firm-muscled, with
legs like twin towers, and a back that would bear
a bullock ? Are the women shapely, substantial
and exuberant with vitality ? . . . The Bulle-
tin stands at its mind’s window, and watches a
procession of three million individuals without
any clothes on, defiling down George-street
Sydney, to the Circular Quay.
* * * *
A mixed lot—is generalisation possible ? Such
an area so many heights—so many tempera-
tures—such varying humidity: the coast-dweller
and the man of the interior eastern plateau, the
sun-dried Queenslander and the ruddy Tas-
manian, the mother in 15 degrees south latitude
and the mother in 35 degrees— what deductions
can be drawn from conditions and denizens so
dissimilar? None, to cover all Australia and
the future; but there are some which rest
fairly established for the bulk of Australians in
the present.
' • * .
The procession passes interminably. . What
a weedy lot we are breeding in the great cities of
Melbourne and Sydney— pithless, sallow, with
pipestem limbs and narrow shoulders, alternat-
ing between fever and lassitude, ripened pre-
maturely. The coastal climate of S.E. Aus-
tralia is decidedly not an invigorating climate
for most of those who live in it. It strikes at
the root of strength by discouraging abdominal
respiration : the Sydney youth just flutters the
top of his lungs and keeps his chest-cavity con-
tracted. His blood is never sufficiently aerated :
his muscles grow rapidly flaccid, for the
enervating moist warmth indisposes him to
physical exercise. He rarely walks when he
can afford twopence for a seat on a ’bus-top :
wonderfully few of him play cricket in propor-
tion to the thousands who look on. Grown
older, the Sydney man dislikes hard labor,
prefers finger-work to arm-work. He drives
a parcel-van ; but the heavy drays, with
heavy lifts, are driven by imported Britons
and Irish. He is rarely big enough to be a
policeman. A great deal of shipping comes
into his port, but he finds a sailor’s life too full
of hardship. Sometimes he goes w harf-lumping,
but it is to carry nice light tea-chests for Gibbs,
Bright and Co., not heavy sacks of Maoriland
potatoes for John See. Work is very severe
in the gas-works : there are no Australians
there ; but you will find plenty in the printing-
offices and tobacco-factories. And it is much
the same in Melbourne.
* * * *
And the city girls and women ? Look at
them : plump at 15; “ going off” at 20; at 30
yellow and cadaverous in maidenhood, or cada-
verous and yellow in marriage; at 40 dowdy
and draggletailed, with an eternal tale of their
diseases. There are many exceptions, but that
is the prevailing impression one gathers from
the crowd of naked femininity passing before
The Bulletin’s mental window.
* * * »
And in the cities of Melbourne and Sydney,
exposed to the urban influences alvrays disas-
trous to physique, reside 24 per cent, of the
whole Australian population. The Govern-
ments of Victoria and N.S. Wales do their
best to keep them there by centralising the
civil service far more than is necessary; by bor-
rowing large sums of money of which the
metropolis gets a disproportionate share ; by
failing to encourage interior settlement, and
succeeding in encouraging people to loaf and
be fed on the coast; and by helping to make a
country life as dreary and uninviting as a city
life is pleasant and easy. Like many of their
people, the S.E. Governments want bracing up.
* • »
The procession passes with its clothes under
its arm. . . . Decidedly the countrymen and
country women are better-looking: their food
often wants variety, but they generally get
plenty of fresh air and exercise. The men
and women from the western selections
and squattages of N.S. Wales and Queens-
land are generically lean, lank, and tough:
in the South they are often as lank and
tough, hardly so lean. Many good specimens
of manly and womanly beauty lurk in favored
districts. The small lot of Westralians pre-
sents no identifiable characteristics. From the
w'hole survey one main fact seems established:
physiological reasons elsewhere urged against
the aggregation of population in cities have
more force, instead of less, in Australia. And
the economic reasons hold good too.
* * * *
A convention is soon to meet for the purpose
of making an Australian nation. But an Aus-
tralian nation is not to be made by any draft of
laws or set of principles. It can only be made
by strong fathers, sound mothers, healthy en-
vironment. “ Jinyus ! jinyus ! ” says Charles
Reade, “ take care o’ your carkus!” Australia
has plenty of genius, but its carcase leaves
much to be desired. That will never reach the
ideal, cr anywhere near it, until the city popu-
lation is driven forth to draw, like Antaeus,
new life from contact with the vital earth.
* * * *
The Bulletin will never be easy in its mind
until convinced that, man for man, the Austra-
lian race is physically as good as the best race
bred from human loins. This country is
rich and very thinly populated, and there are
several warlike nations who may cast a covetous
eye upon it when circumstances draw their
attention this way. The chances are that some
day Australia will have to choose whether it
will become the dependency of a foreign despot
or will fight that despot’s armies as long as it
owns a man to carry a rifle. Presuming that it
will fight, The Bulletin wants to know how it
will fight? Australians have plenty of pluck,
but pluck is not the only requisite in a soldier.
He wants good leers to walk fast and far; a
good stomach to digest hoofs, hides, and what-
ever may come unhandy when the commissariat
breaks down ; wind, and endurance, and the
power to match hand-to-hand any man that
may come against him. The Bulletin would
like to think the average Australian, especially
the average city Australian, has these requisites
of a good fighter, but, looking at the naked pro-
cession passing its mind’s window, it has con-
siderable doubts in the matter.
* * * *
So, putting two and two together, The Bul-
letin suggests that one of the first duties of
the Australian Federal Government will be
to compulsorily enrol every able-bodied male
to serve in a citizen army,- giving him a rifle
and ammunition, but no pay, and making him
drill at least three hours a w'eek for three years,
and intermittently after that so as to keep up
his training. The effect on the physique of the
race would be good and prodigious ; think what
regular drill would do for Premier Reid, from
whom this paper had to turn its head away as
he went past just now with his clothes in a
bundle. And the capacity of Australia to de-
fend herself from foreign invasion would
increase enormously. . . . The procession is dis-
missed : the Australian race can put its clothes
on.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Railway Myth.
R. E. O’Connor, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), whose legal
training and reputedly vast intellect should
qualify him for better things, throws in his lot,
in a mild sort of way, with the provincialists,
and announces himself as a Federal candidate
with the same limitations as most of the rest.
About three-fourths of the N,S.W. Federal


candidates stop short at the point where
O’Connor’s federalism breaks down ; they are
willing to federate almost everything except the
railways, but they draw the line there because
the N.S. W. railways pay better at the present
moment than those of any other province
except Westralia, and they assume that this
has always been the case—which it hasn’t ; and
that it is always going to be the case—which is
quite unlikely ; and that it is the natural order
of things—about which there is no evidence.
R. E. O’Connor, on this point, raises the same
stereotj’ped argument as all the rest:
The New South Wales railway system at present was a
paying concern, and It was in a very complete state. The
loss, if any, only amounted to a very small fraction. The
loss on the Victorian railways was 1.13 per cent., whilst
in Queensland the railways showed a loss of 1.23 per cent.
That on the South Australian railways was something like
3 per cent. Our railways were not only the best-paying
and best-managed railways in Australia, but, having got
through all this dead-way, as it were, must rapidly begin
to pay better in the future. The people of the colony
were now asked to pool their railways—to throw in their
paying concern —with others that were run at a loss.
The New South Wales lines are not, by a very
long way, a paying concern—they have only a
smaller deficit than those of some other pro-
vinces ; also, they are not “ the best-paying
railways in Australia ” by a big margin ; and
the South Australian deficit isn’t “ something
like 3 per cent.” but a good deal less than l
per cent. ; and the country expected something
better than this awful ignorance from a man of
R. E. O’Connor’s standing. Still, these are
mere details. The matter really at issue
is the stupid unfairness of New South
Wales politicians generally in their con-
stant harping on the railway question to the
exclusion of all others. R. E. O’Connor is
quite willing to amalgamate the Postal de-
partments and say nothing about it, though the
N.S. W. Post Office shows a deficit on working
expenses alone of <£70,000, while Victoria has a
profit of over £4OOO and S.A. of nearly
£50,000. Also there is a general willingness,
even among many people who take up R. E.
O’Connor’s attitude on railway matters, to
throw the public debts into a common heap,
and to say nothing about the fact that N.S. W.
has nine millions of debt which is represented
by no revenue-producing assets, against \ ic-
toria’s 2§ millions or rather less. The tele-
graphs are likewise, by unanimous consent, to
go into the common stock, and no reference is
ever made to the fact that the telegraphs in
N.S. W. have, in addition to the loss on work-
ing expenses, to pay interest on £866,000 of
borrowed money, while those of Victoria have
no such burden, and it is proposed to lump them
all together regardless of the mother province’s
£30,000 annual deficit for interest alone. The
great O’Connor idea, in fact, is that N.S.W.
should throw into the common interest all its
losing speculations, and keep its one reasonably
. profitable investment, the railways, under pro-
vincial management. And it is this sort of
policy which makes N.S.W. the aggressive J.
Bull of this continent and the chief obstacle
to Federation.
Jolly and Fat.
Tub first complete year of the great Foreign-
trade “ Jollv and Fat ” policy in N.S.W. ended
on 31st Dec., and the returns are beginning to
come in. Almost the first one is the melan-
choly wail of Sydney Benevolent Asylum
authorities who announce that 1896 was the
worst year in their experience, and the very
first line in the statement is a story of how
N.S.W. isn’t jolly and shows no signs of fat
whatever :
The report of the society’s operations for the year 1896
is of greater importance than that of any previous annual
record. The work in the interests of the distressed poor
has been of larger extent and of more varied character
than that accomplished during the history of the institu-
tion—now extending over 76 years
As the experience of the institution dates back
beyond the lifetime of most people now living
it probably speaks with authority on this
matter. Parson Boyce, who seconded the
adoption of the report, lent additional weight
to it by stating that “ he had never known a
year so bad as 1896.” The figures and facts
given certainly bear out these assertions. In
the outdoor relief department the average of
cases where assistance was given started in
January with 820 per week. Then it steadily
climbed upwards till in December the weekly
average was 1256. These were not per-
sons, but families ; the number of individual
destitutes receiving relief on January 1 was
2856, while on the 31st December they num-
bered 4337. This work represented the regular
business of the institution, but, in addition, it
ran for ten months a special unemployed relief
department, which began with 102 cases per
week in February and ended with 575 per
week in November. Then this department was
taken over by the State Labor Bureau, and its
doings are now judiciously buried out of sight,
but the record while it lasted was sufficiently
disastrous. These depressing figures deal with
outdoor-relief only ; they take no account of
destitutes received into the society’s institu-
tions. Also, they take no account of the fact that
the Benevolent Asylum is only one of 27 organ-
ised charitable institutions in Sydney alone.
The figures show, not only that 1896 was the
poorest, hungriest, and most miserable year
known in Sydney for a generation or two,
but that it went from bad to worse, and
ended in a much more lamentable state
than it began. And the society’s results
were arrived at despite the great efforts
of the Government to meet the increasing
distress by relief-works, mostly paid for
with borrowed money, and despite a
large exodus of population. In 1896,
for the first time in all the years covered by the
State statistics, the departures from N.S.W.
exceeded the arrivals, the loss being 842 against
a gain of 2559 in 1895, and 4299 in ’94. There-
fore it seems that, whereas up to the beginning
of the first year of Reid’s great Foreign-trade
policy N.S.W. was so much less distressful
than its troubled neighbors that population
continued to come in from adjacent lands, in
1896 it grew to be so much worse than its
neighbors that it became a remarkably good
place to get away from. The loss was not on
the oversea trade, but through a steady exodus
to the three adjacent protected provinces—Vic-
toria, S.A., and Queensland. These, however,
are only scraps of information ; the bulk of the
1896 records won’t be available till the State
statistics are issued, a good many months
hence. But, so far as they go, they show
that nothing very good has happened to N.S. W.
under its new Foreign-trade regime, and that
the alleged inrush of population from the adja-
cent provinces to get into the Promised Land
of Shoddy and away from the curse of Protec-
tion is a blatant lie ; and that the policy which
was to abolish the unemployed has rather in-
creased them ; and that the good time which
was coming hasn’t come, and that G. Reid is
only dust and ashes and imposture.
“ Lex ” to The Bulletin :
The late railway accident at Sydney station is an exam-
ple of only some of the risks run by railway passengers
under the present regime. Fortunately, no one was
seriously injured, and the latest swindle worked on the
N.S.W. travelling public lies hidden in the statute-book.
How many people in the province are aware that, under
no circumstances, can damages greater than the paltry
sum of £2OOO be recovered from the Railway Commis-
sioners for any injury sustained on the railway ? Yet such
is the fact. In Oct. last year the Commissioners, aided
by the indifference of Parliament, obtained the most
scandalous Act ever passed in the province. Sec. 2 of 60
Vic., No. 21, enacts that “ No larger sum than two thou-
sand pounds shall be recoverable in any action, either under
the Act 11th, Victoria No. 32 (this is Campbell’s Act,
which provides for compensating the families of persons
killed by accident), or otherwise, against the Railway
Commissioners of N.S. Wales, as carriers of passengers,
in respect of injuries sustained after the passing of this
Act, by any person while being carried upon any railway
or tramway vested in the Commissioners, or while being
in, upon, or about any station, yard, land or premises
vested in or used by the Commissioners.” The Commis-
sioners may now kill or maim anybody at a cost of £2OOO
—a sum insufficient to provide for the family of any
worker in the community. Two thousand pounds at 4
per cent, (which interest cannot now be realised on abso-
lutely safe security) will only return 180, and this is what
N.S.W. Parliament in its wisdom and with its high ideals
of justice considers sufficient compensation for a family
robbed of its bread-winner. A reference to Hansard will
show that the Bill, as presented to Parliament, contained a
provision making it compulsory on the Commissioners to
insure passengers at a small rale ; but some wiseacre ob-
jected to it, and it was sacrificed. A noticeable feature
about this Act is the silence concerning it and its pro-
visions which was maintained by the “free” press of
Sydney. As far as the writer can remember not one word
was said or written against it. The Act has come to stay,
and will be law for many a year ; for the most difficult
thing to get any Parliament to do is to repeal an unjust
Act which finds favor amongst “ persons with influence.”
[lt would probably surprise “Lex” to learn
that there are some very good reasons why the
sufferers by a State railway accident which
happens in defiance of reasonable precautions
should not be compensated at all ; there are
many why a limit should be set to the eccen-
tricities of jurymen dealing with the public
funds. The Commissioners have nothing to do
with the payment ; it is the community that
pays. The railway traveller can insure his
life just as a steamboat traveller has to do.
When the community explicitly announces that
it will be responsible for £2OOO and no more,
the conditions of the contract between itself
and its individuals are perfectly fair and above-
board ; and if any individual doesn’t like the
conditions he need not travel on the railways.
—Ed. B.]

Cardinal Moran announces himself definitely
as a Federal Convention candidate for N.S.W.,
and the yellow pup of sectarianism may be ex-
pected to howl shortly, and the Holy Roman
and the bitter Orangeman to reach for each
other’s hair in the sacred cause of unity. Mr.
Moran’s interference will probably add all the
cussedness of religious prejudice to a question
which was sufficiently tangled up without any
such addition; and, judging by his published
sentiments, he promises to be a weak and miser-
ably incapable Federationst as well as a cause
of strife. The Cardinal suggests —not forcibly,
as one having authority, but feebly like a
bruised reed leaning up against the wall—that
the railway systems may possibly be kept
separate for 12 or 18 years, and that each pro-
vince may perhaps run its own separate fiscal
system for the same period or thereabouts—in
other words, that the border tariffs and the
five or six or seven separate fiscal pro-
blems should rage along just as usual,
and the differential railway rates should do
likewise, and the break of gauge become a
bigger question every day, and there should be
unity without anything being united. Cardinal
Moran may be fairly set down as almost the
most hopeless candidate on the job up to date.
A man who vaguely believes in a Federal
railway system, yet suggests postponing unifi-
cation for 18 years, and who proposes an alleged
Federation with half-a-dozen different fiscal
policies raging furiously in its midst, is a unique
combination of how to do it as badly as possible
and how not to do it at all Also, his Emi-
nence doesn’t suggest how the Federal re-
venue is to be raised if the Federal Govern-
ment is to have no control over Customs re-
venue ; he seems to have no opinion at all
about the Federal Constitution or the public
debt —in fact, his whole policy, so far as it is
announced, consists of the proposal that Sydney
shall be the capital and the suggestion that the
most urgent matters shall be postponed for 18
years or so, and the rest is dead silence and
platitudes. Cardinal Moran is unique as being
the one candidate up to date, barring Joseeii
Abbott, who offers nothing at all —except his
blessing and the inevitable sectarian row that
comes behind him, and who is all negation and
apostolic gorgeousness. And between Abbott,
who relies for public support on the fact that he
is long, and Moran, who does the same because
he is bright-red, the Federal business is getting
into pretty deep water. The Moran Federal
policy is—“ Ah, it’ll all come right, plaze God.”
The old Federal Council, which has just held
its seventh meeting in the eleventh year of its
precarious existence, looks even less real than
usual this time. In its early stages the F.C.
professed to have some belief in itself. It met
once a year, and talked about things with a
certain amount of energy. Now it meets every
two years—-it would probably do it at much
longer intervals only a clause in its constitution
sets forth that if it lets two years pass without
being officially heard of it shall be presumed to
be dead and shall meet no more. So in the
last days of the last year of grace the Federal
Council crawls together to inspect itself and
see that it is still living, and holds a meeting or
two about nothing in particular, and thereby
provides for keeping itself nominally alive
for another two years, in case it should
want itself —which is possible, but not likely.
The fact that the Federal Council now only
gets together at the very last moment to save
itself from extinction is a sufficient evidence
that all public interest has gone out of the
business. The recent suggestions of Queens-
land Attorney-General Byrnes for putting fresh
life in the dry bones hardly attracted the
notice of anybody. Nobody could revivify
these bones, and nobody wants to, and hardly
anybody is even aware now that there are any
bones, or that there ever were any, and nobody
cares much whether there are any bones or not.
“ Died through being a dry thing talking itself
to death about nothing in the wilderness ”
would be a respectable epitaph for the late
Federal Council.
Writes “ Marie Blaas ” :
“ Let the city consume its own smoke ! ” This par-
ticularly applies to the social wreckage produced by the
economic system of society, which creates and then
punishes its victims for being what they are. For a
number of years now N.S.W. authorities have found it
convenient to play battledore and shuttlecock with the
unemployed. They first deny the existence of this body,
and then give themselves the lie direct by establishing
and carrying on a Labor Bureau to provide them
with laborious work at sweaters’ wages, and call it
charity. Some thousands of the workless have been
given free passes and then shunted into the
bush—to beg, thieve, starve, or die under a gum-tree.
The bush towns have been burdened for years to support
a floating population mostly from the city. Butchers,
bakers, and storekeepers are all bummed to death to
supply great numbers of men with the necessaries of life.
The undesirable element —gaol-birds, deadbeats, and
sneak-thieves —is pretty numerous. No camp or tent is
safe from their depredations. To return from a long and
hard day’s work to And tucker and clothes missing is a
common experience. This can be fully appreciated when
the nearest township is often 20 to 40 miles distant. For
the State to shirk its duties and responsibilities and to shift
its own load on to the shoulders of the bush towns is the
essence of injustice. Most of the squatters have ceased to
give rations, knowing that they can get men from Sydney
without going to the expense of keeping travellers.
Three-fourths of the men employed in sheds are cockies’
sons, who take up land with the ostensible object of
making a living out of it, but, instead of cultivating it,
compete in the labor-market with the nomadic worker.
In fact, the swagman has outlived his usefulness and is
no longer required, being an eyesore who is despised and
looked down upon with contempt. “ Let the city con-
sume its own smoke.”
The action of the N.S.W. Labor politicians in
putting forth a party ticket and nominating ten
party candidates for the Convention looks like a
ghastly mistake. Assuming that the ten get in
—and it is presumable that the party wants to
get them in or it wouldn’t have nominated them
—they are responsible to each other and to
their party for each item of their programme.
There seems to be no room for modification or
concession ; it is their whole programme or
nothing, and the party which demands all or
nothing generally gets nothing—also, it often
prevents other people getting anything. The
Labor ticket demands that there shall be an
ideal Democratic Commonwealth from the very
beginning, or else no Commonwealth at all.
Australia must either get the perfect article
all at once or never get anything. If this
country could construct a Central Government
which was just a little better than the
average of the Provincial Governments,
and which had reasonable possibilities
of improvement, then it would have gained
sometliing, but the Labor-party isn’t taking
that view. It demands that the people who
can’t make a perfect local Constitution should
make a perfect Federal one, or go without.
If there was any promise of ever getting a per-
fect Provincial Government, then there would
be some reason in rejecting the Federal
Government for not being perfect, but there is
no such prospect. F'urther, there is no pro-
spect that the rejection just now of a partially
imperfect Federal system will bring a perfect
one any nearer. As matters stand, the
spectacle of a party going to discuss a great
national question, and announcing in ad-
vance that there is no room for discussion —
that its programme is cut and dried, and
must be accepted whole, or it will, as
far as possible, shut down on the entire
business, also creates antagonism. If every
individual member of the Labor-party had come
forward as a candidate on his own principles
and his own responsibility, it would have mat-
tered nothing. But the party programme and
the 10 party nominees looks like woefully bad
generalship. The Labor-party puts forward a
remarkably good and clear and liberal policy,
but when it announces that it will surrender
nothing—that it “ insists ” on everything, even
the name to be given to the new combination—
its principles are a great deal better than its
generalship.
The American struggle to popularise religion
and keep the fat parson’s table well supplied
with buckwheat cakes and m’lasses is yearly
growing harder. The most orthodox of wor-
shippers now take their lectures on current
topics mixed with arias by operatic stars. And
the less orthodox well, how their five-cent
bits are gathered in is told by Rev. W. B.
Hale in Dec. Forum. The American church is
rapidly being turned into a circus. To raise
cash last year, for example
St. John’s Methodist Episcopal Church, Toledo, gave a
stimulating entertainment by the Peak Sisters, intro-
ducing that touching- ballad, “ Do You Know the Mouth
of Man ?” in which the gentle art of kissing is referred to
90 times.
At Fredonia, New York, the Sacred Female Minstrels
only corked and wore bloomers ; but at Woodside, Long
Island, one of the holy band kicked a tambourine held
above her head -all for the love of (lon, and dollars.
In the Trilby Social given by the Suffren Methodist
Episcopal Epworth League and the New Brunswick Pro-
testant Episcopal Olive Branch Society, the young ladies
of the church displayed their legs behind a curtain lifted
to a height described as “ tantalising.”
Few cities or towns have this year been without a
Bicycle Service. Floral wheels make appropriate decora-
tions, and if the organ has a Swiss Bell stop it may be
appropriately played. A favorite text is Psalm lxxxiii.,
13, “ O my God, make them like a wheel.” (Hebrew
galjjal, a whirling thing).
The pastor of the Methodist Church of St. Louis,
Michigan, having entertained firemen, veterans, and
blacksmiths, outdid himself in a Barbers’ Sunday Even-
ing. Scissors, hair-dye, cups, soaps, brushes and combs,
mirrors and washes, tastefully arranged on the walls and
platform, with festoons of towels and rosettes of brillian-
tine and bay-rum bottles, gave a homelike appearance to
the church ; sitting in a barber’s chair the pastor gathered
inspiration for his lecture, and then, rising, he pressed
home in the choicest terms of the tonsorial profession the
lesson of the razor and the strop.
At Otsego, Michigan, the pastor gave Bdols. every night
for a week to decipherers of his text, printed on the bill
like this : —“ Text for Friday evening : Eodht anrfo ehfte
litbso enout awsol belet eosfo dgons hdlse duhct hsupd
wteha rhaet vloen mawlh.”
And so on ad infinitum. A few remarks are
obviously needed just here, and The Bulletin
cordially invites the spirit of John Knox to
step forward.

Coercion Byrnes’ Federal Council proposal
that the Council be made elective was only
upset by the casting vote of the President
given in accordance with the Standing Orders.
It was a cunning device calculated to take the
wind out of the Federation sails, and was so
skilfully handled that it would have been
carried by a big majority but for the fact that
most of the Federal Councillors expect to be
Convention delegates, and were afraid of mini-
mising their chances of election by supporting
the proposal. Queensland evidently desires to
wreck the ship it refuses to sail in because it
doesn’t carry a black crew.
Song- of the New Patriots.
Raise the Flag of Prudence, boys,
Let our cry be “ Wait ” ;
New South Wales must boss the show
When we federate.
“ Australia for Australians ” sounds
Right as right can be ;
But we’ve got to look at home—
New South Welshers, we.
New’ South Welshers, we, my boys,
New South Welshers, we ;
Proudly let us guard the name—
Keep from Uitlanders the fame —
Ours from Botanie.
Raise the Flag of Prudence, boys !
Victorians, so spry,
Joined with South Australians
Wring us soft-heads dry.
Diemen’s-landers with them too !
Where would our chance be ?
Smash the federation trap—
New’ South Welshers, we.
New South Welshers, we, my boys,
New South Welshers, we ;
Proudly let us guard the name—
Keep from Uitlanders the fame—
Ours from Botanie.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

Motto for N.S.W. Mounted Rifle team :
“ Nemo impune Lassetter.”
“ Butler waited for at the Golden Gate”
was a Sydney newspaper heading over the cable-
gram stating that the detectives were “laying for”
the Swanhilda at San Francisco. It sounds just
the sort of gate where he would be waited for, too.
A difficulty in surgical operations aided
by Rontgen photos, is that the photo, doesn’t
show how deeply an intruding object is embedded.
W. D. Filmer, an electrical engineer in N.S.W.
Railway Dept., has ingeniously surmounted the
difficulty by taking two photos, at different visual
angles and combining them in a stereoscope, which
brings out the perspective admirably.
The Australasian Photographic Review
begins to be a really creditable and desirable
magazine. The January number delights eyes
and taste by two photo.-reproductions in parti-
cular—an admirable picture of a lady by John-
stone, O’Shannessy and Co., of Melb., and a more
pictorial study by Falk —in which an eye blurred
against the background is a flaw. A. J. Relph,
of Melb., has found a fine old bullock-driving sub-
ject, but a mistake was made in keeping the
bullocks so close that they are merged in the main
figure. The literary matter is of fair interest.
The Electric Photo.-Eng. Co. and Batson and Co.
deserve credit for the fine engraving and printing
of the special blocks : the printing of the ordinary
pages is not so good.
