Frank Thornton and the “Strange Adven-
tures of Miss Brown ” filled Sydney Gri. to over-
flowing on Saturday. “ Brown ” —the full title is
too long for ordinary, everyday use—is in three
acts, of which the first is rather slow and introduc-
tory, while the other two are fast and furious and
complicated. The plot can be written on the
back of a postage stamp, if the stamp is large
enough. A giddy captain abducts a girl-ward in
chancery from Cicero House Academy, and the
natural consequence is that, half-an-hour after
their marriage, the police recapture the bride and
take her back to school, and the bridegroom flies,
disguised in woman’s clothes, from the fury of the
Lord Chancellor who is after him with a warrant.
Then, in his lone despair, he decides to recover
his wife or burst his corset in the attempt, and
his accomplice, the Major, leaves him at the
academy as an orphaned female of deficient
education, and promises to send a cheque for her
first quarter’s schooling. The cheque never comes,
neither do Miss Brown’s other clothes, but that
is a mere item. The second act leads up to the
elopement; the third shows a sullen, dishevelled,
fighting Brown back in handcuffs at the ladies’
academy, with her skirt torn and her hair any-
how, and sulking muddily in a corner under the
severe disapprobation of everybody. The bride
got off in the carriage that was in waiting, but her
Brown stayed behind to fight out a chance encoun-
ter with a policeman and a German music-master
and to cover the retreat. In the conflict the long-
haired German is bashed out of all semblance of
respectability, and the policeman and Miss Brown
have about eleven tooth-and-claw fights in as
many separate ditches, and run several miles
across country. But two more policemen arrive
and overwhelm the orphan girl; and but for the
sudden death of a rich relative which raises her to
the peerage, and mollifies the Chancellor, there is
no knowing what might happen to her.
Some things in “ Brown ” are not quite so
new as they might be. The story of the captain’s
great joke in India is lifted bodily from Kipling,
and the brown schoolgirl from Demarara is collared
bodily from Thackeray. Also, there is a suspicion
that a good deal more fun might be made out of
Miss Brown’s embarrassment while she is still
trying to be a modest girl-captain at Cicero House.
Still, “Brown ” is a hilarious joke, and should do
well. Thornton’s girl is a luridly amusing
character, and her struggles with her skirt and
her umbrella, and the impossibility of finding
her pocket, and her tendency to take off her
hair and wave it in salutation, and her weak
yielding to the cuddlesome tendencies of the
sentimental brown girl from Demarara are
well worked out. The brown girl, played by
Miss Bessie Rignold, is a lady who calls for
much commendation. When The Bulletin last
saw her, Bessie Rignold was nothing in particu-
lar ; now she is a very clever actress of great
promise. Miss Meta Pelham, Fred Shepherd as
the designing Major, and Miss Honey Seabrooke,
are all good, though the latter isn’t an overwhelm-
ing success all the time, and Herbert Terry’s
German is a fine, soulful, eccentric Herr of the
deepest dye. Guyton Heath’s detective is the
weakest feature of the co. ; he isn’t much of a
detective to begin with, and when he has finished
his eighteenth round in the ditch with Miss
Brown he looks a torn agricultural person with an
everyday air of turnips. Still the detective is
only a circumstance, and as the schoolmistress
may be fairly balanced against him, things are
about even, and no one can complain much.
Besides, the outbreak of Cicero House in its snowy
night-dress, and with its hair hanging down its
back, is sufficient to compensate for many dozen
policemen.
** * *
That good old hunk of woe, the “Lights
of London,” helps to fill in the latter end of the
Rignold season at the Sydney Royal, and does it
with as much success as could be reasonably ex-
pected. This paper doesn’t pretend to any
great affection for the “ Lights ” or any other
drama of its kind, but in its own bulky way that
piece fills a place which probably wanted filling.
It is distinctively the pig’s-trotter drama—the
play of the costermonger, and the pie man, and
the baked-potato can-man, and the hokey-pokey
man, and of the person with the consumptive
cough under the bridge. The story is given to
long stoppages, in the course of which a London
street scene is turned on, with a struggling mass of
’Arry and his donah, and the ha’penny ice-cream
man barracks irreverently at the hot-spud man,
and the bloater man pokes borak at the saveloy
man, and ’Liza, coming out of the gin-shop with
threepenn’orth in a bottle, foregathers with Sal,
and Bill gives back-talk to the policeman, and
there are many blokes and ooves, and finally some-
one hooks a wipe amid great confusion. Also, Jim
is frequently seen buying some sandwiches for his
gal, and an old woman lifts up a cracked voice
from underneath a poke bonnet and wants some
dog’s-nose on tick. These things have no direct
bearing on the story, but the bloater and the bloke
and all the rest are supposed to lend it local color
and human interest. The “Lights of London”
has got the bloater and the bloke and the pig’s-
feet very badly indeed.
* * * *
George Rignold does his old character of
the slim young hero in very much his old way,
and there is nothing especially good to be said
about that person, except where he makes his
great dive into the tank. Plimmer, Sweeney,
Miss Emily Hughes, and Miss Ada Guildford
also do good work, and manager Marcus lifts
up a very tuneful voice in song. But nobody
gets much of a show in Sims’s huge drama, partly
because of the forty or fifty other people who get
into their road, and partly because they are much
interrupted by the pig’s-feet and the baked-’tater
man, and partly because they are overwhelmed
and snowed under by the tank. The tank-scene,
which is a very beautiful and elaborate affair, is
still the crowning glory of the show, and when
George Rignold casts his 2cwt. of solid hero into
it head first, the gallery feels that it hasn’t lived in
vain. The Rignold season ends at the close of next
week, when the management gathers the tank
under its arm and departs for an extended tour in
other parts.
* * * *
The “ Silver King,” concerning which this
paper said some kind things last week out of the
depths of a full heart, is still living up to the
character then bestowed upon it, and Her
Majesty’s is prospering greatly. Judging by
present prospects, Scot Inglis will return loaded
with wealth from far Nevada and Roberts will
struggle to wrest a shilling from Bodger every
night for a long time to come. The present
writer’s private opinion is that the average audi-
ence doesn’t sufficiently appreciate the importance
of Bodger, and that that invisible and grumpy
baker-man has never received his full meed of
praise. The Denver family is at the very lowest
ebb of its fortunes on the day when the lost
father returns, and but for the shilling contributed
by Bodger to tide over the last few hours death
might overtake them before the Silver King turns
up, and then the story would be wrecked utterly.
The horny and probably unclean hand of Bodger,
coming with a shilling in it out of the infinite, as
it were, stays the calamity. If the hand of Bodger
didn’t drop that solitary bob into the story there
might, to all intents and purposes, be no story at
all. Therefore The Bulletin gives three cheers,
in the spirit, for Bodger, whose importance has
just occurred to it, and trusts that he will be better
appreciated in the future.
* * * *
The twenty-third instalment of Harry
Rickards' new imported artists is now due. Part
of the instalment, in the shape of Alice Conway
and Edward Clarke, is to open at Sydney Tivoii
on Saturday, and some more instalment, whose
name is Miss Jessie de Gray, appears on Monday
at the Palace. Some remarks about both these
instalments will be made in next issue. Mean-
while both the Tivoli and the Palace are prosper-
ing exceedingly, though there is such a hurried
shifting of characters from one house to the other
that it is difficult to remember where anybody
are, so to speak. To-day (Thursday) Henry Lee
has a matinee of his own at the Palace, when a
large number of artists from all the Sydney
theatres conspire to give him a benefit.
* * * *
The usual large quantity of Cinemato-
graphe was still filling a great public want when
this paper went to press, and new pictures are
being constantly added. Jimmy MacMahon’s
latest triumph is a picture of the lakelet at Wind-
sor Castle, with the swans swimming on the
placid surface and an interfering person chasing a
crowd of long-legged birds on the shore. There is
no collusion about this picture ; the birds aren’t
stiffened in any way, and it is a very pretty piece
of nature. The Lumiere “ Cin.” has a new
speciality in the shape of a high-diving exhibition.
And neither of the two has a picture of the arrest
of Butler, which is a lamentable omission and one
to be deeply regretted.
* * * *
“ A Woman of No Importance ” got a pro-
mising start at Melb. Bijou, insofar that a mixed
audience listening with close attention to Oscar
Wilde’s dialogue forgot to notice that it had very
little bearing on the plot. But that matters little.
Wilde’s play reminds one of a “ Roman candle ”
firework, which discharges a steady shower of
sparks and an occasional colored ball, the ascent
of each ball being heralded by a gentle pop. There
are three mild explosions in this story. A wicked
lord discovers himself to be the illegitimate father
of his private secretary ; the secretary’s mother
informs her son of his relationship to the wicked
lord ; thirdly, that unhappy lady flicks a glove in
her son’s father’s face, as a hint for him to quit
the premises. The rest of the entertainment is
decorative talk—bright, ingenious, obviously
forced, and quite impossible in any sassiety circle,
but fascinating, nevertheless. Also, it is spoken
under real human conditions.
» • * *
The gilt-edged people who give vent to
O. Wilde’s false philosophies, contorted truths, or
sincere epigrams are required to be reposeful the
while, after the manner of their kind. In the
most natural, so called, of comedy dialogue scenes
the characters interchange places every tvvo or
three minutes, like a fielding cricket-team. It is
an absurd rule, yet it is indispensable to the suc-
cess of ordinary dramatic work. Here the talkers
stick to their seats; therefore, considering that
the conversation is extraneous to the plot, its
literary smartness is emphasised by the author s
audacity in putting it to the severest test. A
Woman of No Importance ” contains many sav-
ings that sound very clever (although they don t
bear examination when printed), and a few bril-
liantly unpopular truths. ‘'Children begin by
loving their parents ; then they judge them ; they
seldom or never forgive them,” is a case in point.
Surely, if the virtue of a new moral reflection is
visited upon the father, O. Wilde may hope to be
forgiven a good many sins when settling day
arrives.
* * * *
The three right-thinking characters in
this bizarre play—the wronged mother, her duti-
ful son, and his American sweetheart—are the
least satisfactory as works of art. The mother
expresses foolish melodramatic sentiments concern-
ing her “ shame,” the son has false notions about
bonds of matrimony, and the girl is a pious libel
on American heiresses in general. They combine
with the wicked lord in running the thin plot of
Oscar’s play, and the noble reprobate is the only
convincing item in the trouble. Titheradge, ele-
gantly made up for the occasion, gives a fine per-
formance of his lordship, whilst Mrs. Brough, as
the mother in black, appeals for more sympathy
than she can get out of her exaggerated suffer-
ings. Miss Emma Temple is not at home in the
unthankful position of a priggish ingenue,
and first Melb. impressions of Mr. Edward Ferris,
who has a very difficult part to fill, suggest that
he is a rather heavy young imitator of Forbes
Robertson. The four ladies who carry on the
bulk of the irrelevant conversation are a well-
distributed success. Miss Florence Seymour (the
ope who likens her husband to a promissory note,
because she finds it an awful effort to meet him)
is a much-improved actress since the days of
“ Jim the Penman ”at the old Bijou. Then she
used to worry the present writer by shaking her
head, but now she could carry a cup of tea upon
her handsome box of intellect without spilling a
drop.
** * *
“ Matsa ” is dying hard at Melbourne
Princess’s. The gaudy run of that expensive
panto-ganza terminates on Friday, and the last
chances to sniff the peculiar odor of Leoni Clarke’s
animal kingdom will be eagerly snapped up. The
Lucifers are the latest additions to “ Matsa.”
■* * * ♦
On Saturday (13th) “ The Prisoner of
Zenda ” will introduce the Firm’s new eo., to-
gether with a fine collection of new dresses, to
Melbourne notice. The Bulletin has already
mentioned that the “Zenda” production is to be
graced by a choice assortment of local beauty in
the background, and a full report of this unusual
proceeding will appear next week. Meanwhile,
George Gordon is putting the final touches to some
massive scenery. Also, everybody concerned is
in active rehearsal.
** * *
Bland Holt intends to get even with the
opposition by bringing forth “ The Derby
Winner ”on Saturday. Bland’s current show
has been an enormous success, mainly on account
of its horse spectacle, consequently he reckons
that a second edition of horse, issued under an
attractive turfy title, will suit Melbourne better
than any other drama on his list. And, judging
from “ The Derby Winner’s ” triumphant career
in Sydney last winter, the Royal management
will bring off another coup with that sensational
quadruped.
** * *
Harry Rickards was to make his farewell
appearance at Melb. Opera House last night
(Wednesday) prior to sailing for Europe and
America in quest of Unique Novelties. The only
Henry leaves a strong programme behind him, and
the business promises to sustain its usual flourish
during its absence. All the same, The Bulletin
hopes he will keep his absence within reasonable
limits and return like a giant refreshed.
At Poverty Point.
Southern sassiety has been suddenly seized with an un-
controllable desire to display its legs in tights—probably
to prove to incredulous mankind that it possesses as
tangible an article as the coryphee who makes the game a
profession. Anyhow, the most photographed of all'Melb.’s
alleged beauties, who cannot be as thin as she looks, pur-
poses airing her lower limbs in the chorus of a certain
opera co., when it re-opens.
Journalist: “What a miserable phase of our business
is sitrnalised by this concurrent rush of the telegram-
mongers into the assumption that Mrs. Charnngton must
be Miss Achurch ! A little knowledge is a fiangerous
thing, etc. Here are the promoted reporters making a
dig at their old line of ‘Another Actress in Trouble
when the woman who sweeps the stage comes before the
police court for boozing. What would be the value of a
journalist if he didn’t know that Mrs. Charnngton must
be Miss Achurch ?”
A man in London, who called at the bloated Hotel Cecil
to see Jimmy MacMahon (the Mighty Atom), had much
trouble in finding him—there were so many colonels, am-
bassadors, and such staying there, named MacMahon. At
last, the caller was leaving in despair when a small page-
boy nudged a be-buttoned comrade—“ You know him,
’Arry ;Vs the gent as Maggie says is a puffeck gentleman
—a little cove, clean-shaved, with a high-glass 1m that s
always in a ’ell of a ’urry and never gets nothin done.
Ernest Fitts, the Melbourne baritone-dentist, who
lately accepted an engagement with the Firm, isa solemn
warning to stage-struck amateurs. In " Matsa he was
required to shave off his moustache and warble drearily
from amidst an awfully unbecoming robe, and his former
lady patients have ceased to talk about him. I itts mo.
was all-in-all to his appearance—as necessary as a black
bell-topper to a city-man’s respectability. And now, to
complete his discomfiture, the unlucky aspirant is down
with typhoid fever.
Young Terrv protests against a Bulletin assertion that
he plays the ‘German music-master, in “ Adventures of
Miss Brown,” with a variable accent, and, by way of
proving that his accent must be consistent, he mentions
that he speaks German fluently. All the same, Terr} s
accent under notice is Franco-German-Savoyardian. At
times he talks exactly like our late friend, Gecko.
Ethel liaydon gets mild reproof from some of the
London theatrical napers for not playing her part in
“ The Circus Girl ” with more dash and abandon. But
Ethel’s ingenue style doesn’t run to abandon.
Minnie Palmer persists in being as young as ever, if not
younger. At present she is playing name-part in “ The
School Girl” with her flossy hair a-hanging down her
back. Among other giddy-girlish extravagances she sings
a “ bubble ” song, whilst the other alleged school girls sit
around and blow a soap-bubbles chorus.
It is time that somebody warned the ladies of Bland
Holt’s company against adopting a certain clever actor’s
chronic laugh. The habit has been growing upon him
these last three years. He breaks into smiles, and
chuckles at the most inappropriate moments, and the
ladies seem to find it infectious. Miss Hilda Spongcaught
the irritating mannerism (for a love-making scene in
which both parties laugh irreverently is rather irritating),
and now Miss Elizabeth Watson threatens to fall a victim.
Among the many musical virtues of George Howard*
the Melb. ’cellist, was an elegant style of handling his
bow, which he impressed upon his pupils. The lady
members of the Melb. Amateur Orchestral Society 7 and
Howard Conservatorium were dainty fiddlers without ex-
ception. People who didn’t know Howard’s real character
used sometimes to sneer at him for putting on jam, but
the “jam” of that amiable, painstaking artist was de-
cidedly' preferable to the affected soulfulness of the long-
haired musicians who didn’t wash themselves.
When Knowles was added to the Firm’s Melbourne
panto, a local newspaper observed : “If there was one
thing needed to improve ‘ Matsa,’ it was the introduction
of a thoroughly good comedian with an undoubted sense
of humor.” Rather rough on George Lauri, who plays
the chief funny part.
Wilde’s name, in big red letters, being on the Melb.
Bijou streamers, the clergy are warning their Eves against
the forbidden fruit.
A Melb. Herald correspondent protests against the
Brough co. playing a comedy by that “ very monster in
human form,” whose “ filthy, slimy' name stinks in the
nostrils of all decent men and women ” —Oscar Wilde, to
wit. Unthinking rant. On this basis, the solitary ex-
ample of Turner’s painting that hangs in Melbourne
National Gallery ought to be barred, because Turner was-
notoriously', an evil-living man.
Porson : “ If Wilde’s plays are to be burnt, so must the
works of half the classic authors taught to schoolboys.”
Miss Ada Ferrar, the Firm’s leading lady for “ Prisoner
of Zenda,” was released from her contract with Otho
Stuart in order that she might accept George Musgrove’s
engagement. Stuart and Co. presented the fair
voyageuse with a dressing-bag on her departure for Aus-
tralia.
Music-halls, variety-entertainments and concerts were
lately' prohibited in Sweden, by' Act of Parliament, at the
instigation of the public-school teachers, who are seem-
ingly controlled by Ibsen.
Wilson Barrett’s eldest son is gathering “colonial ex-

And this because Irving had sprained his foot when play’ing

Brisbane (Q.) Musical Union is in Queer-street. It has
been under a “ new constitution ” for two years. It paid
its conductor his first quarter's salary, and hasn’t paid
him a cent since ; now, he asks for a guarantee of at least
£5O a year ! The Union has got a bank overdraft of £2OO,
for which guarantors are responsible ; it is now scratching
its head whether it will let the guarantors sell off its
library (worth £500), or whether it is game to float a co.
to take up the running, in £1 shares.

the conservatoire will flourish under her direction.

The Wild-Cat Column .
N.S.W. Govt., m the first seven months of the
present financial year, frittered away £832,700 of loan
moneys, so that the debt keeps increasing at the rate of
£1,400.000 a year. There are no big reproductive works
in progress, but statues and repairs and small patches
and alterations and sand-shifting and scrub-cutting and
similar odd jobs account for the drift. Almost every one
of these is a current expenditure item, but then the
acknowledged current expenditure for the year is already
£318,000 ahead of revenue, and the accounts must he
faked somehow. And even now, despite the wiping off
of all arrears by charging them to the public debt, and
the setting up of the cash basis, Treasurer Reid is still
charging items of expenditure back to previous years,
and thus making things look better than they are. Appa-
rently that cash basis is never going to get quite started.
* * * *
The Victorian Government has to raise a little
over £2,000,000 this year to redeem 4 percent, debentures
falling due, and the question at issue is whether this
amount can be borrowed locally at 3 per cent. If it can’t
the Vic. Government should certainly try a local loan at
3| ; considering the saving in stamp-duty, management
expenses, cost of remitting interest, &c., 3J4 percent,
locally would be cheaper in the long run than 3 per cent,
in London, though it might not look quite so big an advt.
to whoop about in the meantime. And even at 3i the
interest saving would be £15,000 a year.
* * * •
The Vic. State Land Bank, in January alone,
received applications for loans amounting to over £61,000.
And to meet this demand it is raising cash at the rate of
£25,000 in three months. If the Vic. Land Bank means
business, and doesn’t intend to kill a great idea by
excessive slowness, it needs to dump about £150,000
worth of bonds on the market and go to work in real
earnest.
* * * *
N.S.W. Government now demands income-tax
upon “ interest on mortgages due but not received.” One
man that The Bulletin' knows of has an £BOOO mortgage
of long standing on a property which might now possibly
sell for £6OOO. His interest is sometimes received and as
often not received, and he wants to know where the joy
comes in of .paying tax on £4BO that he often hasn’t got
by way of satisfaction for the ±2OOO which he never
expects to see again. Many others are in very much the
same situation, and if the “due but not received ” clause
is worked for all it is worth, still another streak of blas-
phemy will fill the cornstalk atmosphere.
,*« * *
Melbourne Board of Works is floating in Melb.
at 3£ per cent, a £250,000 loan for more water and sewer-
age works, and a £500,000 loan to redeem an old 5 per
cent, liability for the same amount now falling due. The
Board is off the London market since that dreadful
episode when the wild-eyed poet Fitz Gibbon floated a
£1,000,000 loan in England at 4 per cent., and only got
£9O 17s. for each £lOO debenture, after which the money
was deposited in three banks, one of which burst while the
other two reconstructed.
* * * *
The Ballarat Banking Co. has a half-year’s pro-
fit of £2937, which, compared with the £3250, £3463,
£3449, and £3710 of the previous four half - years,
shows a pretty steady and continuous decline. The
usual 5£ per cent, dividend accounts for c'2337 ; £5OO is
carried to reserve, making it £52,500, and the remaining
£lOO, added to previous balance, makes £2421 carried
forward. Ballarat is none too prosperous just now, which
probably accounts for the fall in profits, and it looks
about time the local bank was knocking the odd, un-
gainly J per cent, off its dividend to meet the times. The
Ballarat Bank has, as usual, a very large reserve in cash
and State securities — £86,225, equal to 41 per cent, of the
total liabilities to the public ; and it looks, as heretofore,
a thoroughly sound and well-managed institution.
*.* • *
With regard to a remark, in the issue of Jan.
30th, that the entire investments of the Trustees, Execu-
tors and Agency Co. (Melb.), “ only return a shade over
2 per cent.,” and were, consequent!}', to be viewed with
some suspicion, it is now explained that the interest
received (£650) only refers to £27,337 of the investments,
the receipts from the other £35,000 being kept in a sepa-
rate account. This makes the interest nearly 5 per cent,
instead of 2, and entirely removes the suspicion which
apparently lay upon the mortgage business. The Bul-
letin regrets that such a misconception should have
arisen ; at the same time, it thinks the accounts might be
a little more lucid. The chief remaining causes of com-
plaint, therefore, are the £lB,OOO of deposits in the Aus-
tralian Deposit and Mortgage Bank, which are put in at
face value, when their selling price is somewhere between
£12,000 and £13,500 ; and the £14,000 (nominal) of prefer-
ence shares in the same institution, whose present market-
value is much more like £2lOO. These are points which
appear to still require explanation.
• * * *
Under the provisions of the new Vic. Companies
Act the Royal Bank (Melbourne) proposes to write off
£lso,ooo—half its paid-up capital ; the Commercial of
Australia is writing off £1,200,000; and the Colonial,
which has also some serious writing-off to do, is yet to be
heard from. In the case of the Royal the writing-off
means that one-third of the advances have been lost, and
even when the capital is written down to one-half, the
profits, at recent rates, won’t quite run to a 3 per cent,
dividend. The Royal has a large capital in proportion to
the size of its operations and can stand the shock ; if the
Colonial had to write off one-third of its advances it would
wipe out all its capital in a single act and practically
write off the whole bank.
* * * *
North Queensland Insurance Co. (fire and
marine) has a loss of t'776 to show as the result of a year’s
work, and dividend-paying is suspended. In 1895 the
profit was £3113, and in 1894 it was £3399. Last year the
interest on the co.’s own invested funds more than
covered the loss on the insurance business ; this year the
loss on insurance swallows theinterest. Something appears
to be badly wrong with the management of the North
Queensland, as working expenses for three consecutive
years are £11,585, £13,068, and £16,204, while the business
has actually grown smaller. The sinful enlargement of
the expense-account is the sole cause of the loss ; this
year’s increase alone, for which there is no extra business
to show, has more than swallowed up a 5 percent, dividend
—and in the increase as compared with two years ago a
good 8 per cent, dividend has gone bung. Now that the
N.Q. has to put in £5OO aggregate loss as one of its assets,
it is time for shareholders to kick about the growing ex-
penses.
* * * *
N.S.W. Shale and Oil Co. has a half-year’s profit
of £lOB7 compared with £2507, £6075, and £4998 for
thretfprevious half-years. Dividend is cut down to £3
6s. Bd. per cent. (3d. per share), and even that absorbs
£1250, or £163 more than the earnings. This big co. has
a reserve of £271 and its shares are at 50 per cent, dis-
count and its business, apparently, fading very badly.
Yet it was one of the concerns quoted by Premier Reid
as an instance of how industries prospered under Free-
trade—Reid’s other great example being Hudson Bros.,
now burst and in liquidation.
* * * *
The great Westralian Thirst. Swan Brewery
Co., Perth (W. A.), made £7564 last half-year, and after
paying 341 per cent, dividend (£5508) had £2056 left.
Capital £32,134; reserve, £lO,OOO ; balance to credit of
profit and loss, £14,000 odd. And the Swan Co., with a
profound faith in the continuance of the boom and the
thirst, pays dividends like this with £9967 overdraft un-
pairt and a big- mortgage still on its hotel. They have all
got the same boom complaint in the West.

The prospectus of the Rockwell Boulder was
lately issued. The mine, it is said, used to be known
as the Dora ; and the Dora, by the way, is not remem-
bered for either good or ill on the Barrier. “The lode
formation,” says expert Phillips, “ is from 15ft. to 18ft.
kHu ' I, ’ but the outcro P shows in places from 40ft. to
DUtt. wide . . The ironstone taken from the outcrop
resembles that found at the B.H. Proprietary, and the ore
discovered at the surface is far richer than that of the
Proprietary when first discovered.” The writer remem-
bers that precisely the same thing was written of about
300 now-deserted “shows” on the West Coast of Tas-
mania ; and then looks in vain for any estimate of the
proportion of ore to lode formation. The Rockwell
Boulder is a speculation pure and simple. It is a prospect-
ing venture, upon which not £5 worth of real
mining has been done, and in those circumstances, and as
the area is only 40 acres (though millions of acres
are available all round), it is not easj - to see why the pro-
moters and vendors should collar about £2OOO clear,
besides 10,000 fully paid-up and 40,000 partly paid-up
shares. Anyway, though, the Rockwell is a very good
district to let the other fellow prove.

A Broken Hill correspondent:—You Sydney
people seem to know a great deal—or, at any rate, to talk
a great deal—about the B.H. Ore-recovery Co. Tell us,
do, where its claims are. I don’t know. I have just been
on a quest, and have asked at least a score of mining men,
and not one of them knows either. Let us join in the
fun, please.

* * « «
Chairman Clarke, at the B.H. North meeting,
owned up to the co.’s financial difficulties, but was san-
guine that a call could be dodged. In a month, when the
new mill is at work, profits, he predicted, will commence.
But they will be very small, and unless t-he Junction and
Junction North lode develops well in the western ground
of the North, divs. are still in the far distance. Still, the
prospects of the North getting the Junction body are
decidedly improving—in other words, The Bulletin’s
theory that the Junction development is an elbow of the
main lode (a theory admittedly shaken severely once or
twice) promises well to come out right, after all. But it
is not to be forgotten (the Junction North people, who
are lending the North folks their workings, haven’t for-
gotten it, anyway) that the underlie out of the North and
into the Junction North is rapid. Obviously, too, Junc-
tion North shares ought to fall as Norths advance-
assuming the North’s advance to be justified ; because if
the North has the ore then it is only at the expense of the
Junction North.
* * * *
R. E. Kemp, ex-chairman of the Great Cobar
Copper Co., has issued and advertised a circular in which
he gives long and numerous reasons why he was opposed
to the advice given by the board to shareholders, not to
part with their shares. He remarks that since the tribute
was let considerably over 40,000 shares have changed
hands, and it is well-known that these shares, or more
than half the eo., are now held by the trihutors. If Mr.
Kemp would discover and publish “ the reasons” why the
mine was dunderheadedly worked for so many years
without the shareholders being aware of the presence of
gold in the ore or getting any return for it, he would be
telling something that people really want to know. He
says the interests of the trihutors are directly opposed to
those of the other shareholders, therefore he cannot say
that “the shares are now a good permanent investment.”
Be that as it may, the trihutors went into this mine when
the shares (now £2) were down to 3s. 6d. They have paid
the co. many thousands in royalty, and give employment
to upwards of 1000 hands ; they have resurrected Cobar
and proved that every ton of copper averages 4oz. in gold ;
also, they have done many other unobjectionable things
while keeping their weather eye open to their own in-
terests.

The powerful United Exploration Co., of
London, has taken over the Baker's Creek Extended and

in trying to starve the Extended out, and come to terms,
to enable these mines to be operated on through its
worked-out levels.
* * * *
Adclong, N.S.W. “Mr. Eissler has declared
the output from the Gibraltar mine for Jan. as l.iOOoz.
from 500 tons. This amount does not include the gold
from tailings, which are rich and will be cyanided.” The
latter is probably intended by the newspaper man as a
compliment. This yield is quite good enough, and the
stone at grass is still very considerable. The point is—
can the 30-head battery he kept going on this grade of
rock? If so, the mine is a much bigger one than was
generally thought, and Eissler is a very able and very
lucky mining man to foresee it. The country rock con-
sists of the hardest granite, the vein is small and frosted
on to the country. Everything that money can provide
to ease things has been done—rock-drills, battery, and
other plant, driven by water power, at a cost of some
£50,000. It only remains for the ore chutes to have much
longer life than others worked to a standstill in the same
camp. The writer sincerely hopes so, but has his “ doots.”

The Vic. Government wants to establish an
office in London where information about Vic. mines may
be supplied, and to fool away the public money cabling
mining statistics to England, printing boom pamphlets,
and sending round a lurid person to promote the invest-
ment of British capital in the industry. Melbourne Aob
advises also that special encouragement should be given
to the Bull person who conies to take away Victorian gold
by “ granting large areas ” and making the labor condi-
tions easy. This yearning to smooth the path of the
stranger who comes to annex the country’s best asset is
very touching.
* * * *
The story of the Queen’s Birthday United Gold
Mines (Dunolly, Vic.) is a solemn warning to the people
who yearn to develop Victorian mines by the aid of British
capital. Somebody agreed to buy the property for £12,800
oash and £55,000 in paid up shares. Then a British co.
was to be formed to buy it off him for £40,000 cash and
£65,000 in shares. The co. proceeded to issue shares in
order to raise the purchase-money, and the expenses, and
the cash for the first robbers, and so on, but the public
only applied for about 9500 altogether. Then cer-
tain people interested in the promotion of the co.
applied for 40,000 shares, some of them in the name of
“ Thomas Brown ” and other imaginary people, apparently
to make a boom and create a demand. In most cases the
allotment money and the calls remained owing. Then
the 00., on the strength, presumably, of the alleged rush
for shares, started issuing debentures with such bad
success that it owes £26,000 to the debenture-holders,
yet apparently only received £ll,OOO. Finally the co.
burst up. There was no real mining done; its whole
existence was a struggle in a London office to sell shares
and debentures. No capital, apparently, ever came to
Victoria ; no labor worth speaking of was employed, and
the “resources of the province” weren’t “developed”
worth twopence. And of such is the encouragement of
the Australian mining industry by British capital.

At last Rutherglen’s boom is within seeing
distance. Three months hence the Great Southern No. 1
and North Prentice should be in big-yield form, and the
Great Southern well on with the cutting of its wash. All
the smaller shows are hustling into line as quick as
crippled finances will let them.
* * » _ *
Rokewood is the failure of Vic.’s alluvial fields.
Year ago it was in the thick of a boom, and to-day the
blind-headed public has a hardly-breathing infant to coo
over. Carr’s Freehold knocks out small divs. for its
working owners, but none of the other shows are feeling
pert. Hanlon’s, going to ladle divs. six months ago,
dangles between 40 and 56 oz. yields, just sufficient to
scrape along without calls, and the prospects no better.
Months back the Rokewood Consols promised fair yrields,
and it is still promising and likely to. Pending a hope-
less wait for calls to come in to put down another bore,
Stanley Proprietary will wind up, and the salted Lord
Salisbury is a buried deed. The black shadow gradually
closing over Rokewood.

Coolgardie, in 1895, turned out 125,0000 z. gold.
In 1896 the return was down to 69,1350 z., or not much
over one-half. The boom has shifted along, and with
another year of such luck it will be time for Coolgardie
to hump its swag into the wilderness in search of a new
location.

Isn’t this gorgeous ? An English paper:—
“Mr. W. G. Brookman leaves by the Friedrich der
Grosse next week for W. Australia. He is entrusted by
about 20 mining co.’s with the task of organising and
putting their properties in working order. Sir Somers
Vine has expressed a desire to accompany him. Mr.
Brookman will receive an honorarium of 10,000 guineas
besides expenses.”
* * * *
The Black Flag Proprietary has reconstructed.
When first unfurled it had 600,000 shares, of which
vendors got £525,000. The pound shares once touched
425. 6d., say, £1,200,000 for a show with £75,000 working
capital. Mr. Samuel Gifford, an expert who visited the
property, said the lode was 30ft. wide for 3000 ft. —but at
the 70ft. level it had skedaddled. What a rude thing for
it to do ! The meeting put up another £25,000 and went
home.

The Golden Horseshoe, W.A. (next Ivanhoe),
has just issued 20,000 new £1 shares at 30s. each. £11,225
has been expended on the mine, including £2600 thusly :
directors’ fees, £1349 ; law costs, £lO2 ; secretary, £278 ;

How heavily the London director does sit on Westralian
mining! Just imagine the South German paying £1349
in directors’ fees as compared with £5626 wages as paid
by the Horseshoe ! The co. should change its name to the
Golden Slipper.
* * * *
“ Bcgelholo Westralia Prospecting (final div.),
£2 2s. 6d.” Not out of profits, though. This buries the
son s, not the captain's, enterprise. Said that the
.‘ famous” mining expert has lost a bit of his W.A. scoop
in backing new Vic. ventures.
* * * *
Rumored that the Trenton (W.A.) is about to
close down, and that the battery will be sold for what it
will fetch. One of the many W.A. have-beens, of which
the public hear nothing. What are the great Day Dawn
mines in the same vicinity doing? They much belied
their name.
* * * *
Lissner, one of the Queensland Menzies direc-
tors, is more than pleased with the mine after inspection.
He thinks it will take the lead as the hoss mine of the
Menzies camp, with which he is also pleased. “So far
there is nothing to prove that the reefs will not carry
their gold down to great depths.” He says the field
suffers for want of a big central crushing plant. Does it
not suffer for want of timber?
* * * *
Westralian gold. Great Boulder, 720 tons,
21530 z.; Boulder Perseverance (month), 765 tons, 24750 z.;
Lady Shenton, 342 tons, 14070 z. ; Fraser’s, 1200 tons,
608 oz. ; North Boulder, 160 tons, 3260 z.
* * * *
The coming big producers from Hannan’s should
be the Associated Mines, of which W. A. Irwin, formerly
of the Wentworth, N.S.W., is now'superintending manager.
The Australia is the boss lease, and high-grade ore has
been found in several other of the co’s leases. It has sunk
a heap of money in plant and development, but is said to
be on the eve of a big regular output—foreshadowed by
the rising tendency of the shares in a dull, almost dead,
London market.
* * * *
Stated that Brown Hill mine, W.A., has con-
tracted to ship 2000 tons ore to Dry Creek smelting works,
S.A. The W.A. Govt, has reduced the railway freight, on
ore to jd. per ton per mile, providing the trucks are back-
loaded. Seeing that nine-tenths of the freight is going to
the fields, this should be an easy matter. The concession
means the landing of ore at Dry Creek for less than 30s. a
ton, and will make high-grade mines more independent
of water.
* * * *
Norseman (W.A.)—“ The manager of Mawson’s
Reward to-day brought in two pieces of very rich quartz
broken out of the drive at the 45ft. level.” It Vas floated
nearly three years ago, has been several times in a money
mud-hole, and was helped out by the promoters—the
W.A. Goldfields Co. Also, it has long bad a battery, but
never crushes. Now it has two lumps of rich rock. What
sort of a boom will it have if the boss finds tw'o more ?
• * * •
W.A. wire—“ The Great Scotch Gold Hole is
driving on 2oz. stone, about 80 tons being at grass ; now
awaiting the erection of machinery.” Alas ! how much
machinery is out there awaiting stone ?
* * * «
Reported from Adelaide that a man has turned
up with some telluride ore assaying up to 98oz. to the ton
from the Norseman. Last month the Dundas gold-output,
which includes the Norseman, was 470 z., hence it is time
something was struck.
* * » *
Westralian gold-output for Jan. : 40,3860 z., a
great improvement, palpably due to the big mines of
Kalgoorlie, which camp now turns out as much gold as
the balance of the province.
* * * #
“Five tons of blanketings from the last crush-
ing of the Boulder Main Reef averaged 230 z. to the ton.”
And yet it was wired that the tellurides were lost. Is it
not about time W.A. wires contained some sense, and
that they were better sub-edited at the eastern end?
Five times in six, anything from this mine is put down,
“ Boulder main reef,” whereby the public run away with
the idea that it alludes to the principal reef of the Great
Boulder mine.
* * * •
The Wilmot (Gympie, Q.), after a few years of
suspended animation, has again struck the Russell reef
that was struck in ’94, and petered out in three months.
Fenwick and Co. hold a heap of Wilmot shares, having
stuck pluckily to them when they were considered waste
paper.
* » * *
Croydon has won the third position among Q.
gold-fields from Gympie, although drought seriously
affects the output. Total for ’96, 85,9610 z., of which
19,5530 z. were got per cyanide. Total output in ’95,
69,7420 z, the year ’96 showing a substantial increase and
also the largest yield ever made by Croydon. Divs. for the
year exceeded £58,000. Prospects for the ensuing year
are good.
Gympie now takes fourth place with 73,1880 z.
from 63,000 tons of quartz. Yield for ’95, 78,0260 z. Divs.
for last year, £118,832—a decrease on ’95.
Then comes the Etheridge with 26,4330 z
slight increase on ’95. This includes 8000 oz. alluvial and
3000 oz. got by cyanidation of tailings. Next come
Ravenswood with 14,6480 z. ; Clermont with 12,2670 z.;
Gladstone with 58570 z. By the way, what has happened
the gorgeous Mt. Caminadah, that was half gold ; the
magnificent Burrum beaches that were all gold, and half-
a-dozen other El Dorados of the last two years ?
* * * *
Tasmania, Beaoonsfield, has a .£60,000 share
issue on to cover the new shaft sinking and necessary
machinery equipment. It creates 12,500 £5 shares, paid
up to £2 125., scrippers’ purchase-payments to spread
over tw'o and a-half years. A proposal that will obviate
div.-suspension, but doesn’t it expose the short-sighted
management of this big concern when, after years of div.-
paying, it has to appeal for fresh capital to negotiate un-
avoidable dead work ? The necessity of a new shaft has
stared directors in the face for many a day.
♦ * * *
Melb. Queen-street backers of Curtin-Davia
Prop. (Tas.) fairly on the fizz over the recent turn up of a
2ft. to Sift, ore-shute in the upper levels. The Bin. to
12in. ore-streak that once in a blue moon sprung a 2000 oz.
assay became so monotonous that Southerners had
damned the show’ as a booked funeral, and the aforesaid
strike happened nicely at a time w’hen the scrip was
quivering for a nasty tumble. Metallurgist Schlapp
swears by the C.D.P., but Tas. silver-grubbers wink their
other eye at the show, hence the “ Island ” shareholdings
are very trifling. To test the smelting characteristics ol
the ore 500 cons are breaking.
* * * *
Rumored again that the Oonah, Zeehan (Tas.)
is “ likely to pay a small dividend within the next month
or two.” Always plenty of rumors, but devil a do. Six
months back, the Oonah was stringing stockers with the
div. bait.
* * * *
Despite the warning of returning prospectors,
to which that of Gov. M‘Gregor has been added, men are
flocking to New Guinea at this the worst season of the
year. So far nothing but alluvial has been found, and
not much of that. The prospect is only good enough for
well-equipped parties with money to lose, and at the
right time of the year—commencing about three months
hence. What will happen when some Westralian
boomsters and a newspaper get out there can be easily
imagined, and this will happen very soon after the finding
of gold in reefs is announced.

S.A. State Children’s Department has
been prosecuting a Chinaman for neglecting to
support some wonderful grandchildren. John
Ahang, Chinee, married Topsy, a black lubra.
Harry Ahang was the resulting skewbald. Harry
Ahang, in his turn, married the daughter of a
Malay husband and Irish wife. The grand-
children, therefore, were quadroons, of the Irish-
Malay-China-Australian blend of spotted black-
fellow.

N. : “ Apropos the hugger-mugger teach-
ing of Chinese by young ladies in Australian
Mission Churches. Suppose the men white, how
indelicate it would be ! The Chinese seem to be
regarded as a fourth sex. I remember how the
Georgia Minstrels, with their pallid, moist, kid-
glovy faces, furored the Slowbart women.”
“WHERE LOUD BOREAS TELLS HIS TALE.”
S HE: “I think I’ll have my bloomers made pretty full.”
HE: “I think you ought to pretty well fill ’em yourself!”
The Japs are gradually accumulating in
Townsville, Q., under the patronage of the
nobility and gentry. Many are employed as cooks,
housemaids, &c. Judge Chubb (salary, £2OOO a year
from the Q. taxpayer) has two Jap servants. A
local butchering firm employs three, and there
are 18 Japs doing three months in Stewart’s
Creek gaol. Nice for the aforesaid taxpayer.
* * * *
At an inquest on the body of a Smell-
bourne Chow consort, the other day, the bereaved
heathen stated that his “missee ” had been in the
habit of smoking from 10 to 15 shillingsworth of
opium per week. Coroner Youl, remarking that
the lady was a heavy smoker, was corrected by
the interpreter, who said that many Chinese get
through 40 shillingsworth of the drug every week.
As an antidote to Celestial thrift opium is clearly
an excellent thing.
* * * *
The Chinaman has a strange, uncanny
taste in funerals. A Chinese burial took place re-
cently at Waikomiti (M.L.). Twenty-five cab-
loads of tearful Chow followed the hearse ; pieces
of tissue-paper, each pierced with six holes pre-
sumably so that the air might get through—were
showered from the cab-windows all the way ;
cakes and confectionery were dropped into the
grave, and fusees and crackers were burned around
it. Then the late lamented Mongol was buried,
and gin poured over his resting-place from a
watering-can. Truly this is an upside-down world
where the Aryan goes about with his tongue hang-
ing out for want of a drink, while good gin is
poured over a dead Chow.
* * * *
Re the forthcoming grand loyal Imperial
shivoo in honor of Her Gracious, hear the Argus :
But the slow-beating and somewhat heavy-footed
British imagination does not lend itself readily to glitter-
ing ceremonial. The average Briton always marches in a
procession with an air of half-ashamed protest, and
arrays himself in a many'-colored official costume with an
inarticulate sense that he is making himself ridiculous.
Garn ! What about the average Britisher’s lodges,
societies, orders, associations, &c., &c., the chief
reason for the existence of which appears to be in
the opportunity they afford the average Briton of
arraying himself in ridiculous clothes, and glaring
sashes, and parading the street, with his glittering
chest thrown out a yard, and his eye in fine frenzy
rolling ?
Melb. Herald : “The object (of the grand
Imperial corrobboree to be held in June next) is to
honor the greatest Sovereign that has ever occupied
the Throne of the Empire to which we are proud
to belong.” This is the kind of dreadful drivel
that will appear in the press for the next four
months. It seems almost incredible that any par-
tially sane or sober journalist should identify the
discoveries and achievements of science and in-
dustry, and the progress in art and literature, that
have taken place during the reign of Queen Vic-
toria with that very ordinary little person herself.
Yet readers of Roman history still profess to think
that the world must have been mad when some of
the Caesars were worshipped as gods ! One step
further and the British loyalists of to-day will be
as mad as the.old Romans were. That step will
probably be taken in June, and we shall become —
for a time, until the reaction arrives—a nation of
besotted isolators.

At a Melb. police-court, the other
day, two men, charged with trespassing
upon a widow’s back verandah at 3
a.m., explained that they had strolled
into the garden to get a few apples.
One of the intruders had five convic-
tions to his name, and the widow, it
transpired, didn’t possess an apple-tree,
but the Bench let the apple-seekers off
with a £r> fine. The Bulletin men-
tions this case merely as a hint for Bui
Sikes. Next time William gets caught
in the act of opening somebody’s safe
at midnight he may as well point out
that he was looking for apricots to take
to a sick cousin.
* » * *
On one of the W.A. fields lives
a doctor who is a perfect misogynist.
His brow becomes corrugated, and his
eye stormy, when an obstetrical case
looms ahead. On no account will he
go into a female’s apartment unless
there is a third person present, and
barmaids are his especial aversion. Once
he was called upon to attend one of
these vestals, a regular “spanker.”
The landlord, a bachelor, in obedience
to his mandate, shuffled in diffidently
after him, but the moment the lady
proceeded to unbosom herself for the
stethoscope he rose and muttered that
he had better withdraw. “ Stay where
you are,” said the local Abernethy, “ or
I will go too ! And how much spirits
have you been in the habit of drinking
each day ? ” he pursued, casting a pecu-
liar side-long glance of dislike ac his
pearl-powdered patient. “Oh, doctor!
it’s a thing I never touch,” she modestly
ejaculated; “ whatever are you treat-
ing me for ? What are you going to
do?” “I have not yet made up my
mind,” replied Medicus, “ but for the
present we will call it congestion of
the liver. And as to what lam going
to do, I intend to do what I think right
and proper.” The exhibition largely
comprised bromide.

its accountant =£loo a year.
Sample from exhortation by a certain
Hobart parson who prides himself on his oratory,
and its beneficent effect on his flock :
“ I am sorry to say, my dear friends (pause) that I have
to inform you that the morals of my flock are not in the
condition 1 hoped them to be. I was coming home a few
evenings ago and, friends, I will tell you what I saw. I
saw a man and a woman. (Impressive pause.) They
were on a seat, friends. (Another pause.) And I will
tell you what they were doing. (Awful pause.) They—-
they—were, my friends —they were —no ! my friends, I
cannot—l cannot tell you what those unfortunate (?)
people were doing.”

The inane exaggeration by a Sydney even-
ing paper of the four or five-word cable announcing
the capture of Butler may lead to complications.
Under the cable agreement between the big Aus-
tralian dailies, any paper which obtains a message
from an outside source is liable for a heavy sum.
It is scarcely to the interests of the journal in
question, which apparently got its information
from a very outside source indeed, to break up a

Sydney Evening News got home neatly
on its reptile contemporary :
In America, in San Francisco particularly, headlines are
a high branch of art. “ Mutiny on the Swanhilda.”—
“ Fights on the Deck.” —“ Officers and Crew in Conflict.”
—“A Floating Hell,” &c., would, for instance, convey to
the reader unused to the guileful ways of San Francisco
that definite news had been received of some fearful
trairedy.
In San Francisco—ahem ! This is much prettier
and more biting than the memorable “Ananias”
squabble.

Crowned heads of Europe have always
given their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
Paris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been “Violette Reine” (quite different from
“ Rhine Violet ”), and “Preciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made ; in fact,
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post free for 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

* * * *
The stringent conditions laid down in
international treaties that a man on extradition
can only be tried for the offence for which he is
extradited are very necessary. Apropos, a present
well-known Sydney resident once living in Maori-
land was “wanted” in N.S. Wales. There was
no way of getting at him unless by warrant, so a
bogus charge of indecent assault upon a little girl
was formulated, and upon this the Sydney S.M.
granted a warrant. The accused was arrested in
M.L. and brought to Sydney, whereupon the
charge was withdrawn and another substituted
upon which defendant was convicted. The con-
viction was upheld on appeal to the Full Court,
but on further appeal the Privy Council derided
the whole proceedings and ordered accused’s dis-
charge—after 18 months’ imprisonment.
Fielder: “Four of these 5000 sov. gold
steamboat robberies have occurred in Austral and
Maoriland waters. Thief detected in only one
case. Odds, 3to 1 on. Whole money recovered
in no case. Odds, 4 to 1 on getting the lot, anyhow.
Thief got all the money in two cases. Even about
that. No alarm roused in any case. Why do
5000-sov. boxes of gold run about like pigs with
forks stuck in their backs ? ”

In N. Melb. Court the other day. Wife
sues her husband for maintenance of herself and
family:
Dr. Lloyd, J.P. : “ Do you admit that Daphne, Myrtle,
Olive, Rose and Joseph are your children ! ”
Defendant (scratching his head): “I admit the first
four, but I am not sure about Joseph.”
Case adjourned for 28 days to allow defendant
time to make sure about Joseph. Joseph was
always an unlucky name.
Mrs. Carew, it was cabled the other day,
was after all convicted at Yokohama of poisoning
her husband ; the alleged confession by the gover-
ness, therefore, seems to have been a herring
drawn across the trail of justice. Apropos, the
name of Mrs. Maybrick appears in the papers
again, surrounded by much cheap sentiment or
righteous indignation. Most people think she is
innocent, or rather she ought not to have been
convicted. Mrs. Maybrick is (or was when
last seen) a young, handsome, and interesting
womm, and therefore every man under 70, and a
considerable number above that venerable age,
swear she is innocent. To immure a beautiful
woman or a clever man is simply an outrageous
waste of rare and p recious material,

A N.S.W. solicitor and commissioner for
affidavits, who was disrolled some time ago, but,
having purged himself of his iniquity, was re-
admitted, was discovered the other day adminis-
tering an affidavit. Question—to be tested—did
the re-admission place him where he was at date
of disrollment, or only where his first admission
placed him ?
* * * *
The Rosebrook (Vic.) horror throws an-
other side-light on Church religion. Although
Mrs. Lyfield knew that her husband was guilty
of incest with his middle-aged daughter, she still
lived with him and said her prayers regularly.
The press affects to be astonished as well as
horror-stricken by the story of the Lyfield family
life at Rosebrook. Equally bestial dealings are
reported (and not always in whispers) from every
thinly-populated country district in Australia.
The bush that Australian bards glorify seems to
be the breeding - place of some of the vilest
humanity on God s earth.

N. : “ Melb. War Cry gives
an instalment of what is called an
exposure of the social wound of
prostitution. To this all news-
papers come, one time or another,
like the theatrical manager to Jack
Sheppard. Of course the exposure
is never anything at all but vehe-
ment generalities, for otherwise the
exposer gets Stead-ed. These ex-
posures recall certain trick pamph-
lets vended in the East of London,
and purporting to be extremely
wicked, but which are really quite
innocent. They are sold, with great
mystery, to “elderly gentlemen.”

Sydney girls now ride atop
of ’buses in such numbers that man,
the smoker, will soon have to ride
inside. Until a couple of months
back, they would only climb up
top on particularly festive occa-
sions. When is Sydney to have
’buses, constructed on the sensible
London plan, with stairs, to ob-
viate accidents or immoderate dis-
plays of petticoat ? The- present
vehicles were built to break necks
and give bystanding johnnies pal-
pitation of the heart.
Alongside the two suicided skeletons (male
and female) recently discovered in the bush at
Plimmerton (M.L.) was an unsigned letter in a
bottle. It read as follows : “ Never mind who we
are. We are tired of life. We have left £7 for
burial expenses.” The £7 were also in the bottle,
so a police department with any poetry in its soul
might about as well have respected their incog-
nito, and planted them as “Unknown.” But
instead of doing this, it set to work and labori-
ously dug out the fact that the man was a person
of no importance from Gisborne, and that the
woman was his Second Mrs. Tanqueray, and that
they were both a bad lot from a strictly Social
Gimlet aspect. So having successfully blasted the
moral character of their bones, the law dropped
them into a grave, in a convenient place where
people of uneasy virtue can come rbund on Sun-
day and draw a horrible inference comfortably
after lunch.

A domestic servants’ union has been formed
in Melb., and the great unanimous Bridget rolled
up in a score, with a keen eye to the cash. Ap-
parently Bridget was very willing to be united,
and very unwilling to pay for it. The Argus
report goes:—
Mr. Barker then proposed that the entrance-fee should
be Is., and the subscription 3d. per week.
A Lady : That’s a shilling a month.
Mr. Barker: Yes.
The Lady : And when a member is out of work they
don’t have to pay.
Mr. Barker : Quite so.
The Lady : But there’ll be something to draw.
Mr. Barker : I don’t think any young lady here, or old
lady for the matter of that, wouid bother about a shilling.
Another lady proposed that no entrance fee should be
charged for the first month. The motion was carried
unanimously.
Mr. Barker : Well, no entrance fee for the first month.
What about the 3d. ?
The First Lady : I think it’s too low.
Another lady said that she had consulted with various
other ladies, who thought 3d. per week too high.
The salary of the secretary was fixed at 10s. per quarter,
and of the treasurer at ss. per quarter.
The Bulletin doesn’t exactly envy the male mis-
sionaries who are schooling Bridget to awake and
arise. But probably there will be kitchen pickings
and privileges.

The Domestic Servants’ Union has got a
fine field to work on in Melbourne (writes a Bulle-
tin correspondent). At one public palace this
writer wots of, bossed by an eminently re-
spectable and pre-eminently pious directorate,
the willing girls are worked to death or close up
in a few months; the other sort leave after the
first week, and their wages are confiscated to the
glory of the holy shareholders. The hours, ac-
cording to the girls, are from six o’clock in the
morning “ till we cannot possibly keep awake any
longer.” It is not an unusual thing for the diners
to see one or more of the waitresses plump down
in their midst in a dead faint, the result of long
hours and exhaustion, and the general appearance
of the servants would lead a stranger to imagine
lie had struck a convalescent-home. The male
hands stay a fortnight on an average, and then
generally leave after attempting an aggravated
assault on the manager. The patrons of this es-
tablishment are all of “the better class,”and their
callous indifference under the circumstances is a
sickening thing to see.
Down South a little passing interest has
been aroused by the case of a young lady who
retired to the seclusion of a stable, and there poured
kerosene over herself and set herself on fire. From
a suicidal point of view the scheme was a failure ;
the patient was much blackened, but she emerged
from the stable alive and active, though minus
most of her clothes and hair. Then she told a
thrilling story about how her lover had taken off
her boots and held her feet in the fire and set her
alight in a number of places ; but after a while,
the lover being able to prove an alibi, she with-
drew this narrative and refused to give any further
account of herself. A Victorian young lady,
aged 16, has also created a sensation by causing
the arrest of her brother, aged 18, on a charge of
criminal assault and being the father of her
illegitimate child. When the trial came on, how-
ever, she climbed into the witness-box and said, in
an off-hand way, that it was all a lie, and she
didn’t know why she had said it. Later on she
said she had done it to shield another man whose
name she didn’t know. Further on she said that
she knew the other man’s name but wasn’t going
to tell it. Also, she didn’t know where he lived
anyhow, he had gone away—likewise she declined
to give his address. The usual remarks about God-
less education, and the crying need for more
accurate information about the ancient Jebusites
to keep the rising generation straight, come in
here nicely.

Melbourne people always expect much
wholesome fun at a meeting of Rarson Abbot s
Free Christians. At the recent annual meet
the public was grievously disappointed, and
wanted back the threepenny-bits and buttons ill
had dropped in the plate. Nobody accused any-'

bod y e , £ oll \g to the P IC
brother s wife, and no elder or office-bearer appears
to have been discovered kissing anybody in par-
ticular since, the previous meeting. Only once did
it S eem as if a shocking revelation was about to be
made, and that was when one dear, conscientious
brother arose, and declared that he was not quali-
hed to be a deacon, for the Scnpture saith :
‘ Deacons shall be husbands of one wife ! ’ ‘ And
how many wives have you ? ” gasped the beloved
pastor, and the sisters craned their necks, and the
sinful public sat up. “I have none,” replied the
Free Christian. Then the meeting broke up sud-
denly, and went home disgusted,

Hobart (Tas.) claims to have the best
haunted house in Australia. It has long been
empty, for invisible things whoop and revel and
guffaw and draw corks and slam doors in its dusty
ro oms even during broad daylight. Tasmania
should be a good place for ghosts ; the System
mus t have left enough spectres behind it to haunt
a p Vandemonia. But how a troop of active,
r i o tous, door-slamming ghosts came to settle in
the sleepy city of Slowbart is a mystery.
ORPHANED BY THE SEA
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
BY ETHEL S. TURNER.
“ It seems to me,” she said,
“ It seems to me,
The sea should all be red
As red can be.
“ How can it laugh and play ;
Be blue, with blown sweet spray ;
Sing songs to wake the day,
Lull it to sleep ;
“ While on this wind-swept strand,
With face turned from the land
All red and rainbow-spanned,
I stand and weep ?
“ Somewhere in that wide space
Of blue and filmy lace,
With dead sweet eyes and face,
My mother lies.
“ Somewhere there is a wave
Breaks o’er my father’s grave,
Then comes this beach to lave,
And, laughing, dies.
“ And when I see the blue
Dimpling and leaping, too,
Like baby used to do,
My eyes grow blind.
“ Such little hands and sweet,
Such tiny, rosy feet
For seas to toss and beat
With every wind !
“ That’s why always to me
Blue seems too soft to be
The color for the sea,
The cruel sea.
“ It seems to me,” she said,
“It seems to me,
The sea should all be red
As red can be.”
PIONEERS.
We sail right out in the twilight;
We march right out in the noon ;
Our song is the song of manhood,
Our anthem the fighter’s tune.
You ask the why and the wherefore
We do as we do and can ?
Our march is the March of the Men who Must,
And our song is the Song of Man !
Melb.
