BRISBANE.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Feb. 9, 1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
“ The Strange Adventures of Miss Brown "
opened at the Criterion on Saturday evening with
a rustling of fashionable frills and much society
chatter. The old familiar faces of the first-
nighters adorned the d.c. Even the alcoves were
occupied by dressy patrons of the play, and one
stage-box was principally taken up by a returned
artist with coppery locks, an ample smile, and a
made-in-London frock. Another box was graced
by a party including the society dame who parts
her hair a l’Americaine and is devoted to the
drama. Frank Thornton, disguised as the girl,
gives his sex away by his masculine stride and
also his boots, which, with man-like uppers, end
abruptly at the ankles, whereas girls’ toe-crushers
always gracefully extend some inches up the leg.
Noney Seabrooke, the newcomer, wears pretty,
girly gowns ; Bessie Rignold shows the touch of
the tar-brush in the girl from Demerara by in-
serting herself into gaudy garments and being ready
to love any eligible at a moment’s notice.
The glimpse of a whole boarding-school in night-
apparel brings Miss Brown up to date. No
modern farce is complete without a nightgown.
Even ballets —there was one in the panto—must
now be danced in white longeloth. Nightgown-
humor is adapted from the French farce, which
will be seen here soon under its English name, “A
Night Out ”; the boom began by taking scenes
from it piecemeal, until some clever adaptor dis-
covered a new sort of whitewash warranted not to
crack, and translated the whole play. But if the
English version is one night out, the original is a
whole week in the country with champagne gratis
and all exes. paid.
This month is fashion’s ebb-tide. The woman
who haunts Summer sales and buys weird and
wonderful bargains has but a few weeks in which
to wear them ere Autumn glories shine in the
shop-windows. It is a cheering sight on a muggy
February or March day to see a big drapery show-
window decked out with velvet and chinchilla, re-
produced in the shop next door as velveteen and
dyed rabbit. Velvet is going to be popular ; it is
a becoming fabric, except for a fat woman ; but
fashion ignores the fat woman, or mercilessly com-
pels her to enswathe a hardly-indicated waist with
folded bands of silk, and to accentuate her plump
curves by making them overflow all round a tight
little “ bolero ” jacket. All sorts of spangly, jetty,
beaded trimming is going to successfully augment
the bills for our next season’s gowns. What with
velvet and spangles and beading, a woman dressed
t 6 date will only want a topical song and a trans-
formation-scene to be a whole pantomime.
“Useful blouse lengths at slaughtering prices ”
are a great sale attraction, and just now they
dominate the whole costume of woman. The
blouse-and-skirt has had its death-knell rung many
times, but always resuscitates itself the next
season as cheerfully as ever. For between-tiines
it is the most useful wear. The possessor of a
much-enduring black skirt and some assorted
blouses can ring the changes on her upper gar-
ments, and come up smiling at every festive
gathering with a satisfied expression, implying—
“ I’ve got another blouse.”
The most prominent incidents about the revival
of the “Lights o’ London,” on Saturday at the
Royal, were Mr. Marcus’ song, introduced in the
overture like the prologue to “ Pagliacci,” and the
flaming pink head-dress of a girl in the d.c.
Some audience went out between the acts to
federate at the political meeting over the road
and forgot to come back, but the tearful classic
attracted a fair house. Hetty Preene (Ada Guild-
ford) exhibited gilded infamy in rather a sad-
colored gown, while the woeful Bess Marks was
draped in tea-leaf-hued clothes, and, of course,
wore no hat, but took her misery bareheaded.
Saturday’s tug-of-war and cycle sports on the
Agricultural Ground at Moore Park attracted a
middling-sized crowd. The “gate,” which goes
towards the building up of the impending Aus-
tralian Fair at Easter, was increased by girls with
baskets who made purchasers interpret the sport-
ing idea by handing out large coins for little
flowers and indifferent tobacco. The cycling
child maniac is evidently going to be the very last
development of this tired century. As the racers
hummed by with a crackling rush, some infantile
girls in the front seats shouted enthusiastically as
they barracked for their favorites. One rider,
bound in bright blue satin and gold like a prayer-
book, was much encouraged from the reserve.
Men had the glory of the gowning. The Cardinal
flashed his red robes all over the place like a
danger-signal; and beside the red - and - blue
jacketted tug-of-warsmen the women looked
sartorially faded.
At a singing-pupils’ concert given privately last
week, one of the girls, who comes from Central
Q., warbled in a florid Italian way so remarkably
as to show herself another, and the very nicest,
possessor of the now world-renowned Australian
voice. The young singer is merely a student as
yet. Beginning, like Melba, as a contralto, she is
now capable of bringing out clear trills anywhere
above the stave. Most girls gifted with any tones
deeper than a shrill canary-like warble fall into
the contralto error early in life, and are taught to
growl out deep chest-notes like a hurricane whist-
ling through a keyhole. Melba’s example has
now proved that many of these rich, full-throated
tones belong to the dramatic soprano, a most valu-
able voice commercially.
Victoria Club members turned up at the Mon-
day evening meeting to listen to various musical
performers and to a lecturess with a large collec-
tion of chestnuts culled from the records of Her
Gracious, who was held up to admiration because
she pays her debts—which isn’t hard to do on
£lOOO a day—and has always kept herself
thoroughly respectable, also an easy matter on
the same terms. Her devotion to “ Albert the
Good ” was also descanted upon, the lecturess
pointing out how thankful all H.M’s loving sub-
jects should be to have a monogamous monarch
reigning over them. The little paper was further
remarkable for not once containing the words
“record reign,” and ignoring John Brown and
all his kilts. The club-house has now a cosy
look, and if men-members could be lured in from
the balcony by being allowed to smoke somewhere
indoors, all might yet be well.
A N.S.W. athlete visiting Maoriland writes
enthusiastically to his friends of the fascinations
of the women in the cities of both islands. “Most
of them are pretty, though they crop their hair
short and dress in tweed bags. They don’t expect
you to wait on them, and they take their turn in
shouting their male friends long beers.” This
seems to be the most advanced stage which the
“new” female has yet attained, and among
women so emancipated it is a surprise to find the
petty, old-fashioned female traits still existing.
A school committee in the Hawera region, inves-
tigating charges brought against a head master of
kissing some of the female teachers, found that
in consequence “ considerable friction now exists
among the teachers.” The woman who could
tolerate “the nasty fast cat ” sharing kisses with
her has not yet been discovered even among the
up-to-date M.L. sisterhood.
The wife of a Sydney professional man can take
a pencil in hand and, sitting in a dreamy, trance-
like state, produce strange curly-wurlies and
marvellous faces with fearsome frowns, and
orchidaceous flowers and other marvels. The lady
has never been taught drawing, and these produc-
tions are set down to the “spirits” who vaguely
wander about, and, for purposes best known to
themselves, inculcate art by proxy. Another
dreamy-eyed lady of uncertain age, in a non-com-
mittal bonnet, joins in these inspirations, and,
seizing a note-book, writes therein hurried sen-
tences, explaining the pictures. The remarks,
like the drawings, might mean anything* and
half-an-hour of the seance makes the outsider very
tired. , , „ -
A confirmation service at a suburban church
was glorified by a historical lace veil worn by one
of the girls presented for treatment. The yellow
heirloom was made by a female convict of skill
60 years ago at Parramatta, and has been used in
similar ceremonies many times since. Two
generations of laving-on of sacerdotal hands must
have given the relic the odor of sanctity.
“Ripening Sunbeams” was recently lent to
Hobart Art Gallery ; consequently local sassiety
suddenly took an intense interest in the place and
the Nicest women sat for hours in front of the
picture and talked, while the common herd craned
their necks in the background. A cable was sent
requesting the loan of “ Ripening Sunbeams ” for
another week. The consenting cable came from
Lord B. about an hour after the steamer left with
the picture on board. Then ensued a whole day
of gushful regret.
“The wedding that did not come off,’ is a stale
newspaper sensation, but such a wedding really
didn’t happen some time ago in Prahran (Vic.).
When the bride, cake, guests, &c., were ready and
waiting for the bridegroom, he sent a wire instead
saying he was ill and had gone to the country for
a change. Strict search by an irate mother-in-
law-elect discovered the man so unwilling to face
the music lurking in his office. Result—a breach-
of-promise case, «£75 damages awarded the bride
by Jtidge Hood, and a long lecture for the dis-
sembler on his perfidy. His Honor remarking that
the girl ought to be willing to pay any money to
get rid of such a man. This is one of the very
few actions for breach with sense in them. The
plaintiff had suffered both in business —she is a
dressmaker—and in her neighbors’ esteem by such
an abrupt defection on her young man’s part, and
the law-suit cleared her. And, after all, neigh-
bors’ gossip is a great factor in life.
One of the smartest dog-carts in the city adver-
tises its proprietor—a theatrical manager—as
effectively as a four-foot poster. It is a gorgeously
glitteringiy-harnessed cart, and the icy hauteur
of the menial on the back seat stamps the turn-
out genuine. A private carriage should always
own a coldly-correct flunkey. The hired “ landau”
is generally given away by the free-and-easy air
of a coachman with no more style than a ’bus
driver. Two ladies, who one day hired a vehicle
of decayed splendor to visit some opulent acquaint-
ance in a far-distant suburb, on emerging from
the house disgustedly observed the equipage
drawn up in the shade with the spirited steeds
dejectedly drooping their heads, the coachman
lying under the tree sound asleep, and a general
air of “ four-bob robbo ” about the whole turn-
out, quite visible to the hostess watching the
departure between the slats of her drawing-room
blind.
After a “ toney ” ball at one of the Mountain
resorts, an ambitious damsel, who had broken
through the rigidity of the district caste laws and
been chaperoned by a lofty visiting magnate, was
escorted home by an impressionable partner. She
didn’t want him to see that pa’s house was
weatherboard, with four rooms and a lean-to, and
so passed the front gate innocently, and didn’t
pause until they came to a two-storeyed gabled
mansion, standing in aristocratic seclusion behind
a tree avenue. “As it’s so very late, I shan’t ask
you in,” she said, sweetly, at the gate. But, “ Oh,
I’m going in myself,” the young man from town
replied, “ my family are renting this place for the
summer.” So the poor thing had to own up, and
was hurled for evermore from among the seats of
the local mighty.
In the backblocks, where there are no benches
in the parks, the necessary support to the local
love-making is given by logs. There is a way-
back township where—as in many others—the
chief evening’s amusement for a girl is to be taken
into an adjacent paddock and hugged. A new-
chum was taken round the place by a prominent
townsman and shown the places of interest—
among others the noted courting-spots, each log
the property of a particular couple. “That’s
Jessie Brown and Jack Smith’s log; that one’s
Susie Blank’s log.” “But which is yours?”
asked the stranger. “Oh,” said the leading
citizen importantly, “/have the goods-shed up
at the railway-station.”
Writes my Melb. correspondent :
“One of Victoria’s richest squatting crowds
starved out the local shepherd a little while back
because a lady of the clan took to herself his re-
marks on that Biblical liigh-kicker, Herodias’
daughter. The present incumbent promptly
poured oil and honey into the wounds dealt by his
predecessor. But, for all his crying ‘Peace,
Peace ! ’ there is no peace in the kirk at present’
owing to a fierce family feud between two factions
of his principal patrons—the great people aforesaid.
The trouble began on a recent Sunday when the
head of the house, arriving late for church with a
party of vice-regal visitors, found the ancestral
curtained pew monopolised by a younger brother
and his comparatively insignificant following. So
far from vacating the hallowed ground, the
usurper calmly ignored the newcomers, to the fury
of the rightful owner, who skipped down the aisle
snorting audibly, and vainly ordered the local con-
stable, whom he met outside, to rush into the
sanctuary there and then and eject his bad little
brother.
“ Daylesford has been all agog lately over the
enforced return of its prayerful prodigal lawyer
Geake and the emptiness of the husks left for his
creditors. The other day, when he was hourly
expected back from Monte Video, an outsider
suggested to the wanderer’s advance guardian that
it would be hard work keeping the defrauded
widows and spinsters of the district from scratch-
ing out the absconder’s eyes. ‘No fear ! ’ replied
the official, who knew his Daylesford, ‘ every in-
solvent she in the place, if she had the show, would
gladly hang round his neck, and, as of old, take
her little bill to the Lord in prayer on his recom-
mendation.’
“ Whether due to the effect of mineral water on
the system or to the influence of empty shafts and
deserted batteries on the nerves, Geake’s fellow
townsmen impress the newcomer as an emotional
and confiding community. Theosophists, palmists,
and spiritualists are all more or less in evidence,
and a sententious ancient, who professes to cure
erysipelas by reciting a sentence or two from the
German grammar, is an honored guest at fashion-
able afternoon teas. Her popularity is quite dis-
interested too, for, unlike green-apple colic, ery-
sipelas is by no means prevalent in Daylesford.
“ Melb. shopkeepers complain that while they
are blamed for working their employes until mid-
night, the customers themselves are the culprits.
Women habitually delay their shopping till the
eleventh hour and then linger lovingly at the
counters. On Xmas Eve the hands at the biggest
sell-all firm in Melb. almost despaired of tasting
the morrow’s goose. Customers arrived in crowds,
and the later it grew the thicker they came. At
last the head of the house himself was summoned
to close the doors, but it was one o’clock before he
succeeded, and then there was all the tidying up to
do. While this was going on, a belated customer
battered outside and shrieked that she hadn’t a
morsel of butter or cheese for Xmas day, until, for
the sake of peace, she was let in by a side door.
“In the same way, boarding-house keepers and
proprietors of seaside resorts wash their hands of
the hysterics, varicose veins, housemaids’ knees,
and similar fatigue complaints prevalent amongst
holiday parlor-maids and waitresses. Intending
tourists wait until all the houses are full, then they
badger the various hosts with imploring telegrams
and offers to share beds and dine on tinned fish.
Whether these overtures are declined or not, the
laggards arrive just the same at all hours of the
night and day, carrying crying children and trust-
ing to Providence for bed and board. At this
stage the domestic staff loses its head, and pru-
dent lodgers, who came in time, have to suffer for
the sins of others, for no one ever gets his second
brew of tea in his own cup or uses a towel not
borrowed from someone else’s room.
“ Talking of the sea-side, Lome, where Pre-
mier Reid went these holidays, was deservedly
popular for many years as a place frequented by
eligible bachelors. Now that most of the Victorian
men have gone to W.A., the girls they left behind
them feel shockingly lone and lorn. On every
side, boys still at mammy’s apron-strings con-
descend loftily to their fiancees, the belles of their
respective suburbs. The plisrht of plainer damsels
is even more pitiable. This year at Lome,
attracted, no doubt, by the old reputation of the
spot, the proportion of girls to men was roughly
computed as 60 to 10.
“ The level-headed mother of the two leading
Melb. lady-doctors has plenty of maternal
admiration for her girls, but precious little
respect for the patients who supply them with
practice. The old lady doesn’t believe in sickness,
medical attendance, and physic. There was never
any serious sickness in her family, she says, and if
there had been she would have cured it all herself
without sending for a doctor, male or female.
Evidently the enthusiastic daughters didn’t in-
herit their taste for the profession from dear
mamma.
“ Mrs. Maesmore Morris, the Melbourne
beauty, who has hitherto been on show privately,
is now to ‘go on ’ in the ‘ Prisoner of Zenda’ at
the Princess’s for Williamson and Musgrove, and
exhibit herself to all and sundry. She is worth
looking at, being as near to the ideal beauty of
the lid of a chocolate-cream box as one has ever
seen. She has a delicate pink and white skin,
which stage-paint is not likely to improve, and an
expression that brings to mind Tennyson’s line
‘ insipid as the queen upon a card.’
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

SPORTING NOTIONS .
The Martin-Lewis match was a dreary fiasco
perpetrated in the midst of blinding sleet and mud. The
one-mile event looked square. Martin rode as usual,
hanging behind his man until half-a-lap from home, when
he rushed out. Lewis made his dash same moment, but
when there was 100yds. to go Martin was up to his elbow.
Then the Australian caught sight of the Yank’s rush, and,
making another effort, ran in a winner by half a length.
In deference to the public, who howl unless a race is run
at top speed from start to finish, the mile was paced, but
badly.
The ten-miles looked very fishy. As early as
three miles Lewis looked thoroughly done up, while the
iron man was plugging away joyfully half-a-lap in front.
But suddenly the Yank failed to follow his pace, and in
two more rounds the competitors were together. Then
Lewis took a turn in front, and Bill’s shoe started coming
off his foot, or his foot started coming out of his shoe—
the officials say ; to the outsider the appearance of both
acts is much the same—and he didn’t get a move on him
until half-a-lap from home. Then came Martin’s charac-
teristic run just a length too late. Bill’s boot must have
damaged his judgment as well as his riding.
The Five-Mile race, run off for =£2s—added by
the League in the event of one man winning the first two
races—was a walk-over for the Yank., who went out from
the first and lapped his opponent at three miles.
Then a meeting was held without Bill, who had
left the ground, and it was decided that the absent guest
had been working too hard of late, and that he should
rest for two years. Lewis, whose riding appeared to out-
siders fair and square throughout, was suspended until
Tuesday, when his case was to be heard.
Betting on the three events of the match : One
mile, 3tol on Lewis; ten miles, 5 to 4 on Lewis; five
miles, stol on Martin. They know something at St.
Kilda, as J. G. Knowles would say.
Bilmartin, while alleging that he did his best
throughout, blames the Red Bird man for his defeat by
not providing pace for him; with even pacing, he says,
he could beat Lewis any time over 10 miles—which ali
spectators of Saturday endorse.
Martin’s pacing arrangements were execrable,
and none of the riders appeared to have any knowledge
of their places or to know who was steering the machines.
Three men, obviously novices on multicycles, got on one
of the triplets, and they hadn’t gone 50yds. before they
fell. Another trial was productive of another fall, and
the men were then led away. On the other hand, Martin
numbered among his pacers a triplet steered by Hourigan,
the same man who acted as helmsman in the best team
at the Yank’s disposal in his match with Parsons.
Bilmartin is dead as far as Australia is con-
cerned. for, though he may appeal to the Racing Board,
there is not even Butler’s chance of the stewards’ decision
being reversed. If Bill was guilty, his punishment was
richly deserved, and no lover of sport could do otherwise
but endorse it, yet all who have been used to seeing the
pluck, stamina, and extraordinary determination of the
junky little roan when his adamantine jaw hung over the
handlebars can help dropping a passing tear at the thought
that Australian tracks will know the red and white hoops
no more.
Martin threatens to ride at unregistered meet-
ings if rubbed out finally, and savs that perhaps he’ll
make more money there than under the League. Perhaps.
But whom will they get to ride against him ?
The 10-mile championship of Victoria was won
by Kuhle, the lanky man from South Melb., who has been
making some marvellous sprints this season, while yet
scoring not a single big win. Kuhle is one of the best, if
not the best, finisher Australia possesses. Porta, who
ran second, protested against Kuhle for crossing him
within the length-limit, and had his five shillings for-
feited for frivolity.
“ I ain’t an angel. I’m a man tryin’ to make
all the money I can, but I wan ter make the money
honestly and honorably.” Bilmartin in his protestation
of innocence.
Martin claims that he was disqualified but once
before reaching Australia, and then unfairly, and at no
time was he ever accused of non-trying. By the w*ay
Martin never rode in America under the L. A.W.; his
performances in the States were all at unregistered
meetings.
• • • •
After bursting Bilmartin’s records on Tuesday
at St., Kilda track, Lesna was simply “dead to the
world.” Yet all he did was to out down the Yank’s flying
start five miles by 11 3-ssec. and his 10 miles by 39 4-sseo.
from a stand. Lesna had everything favorable. There
was not a breath of air, and he waited three-quarters of
an hour for the track to dry before he mounted his
machine. Also, he had as good, or better, pacing than
Martin, and every incentive to travel.
Lesna’s stable aver that Martin was only a
third-class man on good Continental tracks ; but the pro-
babilities are that this same third-classer will knock the
bottom out of the Gaul’s times when he next tries.
Lesna’s two “managers,”Raymond Pognon and
Prosper Ulmo, are the most striking figures on Melb.
tracks at present, and their coats are things of loveliness
which drive thrills of envy through the soul of local
? i'i nl ™T'L Pognon affects a loose flapping - tailed
clobber which gyrates like the draperies of a skirt-dancer
as he gesticulates; Ulmo, who is the elder and of the
more serious type of Frenchman, is enveloped in a huge
brown ulster all pleats and pearl buttons, which merely
permits his hat to peep over the collar and allows his feet
to stray beneath its skirt. To see Pognon, Ulmo and
Lesna conferring is one of the sights of the day.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bdurke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ;5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery : 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

Personal Items.
Recent Sydney occurrences leave no doubt
whatever that the Pot and the Kettle were press-
men.
Dan O’Connor is a type of the numerous
class of people who invariably say “ expectorate ”
when they mean “ spit.”
“C. Kendall,” author of the verses “ In
the Half-Light,” printed on page 28 of this issue
of The Bulletin, is a son of the late Henry
Kendall.
Chas. Hallo’s recently-published memoirs
refer to that remarkable Sydney Chinaman,
“ Geeong Tarr.” Which is indeed a cruel blow to
local Presbyterianism.
Queen Vic.’s invitation to the Australian
Premiers to visit London for her 60-years’ celebra-
tion is evidently based on false hopes. All the
Australian Premiers are married.
Puzzle—To find the speaker (a Sydneyite) :
“ One of the best, one of the nicest, one of the
kindest, one of the noblest, one of the dearest, one
of the honorablest, my dearest friend,” &c., &c.
Y ankee cyclist Zimmerman was the essence
of politeness. An Adelaide pedestrian got in the
way and brought his machine to a standstill.
“Say,” drawled Zimmy, “will you please go to
hell! ”
W. Redmond, M.P. for Home Rule Land,
Btands Australian heat well. He has added a
stone to his avoirdupois since arrival, and has
prospects of further substantial increase before he
leaves. Parnell is to him as great a hero as ever.
Louis Becke’s marine “jungle stories,
which were begun in Pall Mall Gazette during
Cust’s editorship, and ceased when Oust and Astor
quarrelled and the former left, are now being con-
tinued in the Daily News and Daily Chronicle.
Oust has gone to South Africa.
Obit.: T. J. West, son of James West,
one of the first settlers at Macquarie Plains when
Bathurst was a penal settlement. “ Tom ” was
born on the Plains, and at one time represented
Carcoar in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly—a circum-
stance which was simply his ruin. At that time
he owned the beautiful homestead of Cudgelo,
was a leading sportsman, and the best handicapper
of his day.
Miss Anne Jane Duncan, who becomes
first female inspector of N.S.W. factories (at .£lBO
a year) won the appointment from a large number
of highly-educated and socially-influential women.
In fact, her appointment proves how necessary
independent commissioners are, for had a Minister
of the Crown been patron he would have found it
as much as his office was worth to have resisted
the influence behind many of the candidates.
If Premier Reid should decide, despite
his present refusal, to visit England to cele-
brate Queen Victoria’s record reign, he will
command the silence of many of his political
opponents who countenanced G. Republican
Dibbs in carrying the Premiership abroad on
a no more useful mission. When Dibbs went
Barton became Acting-Premier. When Stuart
went to Maoriland, Dailey acted for him. And
when Reid goes (if he goes), Carruthers will be
his deputy. Old Parkes always liked to take the
Premiership about with him when he went abroad.
C. G. Wade, N.S.W. acting
District Court Judge, is spoken
of as a certainty for the first per-
manency on that bench. He
is an old King’s-school boy,
and son of the late Burton
Wade, chief-engineer of N.S.W.
railways.
X. Z. : “ The other day I
met a curious case of incipient
mania. The trouble was that the
patient’s particular form of mad-
ness was the idea that he was
going mad. The fact that he was
becom i n g ord inar ily un m anageable
was plain; at the same time his
knowledge of the same fact would
have to be taken as proof of his
sanity. The problem is left for
others to solve.” The law is—it
may be an expedient but it is a
most unphilosophical law —that
the man would be fully responsible
for his actions. The law does not
recognise the co-existence of an
irresistible impulse to do wrong
and a knowledge of the difference
between right and wrong.
Minister Peacock, of the
wondrous chortle, was leaning up
against Melb. Exhibition Building
sunning himself, after doing good
service for his country on A.N.A.
when a small, sticky
Australian, with a grimy face,
leading a smaller, stickier, and
more grimy brother by the hand,
interviewed him. “You’re Mr.
Peacock, ain’t yer?” asked the
diminutive native. The great
Minister nodded. “ Look here,”
continued the small boy, “I’ll
give you this apple if you’ll laff
fer my little brother, he wants t’
’ear yah.” Aleck laughed until
the dome shook, and when he re-
covered the ytgng Australians had
disappeared, and he held an apple
in his hand.
E. Y. Lowry, who went to prison as a
Socialist martyr once, on being re-elected as a
Townsville (N.Q.) alderman, last week, declined
to thank those who had voted for him, declaring
that it was their duty to thank him for having
devoted his time to their interests.
Sargood will not yet retire from the
directorship of the Commercial Bank of Australia.
He had intended to go to Japan with his family,
but will now delay his trip till next year, owing
to the Federal Convention, to which he expects to
be elected. The Commercial management blesses
the occasion, therefore, because Sargood’s retire-
ment would have been bound to be interpreted
with many embellishments of Rumor.
The candidature of W. D. Flinn, the
socialistic Yarra. Bank orator, for the Federal
Convention, is ridiculed by both the Melb. dailies,
who have poked borak at the man until the public
has got to regard him as a cross between Billy
Maloney and the rnoSt blatant idiots of the Yarra
Bend Asylum. A.s a matter of fact, Flinn, despite
his association with the windbags of the city, is
a sensible, level-headed man, with a better general
education and a keener grasp of political subjects
than three-fourths of Vic. Assembly, and four-
fourths of the Council. He is a republican, an
Australian nationalist, a state socialist, but is
neither a teetotal nor W.C.T.U. crank. The
great objection to Flinn, as far as The Bulletin
can see, is that while he used to appear before the
public as William D. Flinn, he is now announced
as a Federal candidate as W. Dudley Flinn. At
this rate Dudley-Flinn, with a hyphen, is only a
matter of time.
Can Theosophy provide any explanation,
Karmic or otherwise, of the evil influence which
apparently attaches to the name Butler? Also,
why is it selected, criminally, in disproportion to
other names ? Some 30 years ago, in London, an
unholy vampire, who made a practice of waylaying
servant-girls after dark and murdering them on
the Burke and Hare principle, christened himself
Butler without baptismal warrant. Dr. Palmer,
of poisonous memory, once signed himself “ But-
ler ”in a hotel register. A notable American mur-
derer of a few years back was run to earth through
representing himself to be a certain J. Butler,
who, unknown to him, happened to be “ wanted ”
at the very time for embezzlement. The Bow-st.
police-cases for December last include no fewer
than four Butlers, two of them aliases, and one
with 38 previous convictions recorded against him !
Taking into consideration our own housebreaking
and corpse-prospecting Butlers, it is perhaps pre-
ferable to be known as Jones these times.
Kipling backs down several times in his
“ Seven Seas,” the nasty remarks in the verses as
originally published being carefully eliminated
from the collection. The most striking instance
is in the “Song of the English,” in the version of
which recited at the Himperial Hinstitoot
Quebec says:—
From my grey scarp I watch with scornful eyes
Ignobie broil of Freedom most unfree.
Fear nothing, mother, where the carcase lies
The Unclean Bird must be.
This most insulting reference to Yankeeland is
knocked out entirely in the book, and there is
substituted a verse marked “ Quebec and Mont-
real,” viz. :
Peace is our portion. Yet a whisper rose
Foolish and causeless, half in jest, half hate.
For wake we and remember mighty blows,
And, fearing no man, wait 1
Which means nothing in particular. Kipling is
like a lot of men who talk fight largely. When
the trouble starts they are wanting.
M. A. NOBLE, the N.S.W. Batsman.
Apropos Q. Premier “ Nevermind” Nelson.
When Morrah was permanent head of Vic. Lands
and Works, all awkward deputations used to be
fixed for days when the Cabinet head would be
absent, and Morrah faced the music with a stereo-
typed “ I will see, gentlemen, that the matter re-
ceives the full consideration that is undoubtedly
due to it.” It became so aggravating that at last
a deputatic nist said : “ Your name’s Morrah, I
believe ; yo I’d better change it to To-Morrah ! ”
THE SUPERIOR RACE.
HORSE-HUNTER: “ Did you notice a grey horse about here, John?”
ABORIGINEE (very indignant): “Baal me John —John b’long that phela
Chinaman. My name Jacky Jacky.”
Myenberg, of Mt. Morgan fame, has just
died at Rockhampton (Q.). He was the first man
to take out a miner’s right in Q., and the first to
erect a battery. The old man always considered
he was entitled to a share in the original Mt.
Morgan claim, and fought it out at law to his
ultimate ruin. The decision meant millions to the
syndicate, and the result ruined four Queensland
men of note. Outsiders always considered that
Myenberg had a just claim to a fortune, but he
died in poverty.
It is related that some years ago John
Hay, driving on a N.S.W. country road, was
blocked by a teamster—“ You don’t know who I
am, my good man,” said John, “ I am Sir
John Hay, Knight.’' The teamster kept on. “ I
don’t give a damn who you are—l’m Flash Jack
of Bathurst to-night, to-morrow night, and the
night after.”
Queensland’s hangman is a burly, grey-
mutton-chop-whiskered old Englishman of the
class that clings fondly to the woollen-wrapper in
winter. His good, broad back is stooped a trifle,
but he carries the weight of 60 years with un-
common spryness—as, somehow or other, hang-
men mostly do. Not long since the gaol officials,
having some “cat” work on hand, sized him up
and, noticing that his arms through lack of play
began to wear a flabby look, decided that a
younger man ought to be sent for. The wily
slayer, however, got wind of the conspiracy, went
at once into retirement with an assortment of
clubs and dumb-bells, and afterwards gave a
couple of garotters such “a father of a cutting”
that his fame was established in the prison for all
time. He receives a retainer of <£l2s a year
with a capitation allowance of a guinea for
hangings, and still is not happy. No fewer
than seven subjects marked by the Judges for
slaughter have been reprieved lately by the Gover-
nor in Council, and thus, besides forfeiting the
moral solace which must accompany the exercise
of his art, the old gentleman has been a financial
loser to the extent of seven guineas.
Writes a Bulletin correspondent :
An adventurous and earnest Pole named Kosciuski
lately arrived in N.S.W. with a scheme for a flying-
machine, and has unobtrusively set about making it on
the shore of Lake Macquarie, where he is camped for the
purpose. By profession he is a master-mariner, and he
came out as mate in the ship King Arthur. For years he
has, like other sailors, watched with scientific apprecia-
tion the seemingly effortless perfection of the albatross’s
sailing-gear, which he believes he can produce in a con-
cern built of bamboo rods and silk, worked with pulleys,
guys and stays in nautical fashion that will compel pro-
pulsion in any required direction provided there is a
breeze on hand. A centra! frame or body—as of a bird—
has been made, 10ft. long and sft. wide. Sprouting out
of this are two wings, each 20ft. long, tapering to a point
from a width of 4ft. A forked tail set latitudinally,
platypus fashion, is cunningly devised as a rudder.
Each fork of the tail can be moved up or down or
otherwise according to varying conditions. Kosciuski
will occupy a saddle like that of a bike, his head only
appearing above the frame. By depressing his feet the
wings will be raised ; they will fall without manipulation.
Some three weeks have been occupied in making the
machine, and it is just about ready for trial. When ready
the inventor will take a header with it from a neighboring
cliff. As showing the self-containedness of the man he
declined solid financial aid in America, and crept into
his hidden location to work out the scheme alone, almost
without financial means. Beyond a saw and a jack-knife
he is not known to have had any tool-.

The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough.*

POLITICAL

The chronic insolvency of Australian poli-
ticians—M. P. ouniosity.
“ Federation is to block out the Republic
with cast-iron precautions.”— Dudley Flinn.
The voting at the Federal Council showed
that there is a tacitly-understood defensive alli-
ance between Kanakaland and Westralia.
Hoolan, M.L.A. : “In 1893 the Labor-
party were accused of trying to break the Q.N.
Bank, and now that institution looks as if it
would break the Labor-party.” The split over the
bills certainly emphasised the possibility.
Cook, M.L.A., Victorian president of the
Natives’ Association, frankly tells his constituents
he has “lived to see the folly of having pledged
himself to support a reduction in the number and
the payment of members of the Leg. Assembly.”
Instead of sneering at ‘ ‘ democrats, with a
big D ” on every possible occasion, Sydney Daily
Telegraph should concentrate its intellect on
pointing out the difference between the Reid
variety of Freetrade and the common or garden
sort of Protection.
There are now two N.S.W. Federal Con-
vention candidates named Abbott—big Joe Abbott
and his brother, W. E. Abbott, the strike-and-
letter-writing bore. “There are two pests in
N.S.W.—the rabbit-pest and the Abbott-pest,”
said Abigail on an immortal occasion.
Cardinal Moran’s Federation candidature
is not the first move of the kind in these parts.
Some years back, Bishop Moran, of Dunedin,
fighting the everlasting education-question battle,
put up for a seat in Maoriland Parliament, but
apparently had no show of getting in, and with-
drew
Australia’s love of England is entirely
selfish. It is all what is to be done for us. The
sheltering fegis of the shadow of the wing of
England. Let there be a bit of a pinch—England
calling for help, the time come for us to put our
hands in our pockets and furnish ironclads and
troops for service abroad. Then —no fear.
Unreported question asked at R. E. O’Con-
nor’s Federal meeting at Narrabri : “ Would you
be in favor of the iaw officers of the Federal
Executive accepting briefs from persons sueing
the Government in courts of law ? ” Candidate :
“ I consider this question a personal insult aimed
at myself, and I therefore decline to answer it.”
Naturally, the Tory Brisbane papers, that
whooped for the Nelson Govt, in everything that
it did to “save the Q.N. Bank,” are hounding
down that same Govt, because it proposes to spend
JGISOO to bring back to Queensland 10 destitute
men, .With their 10 wives and 40 children, from
Paraguay —about the only decently generous
action which the Nelson Govt, has performed.
Vic. Govt, employs Chows on its experi-
mental farm in the King River district, and when
howled at replies that it doesn’t employ them
much. The servant-girl’s excuse, “only a little
one.” The Government that poll-taxes Chows to
save the whites from their ruinous competition,
and then gives them work to the exclusion of
Europeans, is both ends and the barrel of an ass.
That quaint politician, Arthur Renwick,
of N.S.W., is of opinion that “as the railways
are such a valuable asset and had cost the colony
so much expense to build they should be reserved
for local management.” Apparently Arthur
Renwick actually thinks that “ the colony ” has
paid for its railways. So far they haven’t cost
N.S.W. anything to build (though the deficit in
the earnings has cost it something), and they have
cost the other provinces remarkably little.
Card. Moran announced himself a candi-
date for the Federal Convention last Friday, with
a platform of religion for a basis, Sydney for a
capital, no railway or fiscal federation at present,
and no injustice to be done to “ my own colony ”
—altogether as ass-like and parochial a platform
as anybody’s. Syd. Daily Telegraph was ready
next morning with a leader denouncing the eccle-
siastical candidature ; but S.M. Herald waited—
to make sure how the public cat would jump.
Q. Premier Nelson has roused the rage of
his political friends and of the Brisbane Tory
press by preaching (in effect) at the Federal
Council that the New Hebrides might go toSheol,
or to France. This attitude is dead against his
traditions, no matter how artfully he and his
colleagues may attempt to modify it, by asserting
that it was hypothetically hung upon an “ if ” — if
Great Britain wouldn’t take over the islands.
For Nelson is not only a creature of the
MTlwraith who tried to capture the whole of New
Guinea by a coup d’dtat, but he is a Scotch Pres-
byterian, and the Scotch Presbyterians have by
cocoa-nut ventures got “ the sword of the spirit ”
very deep into the carcase of the New Hebrides.
Rather than forfeit their half-million of
interprovincial tariff import revenue and replace
this by a property or land-tax, Westralia’s repre-
sentatives stand out of Federation.

Gov. Buxton has returned to S.A. for a
week or two. His next trip will probably be to
England and America for two years. As a
travelling Governor, H.E. easily beats Kin tore,
who held the previous Australian record.
Trentham (Vic.) contains 340 people. At
a meeting of a portion of said population it was
decided to invite all the 32 Vic. Federal Conven-
tion candidates to visit Trentham and personally
place their views before the inhabitants of this
possible federal capital. It is understood that
candidates from other provinces will also be wel-
come.
A WOMAN'S USES .
HE (teased about sweethearts): “Em never going to get married . What's the good of a
wife?”
HIS THOUGHTFUL SISTER : “ But, then, if a burglar comes, who’ll hold him down while you
beat him?”
John Haynes, M.L.A., says that he is a
Federationist, but is against Federation as at
present proposed, because “N.S.W. will have
three votes to one against her in the Federal
Parliament.” (Each of the other provinces will
also have three votes to one against her, but that
fact doesn’t enter into John’s simple philosophy.)
Obviously, as John wants Federation, but doesn’t
want N.S.W. to have three votes to one against
her, his idea is that N.S.W. should have four
votes, and the other three provinces one each, or
something like that, and then the matter will be
settled quite simply.
Ex-Chief Justice Lilley (Q.) longs for the
flesh-pots of Egypt. Stated that he will take a
hand in a gathering of labor leaders at Longreach
in March.
Maoriland has got all that the chartists
fought for.
L. F. Heydon, Sydney’s vociferous lawyer,
writes denouncing N.S.W. Federationists in
general as traitors who are prepared to sell their
country for a prospective billet in the Federal
Parliament. Heydon also compares them hysteri-
cally to Bazaine arid Dreyfus, and generally asks
his country to beware of the men who want to
sell it for lucre. This naturally sets people
wondering if Heydon gets anything, present or
prospective, for running his little provincial
racket, and what he wants to sell his country to
the Sydney importers for, and how things stand
generally. The Bulletin wouldn’t have suspected
Heydon of being anything save a very dull and
narrow and ordinary person, but for his eagerness
to suspect everybody except Heydon.
Six Parliamentary candidates were chosen
by the Hobart Licensed Victuallers and their
names posted in the pubs, as candidates “ favor-
ing legislation in the interest of the trade.” A week
later the Council of Churches publicly announced
that the Godly should vote straight for three of the
same aspirants. Asked to explain the paradox a
prominent Church Council dignitary hinted that
the publicans had been “ had.” Bamboozled by
Chadband ? Rats ! Another parson, a virulent
cold-tea and anti-Tattersall apostle, admitted that
as many of his congregation held shares in the
Cascade brewery monopoly he had to temper the
heat of his discourses to the requirements of the
fat and woolly lambs. Which accounts for many
things.
The three ages of Australian Federation—
-1856, 1876, 1897. In the first, engineered by
Deas-Thomson, the general principle was laid
down, and N.S.W., Victoria, S. Australia, and
Tasmania named as the contracting parties.
These, so far as names go, are, after 40 years,
again the parties to the desired contract. In the
second age, Gov. Hercules Robinson oraturically
created a Federation ideal. His speech was prob-
ably the most widely effective of gubernatorial
utterances in Australia. He introduced Queens-
land and Westralia into the union, and for the
time being patriots saw a new national birth. But
politicians seized, fought over it, and strangled
the infant. Now, in ’97, the purpose has once
more sprung to vital existence, and—but there are
a lot of lawyers about the cradle.
A Bulletin correspondent who “ ought to
know ”:—
“Toby” Barton is wrong in asserting that N.S.W.
Premier Reid, when in Brisbane on his “ Federation mis-
sion,” was not told that anybody there was averse to it
until after he had quitted Q. territory. Reid knew all
f/om the start. What happened was this : The same train
that took Reid from Sydney carried also a press reporter
(two, but this par. concerns one only). Reid stayed at
Toowoomba on a Sunday en route ; the reporter went on
to Brisbane. On Monday, the rep. went round to pro-
minent Brisbane citizens merchants, manufacturers,
clubmen, politicians, etc. —and “sounded” them.
Whether he did this for Reid or his paper matters not.
On Monday evening Reid came on from Toowoomba
(where he had been Premier Nelson’s guest), and the rep.
made a confidential report to him, as thus: “No use
calling on A., 8., C., D., etc.); they’re dead against you;
try L., M., N., 0., etc. ; they are in favor of federation."
The “ mission ” was all readied up. Mr. Barton ought to
know that all such political missions are readied up. Reid
simply didn’t call on those who would have told him that
his “ mission” wasn’t palatable.
The great Q. political question is now,
Are you a Byrnesite or a Tozerite? Brisbane
Courier is coming out as a frank Tozerite.
Byrnes’s insufferable arrogance is one reason —
people won’t stand it; while Tozer is the most in-
gratiating person in politics. Call him a liar (a
political liar, of course); prove to him that he is a
liar ; and he will merely brush the accusation
aside as irrelevant and flood you with his ideas on
the question you have nearest your heart. Then
politician Byrnes is entirely under the priestly
thumb, and is believed to be pledged to re-establish
denominational ed,ucation if he gets half a chance
—and the Courier knows the’ majorityßeeling too
well to risk that. So it jeers at the Federal Coun-
cil which Byrnes upholds—calls it a picnic, and
points out that Byrnes’s and Nelson’s bombast
that “ Queensland thought ” the council all that is
required at present, was bombast merely; since
Queensland’s opinion has never been asked,
and Nelson and Byrnes have carefully shirked
asking it. “If the Federal Council is to sit in
Melb,” says the Courier, “let it be associated
with the Cup Carnival. And meanwhile, when
we want specific federal action, something with
‘ business ’ in it, let us ask Mr. TOZER to person-
ally conduct the necessary negotiations.”
The Election of Federal Convention dele-
gates is fixed for March 4, and The Bulletin pro-
poses before then to issue a Federal ticket for
N.S.W., Victoria, and S.A., and possibly for Tas-
mania also, if the sleepy candidates of that isle
only sort themselves out in time, and grow a little
more definite in their utterances. For each pro-
vince this paper will endeavor to suggest ten men
who are democratic, with clean records, patriotic
in the Australian, not merely the provincial, sense,
with sufficient brains to fix up a workable consti-
tution and sufficient business knowledge not to get
wrecked among the intricate financial matters which
have to be settled. So far as it has got with the
lists, it has had to strike out the names of some
good men—good in a general sense—because the
frantic absurdity of their demands on behalf of
their own particular province would have made
things hopeless from the start; and some more
because they showed such profound ignorance of
facts and figures that to entrust them with intri-
cate questions of finance looks about as absurd as
entrusting a bear with a theodolite. The Bulle-
tin’s Federal ticket will at least be as perfect as
this paper knows how to make it, and it respect-
fully requests all its readers to vote that ticket—
or as much of it as their consciences will allow.
It also suggests to all those people who
haven’t the time or the energy to read up the
innumerable speeches of the candidates, and
add up their sentiments and opinions, and draw
up a list for themselves, that they might as well
vote The Bulletin ticket as any other. And it
asks all electors to vote, whether they vote The
Bulletin ticket or not, if only for the sake of
showing that Federation is a live question and not
a mere remains. The Bulletin ticket isn’t a
party one in particular it is absolutely independ-
ent of the “ fiscal issue also, it isn’t a list of the
men whom this paper thinks will get in but of
those who, in its opinion, ought to get in—which
is quite a different matter.


Notwithstanding the general
during the past year, the inevitable aceompani'
ment of a Presidential election, the Equitable
Life Assurance Society of the United States has
completed a, remarkable year’s business. The
result is the increase of the: society’s assets by the
very large sum of £3,284,283, making a total of
<£45,161,239, equivalent to 125 per cent, of the
total liabilities of the society ; an increase of over
half a million sterling in the amount of assurances
in force, which now reach the enormous aggregate
of <£190,646,265 ; and the surplus, which for many
years has been larger than that of any other life
officeJn the world, has been still further increased
by -£552,774, and now stands at nearly nine mil-
lions sterling. This surplus may be regarded as
not only a guarantee of future bonuses but also
relied upon by policy-holders as a safeguard
against the remote and unknown contingencies of
the future.*
A thing which is always worth noting is a
good brand of tobacco, therefore “ Two Seas ” is
especially worthy of note because it is excellent.
The smoker who has not tried this brand is behind
the times in his own profession ; he is missing real
enjoyment and trying to content himself with a
substitute. “Two Seas’’has taken many hand-
some gold medals —the last big Exhibition at
Chicago added one more to the list. The fact of
these gold medals is not offered as the encourage-
ment to the public, because the public cannot
smoke gold medals. The real “ all gold ” is the
tobacco itself—aromatic, light or dark. Nobody
ever remembered “ Two Seas ” for the purpose of
ever afterwards avoiding it, nor does any fond
father give it to his boy in order to break the boy
of smoking. But it comes as a boon to all—-to
those who always get it, and to those who resolve
on reading this paragraph to give it an honest
trial.*

THE COUNTRY TO SYDNEY AND
THE MOUNTAIN DISTRICTS.
FRIDAY, 19th FEBRUARY, 1897.
In order to afford facilities for Country Resi-
dents to enjoy a change to the cool of the Moun-
tain Districts and the Seaside, SPECIAL CHEAP
EXCURSION TICKETS will be issued to
SYDNEY on 19th February.
SOUTHERN LINE.
From Albury, Harden, and intermediate sta-
tions, including branches, as under :
# On the Corowa Branch these tickets will be
issued on THURSDAY, 18th February.
WESTERN LINE.
Tickets will be issued to Sydney at Bourke,
Cobar, Wellington, and intermediate stations;
also at Parlies, Forbes, Bogan Cate, and inter-
mediate stations, as under :
NORTHERN LINE.
Tickets will be issued to Sydney at Tamworth,
Narrabri, Murrurundi, and intermediate stations
as under: —
Return Tickets to Newcastle at proportionately
low rates will be issued at the stations named
above, on the Northern Line, by the several trains
mentioned.
Return Tickets at 2d. per mile First-class (mini-

“ The Last of Six.”
Victoria, which has been rather short of a
decent horror of late, has now got one which com-
pletely “lays over” Butler, and can give points
even to Deeming. A few weeks ago one Lyfield was
arrested on a charge of having butchered his wife
then left her body for a few days in an outhouse
(where his daughter and granddaughter looked in
casually at intervals and noted, in a mild, unin-
terested way, the presence of “ mother ” on top of
a heap of sacks), and finally sunk her in the river.
The most interesting feature of the case was the
dull, stolid, agricultural indifference with which
the two women acknowledged, after Lyfield’s
arrest, that they heard “mother ” being killed in
the next room, and that for days afterwards they
kept dropping on the body of “mother” in unex-
pected places, and finally one night they heard
“mother” being hauled away for burial, and
after that didn’t come across her body in
outhouses any more. Since then the two women
have made further revelations, and in their own
slow way they have built up a squalid, shuddery
horror which has few equals in Australia. The
daughter, aged 43, says that she was seduced by
her father as far back as she can remember, and
that there had been “ relations ” between them for
over 30 years. Two children were born, ac-
cording to this dank and mouldy chronicle, and
the man who was their combined father and
grandfather killed them and buried them on
the farm. The third one—a daughter—was
allowed to grow up. Then the mother and
her infant were both passed on to one
Snell, who married the former, partly because
he also had “ relations ” with her, and
partly, it would seem, because the old man pro-
mised him a part of the farm. After six years
Snell somehow dropped out of the hideous narra-
tive, and his wife and her daughter went back to
their old quarters. Then the agricultural house-
father renewed his “relations” with his daughter,
and also started immoral relations with his grand-
daughter-then aged six. Old Lyfield was mar-
ried again by this time, and so he and two women
and a girl led their soulful existence together in
one house for seven years. At the end of that
time the complaints and objections of “ mother ”
led—so the daughter and grand-daughter allege
to her butchery in the next room. There-
fore old Lyfield, aged 70, is in gaol await-
ing trial. His daughter, aged 43, and de-
scribed as a little, hard, lean, wrinkled
expressionless being of no intelligence worth men-
tioning, and the grand-daughter, wooden and aged
13, are waiting around to explain about the body
of “mother” and other matters when the case
comes on. And “ mother ” herself is well clear of
the trouble, and seems more to be congratulated
than anybody else in the story, except, possibly,
Snell, who dropped out so judiciously seven years
ago. The only redeeming feature in the case is
that Lyfield and his daughter both date back to
the days of religious education and were brought
up under the Bible-in-schools policy, and, if their
morals aren’t so beautifully clean as they might
be, the “ godless system of education ” can’t be
saddled with the blame this time.
The strongest piece of circumstantial evi-
dence in the Glenbrook case comes from Captain
Frazer, of the Swanhilda, who states that Butler
was the most pious sailor he had on board. Didn’t
The Bulletin say a week or two ago that he
would probably turn out to be an ex-Sunday-school
superintendent ?
The Q. International Exhibition is born.
It will be a big thing and a little thing. It will
be a success and a non-success. The Brisbane
apathy about it is marvellous. Brisbanites are
the most narcotised business men of Australia.
Time is of no value in the city, whose inhabitants
have for years been hypnotised by a bank, pap-fed
by Govt. The Exhibition may be a success as an
international show ; as a local show, it must be a
dead-sea of dreariness. Brisbane people only
believe in it in the same dreamy fashion that they
believe in Gabriel’s resurrection trumpet.
The old story of the tramp who considered
liquor poor stuff indeed if the drops that fell from
his beard upon his shirt did not burn holes in it,
was recalled in Brisbane City Court the other day.
It appears that while Gabriel (of Mount Morgan
gold-stealing detection and other fame), who has
been sleuth-hounding among the sly-groggeries,
was “ wrestling with a barman to get a flask of
broken-down brandy ” a portion of the liquor spilt
on his coat-sleeve and slurt-cuffs. Next morning
he found holes in said coat-sleeves and shirt-cuffs
wherever the liquor had touched. (Shirt and
coat produced and tendered ; received and marked
as evidence.)


Mr, Thomas Jarvis 9 Step-brother
Interviewed.
HIS VIEWS OF HIS BROTHER’S SUP-
POSED HOPELESS CASE (PARTICU-
LARS OE WHICH APPEARED A
WEEK OR SO AOO IN THIS PAPER.)
WHAT MR. DONOHUE SAYS.
In order to obtain further confirmation of the
Cure wrought by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People (in the ease of Mr. Thos. Jarvis, of 45
Crown-lane, Ultimo, Sydney, who had been told
by the doctors he was incurable), our representa-
tive interviewed Mr. James Donohue, a step-
brother of the patient. Mr. Donohue was dis-
covered hard at work in the boiler of a locomotive
at the Eveleigh Railway workshops. Few men are
better known in trade-union circles than “ Jim ”
Donohue. He is a member of the Iron Trades
Council and is the representative of the Boiler-
makers’ Society. He is the secretary of the rep-
resentative committee of the Eveleigh Railway
workshops. He has always taken the deepest
interest in trades matters, and was secretary for
several years of the Boilermakers’ Society of
N.S.W. He has held responsible positions in Vic-
toria, having been the representative of the Bal-
larat Boilermakers’ Society at the Trades Union
Congress held in Melbourne in April, 1884. He
was also representative of the Melbourne Boiler-
makers’ Society on several occasions in matters
between the Society and employers. He occupied
most intelligently similar positions in Newcastle
and Sydney. Twenty years ago he was a pro-
minent member of the Boilermakers’ Society of
California, and was Chairman of that Society at
San Francisco. The testimony of such a well-
known and representative trade unionist will
speak for itself.
“ Yes,” he said, “ I am a step-brother of Thomas
Jarvis, who is now 35. He is a boilermaker and
was employed at various workshops, and was for
some time with the Colonial Sugar Company at
Fiji. He met with an accident five years ago at
the swimming baths in Pitt street, Sydney. He
was severely scalded about the legs through falling
over an exposed steam pipe. He was bad for
months, but seemed to get alright, but this com-
plaint, which is a conundrum—l fancy it is a form
of paralysis—developed. After the accident he
worked for a considerable time at Glebe Island
abattoirs, and gradually grew worse, ultimately
losing the use of his legs. That occurred two
months ago. The doctors who attended him con-
sidered the
CASE HOPELESS
and reckoned all they could do for him would be
to relieve the torture he was enduring. He was
thus treated with no idea of effecting any cure.
More as an experiment than otherwise, Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People were tried. He
took a box a week and the effect has been marvel-
lous He became better rapidly, and is now using
the fifth box.
I NEVER SAW SUCH A CHANGE
in a man. I didn’t believe in patent medicines as
rule, but have so altered my opinion that I am
recommending Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to all
sufferers of my acquaintance. I may mention
that my daughter, Mrs. Thomas O’Toole, of
Darling-street, Balmain, who has been suffering
from debility, is using these Pills and deriving
great benefit. She was induced to take them
owing to their wonderful efficacy in Tom’s case.
Tom Jarvis expects to be able to
GO TO WORK
in a short time, and he does not want to have
much the matter with him physically to follow
the occupation of a boilermaker. Very naturally,
Tom’s cure has caused quite a sensation among the
boilermakers, and they can hardly credit the
news.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured more than
five thousand cases, besides Mr. Jarvis, of loco-
motor ataxia, nervous exhaustion, rheumatism,
sciatica, muscular weakness, depression, loss of
appetite, palpitation of the heart, headache, early
decay, paralysis, and locomotor ataxy; also all
diseases arising from impoverished and vitiated
blood, such as scrofula, .rickets, St. Vitus dance,
and chronic erysipelas, pale and sallow complexion,
anaemia, wasting diseases like consumption, and
the ailments of ladies. These pills are not a pur-
gative medicine. They contain nothing that will
injure the most delicate system. They are sold by
chemists, or sent (post paid) by Dr. Williams’
Medicine Company, Sydney, on receipt of price in
stamps or post order, 2s. 9d. per box, or 14s. 9d.
per one half-dozen.*
Election Notices.
TO THE ELECTORS OF NEW SOUTH
WALES.
Gentlemen, —In view of the Federal Election
I beg leave to offer myself as a candidate. I
want neither pay nor honors, my hope being that
I may serve my native country at a time when
assistance may be needed. Guided by addresses
and speeches so far, it is not easy to know what is
meant; the scheme promises well for our neigh-
bors. But what advantages are we to derive?
We have the finest country in the world (no empty
boast), and with better government we ought to
do well enough as we are ; still, Federation is de-
sirable, and Queensland, no doubt, would join if
any show of reason presented itself ; but let us
take care we are not to be placed at any dis-
advantage. All sorts of wild schemes and wild
talk will go on, to the confusion of our electors,
and it would be well if many of our politicians
especially could be eliminated for a time, so that
some fair play might be attempted. I appeal to
the thousands of our producers who now deal with
my firm, and to our workers, with whom I have
always sympathised; I can refer to all who have
worked for me. I have worked hard enough my :
self, and my experiences of New South Wales
extend from farming pursuits to the successful
management of one of the largest mercantile busi-
nesses in Australia.
I can also boast of some knowledge of Federa-
tion, both ancient and modern, and promise our
electors that if favored with their choice to devote
my best abilities to their welfare.
Yours sincerely,
JOHN BRIDGE.

Sydney Truth has reduced N.S.W. libel-
law to its lowest terms —which are pretty low.
You can sue Truth civilly for libel, as Seymour
Allen did, and get a verdict for £ 1000 damages,
but you can’t get the cash ; and in bailiff-fees, and
revilings, and general unpleasantness, the verdict
costs you far more than the cash is worth. Or
you can sue Truth’s editor criminally, and then
John Norton gets his chance to orate before a jury
—and he orates well—on that popular topic, the
Freedom of the Press. He has only to convince
one man that no matter what an editor writes, it is
bad, on general principles, to gaol him—a pretty
easy matter —and he leaves the Court two inches
taller. Or he can appeal to the jury’s prejudices
—to their Orangeism if the prosecutor is a Holy
Roman, to their Holy Romanism if the prosecutor
is an Orangeman ; he can appeal to their nation-
ality, to their politics, and—if there’s anything at
all of a proof that he was actuated by public
motives, or even if there isn’t —free he goes. Be-
sides, the double-barrelled libel-law defeats itself.
Prosecuted civilly, John Norton can say : “ Bah !
he doesn’t want to clear his character—he wants
cash.” Prosecuted criminally, it is : “ See !he is
actuated by personal animosity ; he wants ven-
geance ; he doesn’t want to clear his character, or
he would have sued civilly.” Pedestal Truth
and John Nortonas theN.S.W. libel-law reformers,
for they undoubtedly are ! Last Sunday’s issue of
Truth contained two soul-stirring articles, one
about Dibbs and one about somebody else, and
such blasphemy arose that a fog hung over Sydney
all day Monday, but John Norton’s cheerful
countenance glowed through the haze unabashed,
triumphantly. Has he not reformed the N.S.W.
libel-law ?
He Don’t Deny It.
It is not always that people, either socially, com-
mercially, or politically prominent, will boldly
proclaim the author of their happiness, but W. H.
Steadman, Esq., J.P., of Balmain, Sydney,
N.S.W., says, writing on the 26th Nov., 1896 :
“ Warner,s Safe Cure Co. Gentlemen : You will
please pardon me for the liberty I take in thus
communicating with you, never having seen any
member of your firm, but I so much desire to record
my high approbation of the wonderfully meritorious
and curative qualities of your marvellous prepara-
tion— ‘ Warner’s Safe Cure’—and to contribute
my heartfelt testimony to the efficacy of its use, and
to add my humble voice to the grand paean of praise
continually chanted in its favour. I was severely
afflicted with liver and kidney troubles for some
years, but on the advice of several friends in
different parts of the colony, I was induced, after
having been treated by two or three eminently clever
medical men, to try this glorious remedy, with the
most satisfactory results ; and, to-day, I would
earnestly recommend its adoption to all persons
who may be suffering from any form of liver or
kidney affection, with the sure and certain hope of
speedy and permanent recovery. I do not know
that this is of any service to you, but in the in-
terests of suffering humanity, I should be pleased
if you would make this public. I am, sir, yours
faithfully, W. H. Steadman, J.P.”*
