“Bill’’Martin thinks he can keep his bike in
front of Porta’s, and is willing to risk dollars on it.
Martin has still great faith in his wheel of fortune.
* * * *
M. A. Noble, the N.S.W. colt, began interpro-
vincial cricket two years ago by failing in both innings.
To-day he is batting far above what would seem to be his
true form; scoring 71 and 153 not out against Vic., and
last Saturday week 166f0r Paddington against Glebe. His
club average last season was 25 ; this season it was 50
before his 166 score ; his interprovincial average is almost
70. He is 24 years old, and near 6ft. high ; one of a family
of cricketers, he knew the use of bat and ball almost as
soon as he learnt the use of his legs. The man who has
given him most coaching is the prince of soporific bats-
men, Alec Bannerrnan, the small, stern, sad, sleepful
stonewaller. Noble mostly accepted Bannerman’s advice
to disobey it; when told to stonewall he hit and became
a brilliant batsman instead of a baleful blocker. Aus-
tralia wants batsmen who can give the crowd a good go
for its money ; and Noble’s go in both innings was the
best treat the Sydney crowd had in the recent interesting
match. The few times that Noble showed any hesitancy
was when slow, high-tossed ones leaving the hand of
Trott lost themselves momentarily through being in line
with the dark-green foliage of a large fig-tree that grows
outside the fence.
* * * *
Adelaide cricket writers attribute the wretched
fielding of the S.A. eleven to net-practice, and advocate
its abolition. There is some truth in the argument that
it takes constant practice to accept chances in the slips off
a fast bowler like Jones. At the start of the English tour
accomplished fieldsmen like Trumble and Giffen dropped
Jonah wholesale ; in fact, the deposition of George Giffen
from the slips caused some trouble on one occasion.
* * » »
Scene : Sydney cable-tram. Passengers include
youth, straw-hatted, with incipient moustache and bright
yellow shoes, reading sporting paper. Beside him two
Frenchmen jabber in undertone to one another, then—
First Frenchman (turns to youth): “ Tell me, my frien’
—vat ees it, thees Noble now—how mooch he make ? ”
Youth (looks up from paper and surveys Frenchman
critically) : “ A hundred fifty-tree.”
Second Frenchman enthusiastically (throws up his
hands): “ Mon Dieu! He throw one ’ondred fifty-
tree bowls ? Eh ? (turns to his friend.)
First Frenchman (in explanatory tone) : “ No, mussier,
he got one ’ondred and fifty-three legs befor’ wicket.
(Goes into ecstacies.) Magnifique ! Superbe ! One
’ondred and fifty-tree legs befo’ week-ed ! ” (Turns to
friend and explains situation to him confidentially.)
Tram stops. Youth gets out, glances back at French-
men and grins. Tram starts again—first Frenchman still
explaining to companion.
Left explaining.
* • * *
There is much jealousy in Adelaide over the
fact that the Exhibition Oval, recently laid out by Govt.,
is used for the cycling meetings, much to the financial
loss of the S.A. Cricketing Association, which formerly
had a monopoly with Adelaide Oval. The jubilee cycling
track is supposed to be the fastest in Australia.
• * • *
The prospects of the Australasian ’chasers in
England are probably much brighter than the market
odds would lead uninitiated punters to suppose. Norton,
the big Maorilander, has been let into the Liverpool Grand
National at a luxurious weight, whilst Ebor, on his recent
improved form over big jumps, is handicapped within a
few pounds of Daimio, whose Australian reputation is
represented by 12T0. If Ebor be reasonably treated on
his English running, Daimio, according to Australian
reckoning, has a splendid chance of winning the big
event. In any case, the Gibson Bros.’ champion, fit and
well at Liverpool, will test the relative merits of Austra-
lian and English jumpers. Here he would rank as a
“ good thing” for Flemington G.N. Steeplechase at any-
thing less than 13st., therefore his owners can’t grumble
at the Liverpool handicapping without withdrawing their
former opinion that Daimio was equal to the best cross-
country racers at ’Ome.
« * * ♦
The art students of Victoria complain bitterly of
neglect at the late A.N.A. fSte, when £625 were given as
prizes in bicycle races, whereas under £4O was awarded
in the 13 art sections, embodying 300 exhibits. They
seem to think that art ought to have as good a show as
athletics, and don’t feel comforted when reminded that
no prizes were awarded in the department of the noble
science of self-defence —one of the greatest refining and
civilising influences known to human nature.
* * * •
Persistently rumored in Hobart that some
“Tattersall ” money was set spinning at election time
through the L.V. Association. The publicans who didn’t
get “ a fair cut ” from the L.V. disbursements talk of
bursting up the Association from sheer spite.
* * * *
The Corbett-Fitzsimmons fight for is
fixed for 17th March.
* • * •
The Australian Fair sports, held on Sydney
Agricultural Ground last Saturday, represented the latest
attempt of Sydney’s R C. residents to raise funds for the
building of St. Mary’s Cathedral. All the Bhoys were
there —including the Cardinal, Bishop Higgins—and that
Scotch haythen, Quong Tart. Everybody called everyone
else Dhanny or Micky, and altogether the affair was a
birthday for Ould Oireland.
The sports, as a whole, were flat and wearisome.
Exceptions were Roseingrave’s brilliant hurdle-racing and
the finish of the ten-mile League Cycling test-race. Here
M‘Coombe beat Meadham by a most gamely-sustained
spurt home. M'Coombe, therefore, will represent the
N.S.W. League in Adelaide, and a pluckier or more
honest performer couldn’t be picked.
The event of the day was the tug-of-war—lre-
land v. English men-of-warsmen. “Ireland” consisted
of massive men heaven-born policemen. Compared
with the English they were a team of selected draught
stallions ; the English were a scratch lot with two or
three ponies. The fun began the moment the referee
called “Go!” The contestants lay back, grafted like
devils, and screwed their faces into curiosities of ugliness.
The strain on the rope was terrific, but it was nothing to
the strain on the supporters of the Ould Dart. Some of
these flew up and down and around their team ; the
policemen’s feet tore the grass away in strips and threw
up clouds of dust. The yells were ear-splitting: “Howld
iviry blankv inch uv it! Bury thim blanky feet!
Maloney, dig in thim blanky heels ! Maloney, tak off yer
boots and spit on yer feet! Shut yer blanky rat-trap !
Howld on to it, Dinny ! To hell wid ye ! Tak that point
for protest! Get a blank.v foot-holt! I can’t shtand it! ”
Encouraged by these yells, Ireland pulled enough to pull
the ould sod out of purgatory, and the poor, miserable,
busted English collapsed from sheer exhaustion.
♦ * * *
If Newhaven goes ’Ome, which is now an un-
likely thing to happen, Walter Hickenbotham will not
accompany the horse.
Some of the tales re non-payment of jockey-fees
are almost pathetic. The other day, in a train returning
to Melb. from a suburban mee'ing, a wan and weary light-
weight was heard to ask a pigmy companion whether he
intended to have tea in town. “ I can’t slake," said the
youngster ruefully, whereupon the first boy remarked :
“ And yet you had three rides ! ” Here was a case of a
jockey in a seemingly prosperous way of business who
had had three mounts during the afternoon, not being
able to put down a “ bob ” for his tea in town.
» * ♦ *
Melbourne fraternity of Ikey Mo is just now
employed in cutting up a Northern squattah, who seems
to be such a guileless and meaty bird that Ikey talks of
offering up thanks in the Synagogue to the Giver of all
Good Things.
» * * *
Totalisator divs. at the Hobart meeting proved
to be poor as a rule. The machine was run “ blind,” but
the owners, who expected to benefit by the public’s
ignorance as to how the big money was being invested,
will probably prefer to have the blind up for the future.
When punters punt in the dark the money is not likely to
be well-distributed. Not being able to see an inducement
to back one or other of the unfancied ones for the sake of
long odds, they put their money on the popular fancy,
with the hope that it may not be carrying an excessive
burden of tickets. If a very rank-outsider gets home the
two or three lucky investors are pretty certain to be
friends or agents of the tote management, who get private
information concerning the state of the betting ballot
just before the tote closes. Under the “blind” system
the men who work the machines ought to die rich.
They are the only people on the course who possess the
knowledge which should be common property, and which
is common property elsewhere than at tricky little Tassy.
* * * *
Editor Bulletin, — Sir : Let me express my sur-
prise and indignation at the contents of a paragraph
which recently appeared in your sporting columns. I
consider that your contributor’s remarks on the un-
sportsmanlike acts of the members of the N.S.W.
Gun Club, and the insinuations as to their living on the
game are unwarrantable in any paper ; and, in justice to
those members, I cannot allow them to remain unnoticed.
It is generally thought in sporting circles (and' some of
my friends are not slow in affirming) that the paragraph
alluded to was either written by me or inserted at my
instigation. It is for the purpose of contradicting these
reports and removing this erroneous impression, and last,
but by no means least of all, of removing the slur that has
been unwarrantably cast upon the members of the N.S.W.
Gun Club, that I ask you to publish this letter. —Yours,
A. W. Eales. Sydney, January 15th, 1897.
[The paragraph of which Mr. Eales complains
was not written or instigated by him ; the only state-
ment in it regarding Mr. Eales is that he is a mere
amateur who does not live cm the game. The paragraph
is fact ; it makes’no alluSbn whatever to the members of
the N.S.W. Gun Club as a body ; the writer was well aware
that the members in general are too bad shots to live on
the game. Every sport has its few foxes and many
geese. A full, true and particular history of the unhappy
geese who have been plucked on the Sydney Gun Club
grounds would make sensational reading.— Ed. B.]
* • * *
Rumor hath it that Mr. Cooper wishes to sell
Newhaven. This is not at all unlikely, as besides the
risks connected with the horse’s transport to England,
he would be hard to trade off when there owing to the
doubts about his pedigree.
* * * *
The Sydney punter still patronises the five-bob
“ tote ; ” on Sat. last over Warwick Farm races no less
than £855 was invested with three of the peripatetic
“ machines.”
* * » *
Carbine’s first foal in England belongs to Mr.
Henry Chaplin, of Hermit renown. The youngster is from
an unraced and unnamed mare by Galopin—Dee.
* * * *
N.S.W. will be well represented at Melb. during
the Autumn campaign. San Marco, Reka, Coil, and True
Blue are four that should do well in the Newmarket Han-
dicap, whilst Woodford and Coil are well treated in Aus-
tralian Cup.
* * * *
In the Oakleigh Plate, to-day, Metford (Carbine
—Marchesa) should prevail. He will probably have most
to fear from his compatriots, Akarini and San Marco. In
the Oakleigh Purse, Danube should make a big show.
* * * *
That rogue, Duhallow, who started a warm
favorite for Caulfield Cup, has been relegated to hurdle-
racing, and won his first race over sticks at Melbourne on
Saturday. This new departure may give him courage;
if so, he will yet show his undoubted pace on the flat.
• * * *
Pharamond found the distance too far for him
in Hobart Cup; he, however, afterwards won the Lord
Stakes.
* * « *
With the bestof Australian jockeys eitner having
left the country or “ standing down,” the butcher-boy
type is having a high old time. ’Tis a pitiable sight to
see these manikins lamm their wretched mounts at the
finish of a race—chance or no chance ; but what can be
expected when the leading clubs in Australia command
that all horses must be ridden out in a race ?
• * * *
When the cricket-match between members of
N.S.W. and Vic. Parliaments was played in Melbourne
recently, a spectator said to a prominent cricketer who
was also watching the game : “ Don’t you think any
average cricketer would make as good a member of Par-
liament as any of those fellows?” “My oath!” re-
marked the P.C. “ Well, how is it that they play such
poor cricket?” enquired the other. “That’s a different
thing,” replied the P.O, gravel}'; “ to play cricket pro-
perly a man must have brains, and judgment, and train-
ing.”
• * * *
Only a few years ago, a certain clever crowd, at
Caulfield, took £20,000 out of the ring over a Newmarket,
and yet their horse started at 40 to 1. Now, if a horse
were backed to win as many pence, he would be imme-
diately installed a hot first favorite ; such is the state of
the much-vaunted ’Stralian betting-ring.
* * * *
It is rather a forlorn hope to “ ring in ” a trotter
in any of the Australias now, but there is one horse
running about Sydney that the “ talent ” cannot place.
His educated style of going shows him to be no beginner
at the game.
• * * •
The greatest trotting take-down Australia ever
saw was undoubtedly old Commodore. No Yankee cari-
cature could outdo the horse in his latter days. A bag of
bones, bowed at the knees, teeth inches long, and with a
chronic hacking cough, old Com. was rung-iri at Brisbane
some years ago as Jack, and driven onto the course in an
old, broken-down sulky, with string-tied harness. “ Here,
old man,” said one of the gang in the swim, “I’ll lend you
a sulky for the race,” and another offered a set of harness.
But the good thing did not meet with the success it
deserved. George Donaldson happened to be in Brisbane
with Garryowen at the time, and, at once recognising
Commodore, gave the show away, and the old horse and
the clever crowd were warned off for good. They got the
totalisator money, but the stakes went to second horse.
What indomitable pluck the horse had ! He would shuffle
along until the reins were gathered up and a hold taken,
when age and infirmity were forgotten, and he would “do
or diz.”
• • • *
J. H. Hellings easily beat the newly-risen star,
Lane, for the N.S.W. 100yds. swimming championship,
time, lmin. 6Jsec. The race was won by 2yds. Bishop
second, Lane third. Seven started.

Laws form one of the most complicated branches
of legal science.” This is true, and it is practi-
cally impossible for any one unacquainted with
their technicalities to draw specifications and
claims, &e., that will stand the test of a law-suit.
Hitherto expert Patent agents have made charges
simply prohibitive to most inventors, but Mr.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., of 39 Castlereagh-
street (next Hotel Australia), Sydney, and Bolton-
street, Newcastle, having established reciprocal
arrangement with leading Patent agents in all the
principal countries in the world is now prepared
to do the work accurately and effectively at rates
that will place a patent within reach of the poorest
inventor. For the sum of £2O (exclusive of cost of
drawings) he will obtain a certificate of Provisional
Protection (in the first instance) for any invention
in the seven Australasian colonies and Great
Britain, and full Patents all over the world at
similarly reasonable rates. Mr. Griffith also
makes a specialty of designing and registering
Trade Marks-and Copyrights.*

Under the heading of “ Facts,” Pearson s
Weekly—a paper which has allegedly spent Is.
a word upon a special contribution prints
In Victoria, N.S.W., women have now been substituted
for men at no fewer than 200 railway stations. The result
has been a saving' of ±130,000 per year in salaries. Ihe
average wage paid to a station-mistress is ±2O per year,
whereas men used to receive £l5O.

INVALIDS
AND
the aced.
At Hobart. Johnny Bounder, just ar-
rived, to sullen-looking native holding up a tele-
graph-post : “ Aw —I say—you dawn’t knaw
where Dr. Bones lives ? ” The native wasn’t
having any. “Don’t I?” he said carelessly.
“But—aw —do you know?” continued the dis-
gusted “Nicest.” “That’s my business,” said
the native. “But I want to knaw—yer knaw.”
“ Well, that’s your business.” Then the eye-
glassed one stood on his outraged dignity: “You
are vewy wude. I will—aw—have to awsk some
one else.” “ All right ; that’s some one else’s
business.”
The alteration in the running of the Bris-
bane mail, whereby the train reaches Sydney at
11 a.m. instead of 11 p.m., is doubtless a conveni-
ence to Northern travellers, but Sydney people
affected by the change do not like it. To begin
with, two of the city postal deliveries have been
altered, the 11 a.m. and 1.30 p.m. being the cause
of much cursing amongst postmen, as they have
no opportunity to get lunch until close on 3 p.m.
Also, instead of finishing on Saturday after their
11.30 run as formerly they have to deliver the
Brisbane mail at 1.30, and that at a time when
almost all the business houses are closed, conse-
quently the letters are nearly all brought back to
the office. Clerks and others in city houses have
also to hustle, as letters delivered at 1.30 have to
be answered almost immediately to catch the re-
turning mail. Goods likewise have to be des-
patched at high pressure and short notice.
Writes “6x8”: I knew her 20 minutes
when she confidentially asked the loan of my ear.
She was a native, and “my man” an Irish ex-
policeman who kept the Bullock-Team Inn. ‘ ‘ He’s
an awful beast,” she whispered, “ when he’s drink-
ing. He burst out before Xmas and you’d hardly
believe how hard I tried, for the sake of the chil-
dren, to get him off it before our holiday supply of
grog arrived. First I put three bottles of brandy
and four sticks of tobacco into the beer-cask.
That didn’t stop him. He drank it all and started
on the spirits. So I puts all the schnapps and
bitters and a handful of mustard and cayenne-
pepper into the keg of overproof rum ; while Maud
put a mixture of vinegar, turpentine, and baking-
soda into the gin-jar. We thought that ’d pull
him up. But it had no effect—he drank ev’ry
drop ! There was only whisky left then, so we
puts three packets of Epsom salts and a strong
dose of jalap into each bottle. That made him
feel a bit queer, but he drank it all the same and
went across the road to the other pub. But he
soon came back. Their grog beat him. He’s
sober now. God knows for how long. It’s awful
the way he robs me and the children.”

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common?” Ihe
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. r l here is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:—"The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies."

mission of Herr Rassmussen, and praise bestowed upon

mation in which Herr Rassmussen is held by the Austra-
lian Press: —

prove.”

fying to important cures effected by his remedies.”

wonderful Herbal Alfaline Remedies have done more good

factures some excellent Blood and Nerve Remedies.”

mussen is a clever botanist.”

Press notices agree as to the skill he possesses as a
botanist, and the bonafldes of his testimonials appear to
be well established.”

sen, we are glad to say, holds the highest place in the
colonies as a botanist, and enjoys the confidence of many

The above selected Press opinions are quite sufficient
to show the esteem Herr Rassmussen is held in by those
who have the best opportunity to watch his career.

Item from the report of Wells, leader of a
party which went out in search of the two lost
members of the Calvert expedition :
VVe travelled two days S.S.W. for 22 miles. We had a
native secured on a chain, and, although very thirsty, he
could show us no more water. He evidently did not know
the country further. Having-; only two gallons of water,
we attempted, on the following day, to return to the last
well, travelling during the night.
There are various ways of finding water in dry
Australia, and a nigger on a chain is probably the
best of them. The chain naturally becomes hot,
and if the nigger is made to run ahead in the sun
and dust, and isn’t allowed anything to drink, it
is a fair assumption that he will show the way to
water, if he knows of any. If he has been running,
say, two days at the end of the hot chain with his
tongue hanging out, and yet doesn’t point out any
water, it js a fair assumption that he doesn’t know
of any. Then the best thing is to drop him and
get another nigger. Sometimes, of course, the
aboriginal is obstinate, and holds out a long time,
but if salt is blown into him through a tube and
he is kept running on the chain, even the worst
nigger generally caves in and tells everything he
knows.
Dear Bulletin, —Just here (Narre warren,
Vic.) we are in the thick of a grasshopper plague.
On an inside calculation, they run 2cwt. to the
acre — i.e., 64 tons of solid grasshopper to the
sq. mile ! Everyone of them is a cast-iron,
armour-plated warrior with a back-kick like a
gun-spring. They start by clearing off all the
succulent vegetation, and then get to work on the
stalks and stubble. They come into the house,
too —get mashed to a paste in the door-jambs,
climb on to the ceiling, and jump into the soup.
Boiled grasshopper in the cabbage at dinner is a
common circumstance. A sft. jump is a fair
average; but the point-to-point steeplechase is
not their only method of locomotion. The local
grasshopper is also a solid grafter on the heel-and-
toe principle, and in his explorations shows small
respect for the proprieties. It is a frequent
occurrence for one of the women, hitherto placidly
reading, to let out a shriek that splits the
roof, and. make a streak for her private
boudoir with a pallid expression on her face and a
death-clutch on some portion of her apparel. Un-
fortunately, these pests have no natural enemies
worth considering. Our speckled Hamburg
rooster, an old bird with a damaged comb and
three tail-feathers, is the only one to tackle them
so far. He is always getting bunked from the
harem by the other gentlemen poultry when just
on the verge of matrimony, and he finds solace
for his disappointment in the hunting of the
grasshopper. His system is to lower his head
down alongside his prey and then make a sudden
peck sideways. Nines time in ten he strikes the
object, and down it goes to bump itself against the
pebbles and beer-bottle chips inside the old bird’s
gizzard. Watching this procedure is the only re-
lief for a mind jaded with too much grasshopper.
Had Pharaoh’s locusts been of the Vic. variety,
one can imagine that potentate requesting Moses
“ to remove these insects, and resume operations
on the first born.”—W.B.

MELBOURNE
Business, Bobbery, etc.
Melbourne business men are wondering how the
recent death of a certain wealthy lady will affect a large
mercantile Arm and the bank which is supporting it on
the lady’s guarantee. , , ,
Australian business is slowly but surely follow-
ing the American lead, and is run by rings. Melbourne
has two timber-rings (boxes and Kauri pine), a candle
ring, a hardware ditto, a huge wool ring, two coal, a
paper, a fish, and a wool ring.
There may be “nothing in it,” but Melbourne
is being almost flooded with brand-new Jubilee half-
crowns, florins, and shillings.
* * * •
Until a few months ago, stamp vendors licensed
by N.S.W. Govt, got a commission of 2J percent, on sales.
P.M.G. Cook thought they were doing too much business
—they kept long hours, and went out of their way to be
obliging—so he reduced the commission to 11 per cent.
Even at this reduction some vendors persisted in selling
too many stamps, and making themselves too convenient
to the public, to the prejudice of regular G.P.O. sales, so
P.M.G. Joe Cook has cancelled some of the most useful
vendors’ licenses altogether. Somebody used to sell
stamps on Circular Quay, and somebody else in Lower Pitt-
st., and the people nearest to them naturally patronised
them instead of going up to the Exchange P.O. or round
to George-st. P.M.G. Joe Cook has shut off those vendors’
supplies, and the public have to tramp up to the Exchange
P.O. or round to George-st. whether they like it or no.
What does the State P.O. exist for—for the public con-
venience, or—as Herbert Spencer would have us believe —
for the benefit of a woodenheaded official despotism?
The latter, says P.M.G. Joe Cook.
* * * *
Again the Bank of Barataria. Years ago they
tell now that gorgeous institution bought up a little
church adjoining its principal premises at a cost of
£17,000, and turned the grounds into a stable for the
gilded manager. Also, that little church received in
addition a handsome overdraft, and proceeded to build a
stately temple overlooking the city. But this was a very
long time ago, and—of course—in Barataria.
* • * *
A Britisher just out from ’Ome has piled up
£70,000 backing Charters Towers mines. Good enough.
« * * *
A little band of detective-auditors has arrived
in Westralia from London, with the intention of making
one or two big boomsters sit up.
• • » •
British shindykit, who failed to pot the Lon-
doners with the Maritana (W.A.), dropped £20,000 over
their option. Kingsborough, of Adelaide, made an abso-
lute sale to’ the party, but they dallied, and the boom
petered. Wherefore the Maritana has £3OOO in hand to
further toss down its shaft.
• * • «
It is singular that three out of every four minor
mining-experts in W.A. have been either Wesleyan
ministers or local preachers, who have given up their con-
tract for bruising the head of the serpent to pronounce
authoritative opinions upon a subject of which they are
equally ignorant. It was one of these whom Pluto so
peremptorily discharged from Hades a few months ago,
because no confidence whatever could be placed in him.
* * » *
Shocking example of Bendigo “liberality.”
The prosperous Koch’s Pioneer, dividing £10,625 on the
half-year, pays its mine-manager a princely £3 a week !
Best Australian mean-mining feat? And yet Bendigo
wonders why its managers go West. Anyway, million-
aires Lansell and Atkinson deserve the bad tomato tor
their breek-skinning management.
* » * *
Melb. Theatre Royal has felt the cold ever since
Coppin’s ill-fated co. (Amory Sullivan, manager) took a
hand in the house’s fortunes. From a thriving concern
with £lO,OOO reserve, the co. has melted to a heavy over-
draft carrier.
• * * *
The careful, cautious, “ eaten-up-with-pessi-
mism ” mining men are those in charge of the biggest
mines in W.A. ; to wit, Dick Hamilton, of the Great
Boulder, whose name has not been connected with any
cat; Callahan, of Lake View, who knocked the bottom
out of many a duffer in the country ; Hewitson, selected
for the Ivanhoe because of his reliability. As soon as
some really good mines were found the men whose hands
remained clean were put in command of them.
* » • *
“Mary S.” writes to The Bulletin for a few
particulars concerning any inexpensive stook (under six-
pence, if possible) in which she could buy some fully
paid-up shares. She wants the stock at a “sprat.” per
share, to “ take a rise soon,” because she doesn’t wish to
hold. Also, the lady is anxious for “an idea of what
the rise” might be, as a guide for selling, and she will be
greatly obliged by an early reply to her enquiries in this
journal. Can somebody oblige Mary with a parcel of
fully paid-up sixpennies which are addicted to early
rising ?
* • ■ *
N.S. Wales has this harvest for the first
time in history a bigger aggregate wheat-yield than Vic-
toria. Likewise during the last fortnight of 1896 N.S.W.
cheese brought Id. per lb. higher in Sydney than Vic.
cheese in Melbourne. Extension of scientific cultivation
in the Riverina accounts for both facts.
* • * •
The Australian Mail, which barracks for the
W.A. boom, regards the acceptance of a £lOOO a year
billet by Daly, the Argus special, as a counterbalance to
the vituperation coming from Westralia’s eastern neigh-
bors. It also refers to the amount of money sent over
each month by miners to their families. Like the miners,
Daly went out because he could make more money there,
and others will continue to go as long as Bull is willing to
spend hundreds of thousands of pounds in working mines
without gold.
* * * *
The price of copper seems to have established
itself at about £5O per ton. The tendency is declared by
the copper authorities of the world to be upwards, but if
it even remains at £5O it means a large increase in the
Australian production.
• • • •
The accountant of a big Australian firm had his
books overhauled lately, with the result that a four-figure
deficiency was discovered. He was called before the
bosses and asked what he intended to do. “Oh, noth-
ing,” airily replied the embezzler, “it is for you to do some-
thing—prosecute, I think, is the best thing.” “ Very well,
we will,” said the bosses. “ And, of course, if you do,”
added the embezzler, “ I will take care to disclose those
little ways of doing business, such as and ; you
know exactly what I mean.” The bosses decided not to
blast the young man’s life by sending him to gaol, and he
has gone away on a long holiday—at the firm’s expense.
* * « *
Advt. for a mining-boomster : “ Experience in
working mines unnecessary, but must be well up in work-
ing newspapers.”
The re-issue, after three years’ suspension, of
real-property registers, etc., is one of the signs of the
incubation of a new land-boom in Brisbane (Q.).
Gordelpit!
“Pa” Wilson, of Melb. Bijou fame, holds
nearly an eighth of the 48,000 scrip in Ballarat’s div.-
paying South Star.
The South Star is adopting bromo-cyanide for
treating tailings. Tests show a recovery of 91 per cent,
at a cost of 2s. a ton, and the loss in the tailings is 3dwt.
4gr. Plant to treat 4000 tons a month is going up ; an-
other 3dwt. to the ton is expected to be added to the
yield, and much satisfaction is expressed. Not a word is
said in explanation of defective milling. To lose over
3dwt. in Joz. Ballarat quartz is surely something to be
ashamed of.
The Spirit Vendetta .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Your sister Alaiavahas come to the next
village, and she lies there sick,” they said to Siati.
“ Kalofal” he answered; “may she recover
soon.”
“ She longs with a sick longing for soaas and
sucking-pig.”
“ Kalofa ! Soa’as have I none, and my pigs are
old for sucklings; moreover, flesh is bad for the
sick. May she eat vaisalo and cooked esi and
recover.”
And he went not to see her.
“ It is bad,” said the others. “ Brothers should
go to their sisters when illness comes.”
And Alaiava recovered not, but died; and they
made her a fine funeral with baked meats.
To the funeral came Siati, bringing a pig, and
empty papalangi beer-bottles to border the grave,
and a papalangi bowl to place on it; and round
the grave they planted the ti-ula, the red-leaved
dracaena with white flower spikes.
But they said: “ It is not well for brothers to
bring bottles and bowls after death, when they
deny food in life.” .
And the swallows swooped low around Alaiava s
grave, and she rested not in it.
* * * *
After that there was much commotion
among the spirits of the dead in the village of
Valevao, in the Island of Savaii, where was
Alaiava’s home in her life-time ; and the spirits
held a fono round the mausoleum of the greatest
traditionary chief in the village. Then all those
belonging to Alaiava’s family drew apart and
travelled away together.
That night a wise woman of Siati’s village, who
had been holding converse with the owls and the
butterflies, spoke to the villagers and said : “ There
are too many spirits around our village to-night.
The anganga (soul) of Alaiava has brought them.
Evil will come of it.”
And the next day Siati sickened, and three days
after he was dead.
They made a great funeral for him, and forbade
the Siva and the song all through the district, and
kept the high-road and the waterway sacred from
the passing of strangers; for he was a chief and
deserved such honors. But they sighed, with
anxious faces, for they said:
“Behold, it is not well for a brother to neglect
his sick sister ; evil must come of it, and who
knows where the evil shall end ? ”
And the face of Siati’s next brother waxed pale,
and his bones ached with cold, and he trembled
with fear.
Then the wise woman, who was also the village
doctor, spake to him, saying :
“ Send to the wise man at Valevao to know the
truth of this matter, lest thou also perish ; for my
medicines cannot help thee.”
And the brother went himself to consult the
wise man.
“ It is even so,” said the wise man, when he had
thought deeply ; “ I have known it for some days.
The anganga of Alaiava was wrath with Siati for
that he had not visited her when she was sick.
And her soul came to the tombs of her ancestors
and told the spirits of her uncles and aunts and
Grandparents, and they also were wroth with
iati. So they all went to pay him a visit, and
of that visit he died.”
“ And is it ended ? ” asked the trembling
brother. . . .
“ Nay, not so ; for even now the spirit of Siati
is angered and he would punish one of his sister’s
family in Valevao, which is not the right way to
makepeace. For the wrath of Alaiava s spirits'
continues therefore, and they seek another victim
Thou art the next in line.”
“ And what shall be done to make peace ?
“ Bring mats,” said the wise man.
• • * •
So the brother went home and told his
village. Whereat they held a fono and decided to
spare all the mats they could, for fear of further
mischief. They brought together mats in abund-
ance, each household one or more, and those
households that had no mats to offer brought rolls
of siapo. The mats and the siapo were all placed
on the large village-boat and the chiefs and their
. sons started off in it, each man taking an oar. But
there was but one flag flying and that a black one,
and there was neither drum nor bugle heard ; and
the chiefs wore no ti girdles, and no wreaths save
a white strip of pandanus fibre ; and as they
rowed they neither chanted nor laughed to the
stroke of their oars.
Thus they arrived at Valevao in Savaii ; but
when the people there saw the mats that they had
brought they welcomed them gladly, and killed
pigs, and lighted the ovens, and made tafolo, and
there was much feasting and rejoicing.
Thus were the spirits of the uncles and aunts
and grandparents Of Alaiava appeased, and the
anganga of Alaiava came back to its tomb and
rested there ; and the spirit of Siati forgot to be
angry, for it saw that it was in the wrong. So
the brethren of Siati came home and worked
in their taro-fields and planted their yams and cut
down their nuts for copra in peace.
And the swallows flew high, and nobody
sickened in the village, for the mats had brought
pardon.
* * « •
And this happened in the Islands of Savaii
in this present year 1896, within a month of the
writing thereof. And if any doubt —there lie the
mats.
ROSE DE BOHEME.
Savaii, Navigator Islands.
H. Mitchell, pilot steamer, Brisbane,
writes :
Bulletin of 9th Jan. says George Clarke was the dis-
coverer of Mambare goldfield. This is incorrect, for
Clarke never completed the distance you have to travel
by boat before reaching the field : he was killed in his
boat whilst ascending one of the five or six falls you have
to ascend before arriving at the head of the river. I was
in charge of the steam launch which towed Gov.
Macgregor up thp Mambare —the first time a white man
was up. We went up as far as possible with the launch,
and then I took the whaleboat under Capt. Hennessey’s
supervision, and Gov. Macgregor his gig, and we went
right up as far as a boat would float, and then went pros-
pecting. It was our reports that brought G. Clarke and
party there afterwards, so he would doubtfully have been
entitled to the credit of discovery even if he had reached
the head of the river. As far as actual prospecting goes,
his party deserve the credit, because after Clarke was
killed they went further even than we did. I cannot re-
member all their names, but Alick Clunes took the
leadership, and Simpson, M'Laughlin, and Alick Elliott
(all of Sudest) and two or three of Clarke’s party from
Cairns, were the first lot that went inland.


The Maoriland law deals firmly with the
grasping registry-office keeper. One M‘Leod, for
over-charging 2s. 6d. in procuring a situation for a
client, was fined at Auckland £2, and in addition
was mulct in costs to the same amount. A Govt,
scale of charges for all registry-office keepers is
badly wanted in Australia.

boys, and youths’ clothing, underclothing, water-
proof clothing, mercery, hats. Write to us for
one; it is bound to come in useful, especially if
you live in the country and unable to personally
select your requirements from us. Hobdern
Bros., 203, 205, 207, 209, 211 Pitt-street (only),
Sydney. *

This world is full of suffering and unhappiness. In every walk
of life may be seen young girls, pale, sallow, and nerveless, the
victims of troubles peculiar to their sex, from which they suffer
in silence, and with the mistaken notion that there is no help for
them. Their lives are made miserable by headaches, dizziness,
palpitation of the heart, shortness of breath on the slightest ex-
ertion ; an indisposition for either work or pleasure, and frequently
a feeling that life is but a dreary burden scarcely worth preserving.
To all such we say take hope ; there is a cure for your trouble, and
it is within your power to be bright-eyed, rosy-cheeked, and happy.
Dr. Williams’ PINK PILLS for PALE PEOPLE will restore
your lost color, correct irregularities, and bring back health and
happiness. In proof of this assertion, read the following testi-
mony from those who have suffered and found renewed health
through this marvellous medicine.
Miss Catherine Elood, daughter of Patrick Flood, Esq., who
lives a few miles from Mallorytown, Ont., says :—“A little more
than a year ago I began to decline in health. I felt constantly
tired, my appetite failed, and my color was deathly. My father
saw a doctor and described my case, and he said the trouble was
anaemia, and sent me a bottle of medicine. I found that the medi-
cine did not agree with me and discontinued it. I was constantly
growing worse, and was subject to weak spells, and was fast
becoming little more than a living skeleton. One Sunday after I
had been to church a friend who returned with me strongly urged
me to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. I decided to give them a
trial, and my father purchased a supply. The first change I
noticed was that the dizziness was leaving me, then my appetite
improved and my color began to return, and soon I was enjoying
as good health as I had ever done. It is now about a year since I
discontinued the use of the pills, and I have not felt one symptom
of the old trouble. I believe that Pink Pills saved me from the
grave, and I strongly urge other girls who may be in a condition
similar to mine to try them. ”
Mr. William Stoughton is a well-known and much esteemed
farmer living in the vicinity of Barryvale, Renfrew, Ont. He is
one of many who have reason to bless the day that Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills were brought into his home, for they saved the life of
his daughter. Mr. Stoughton says“ In 1893, my daughter

In the Half-Light .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Still in the half-light halting we have heard one
more patrol,
And the sound of a challenge fallen out of the
waking West ;
There is a voice in the mountains, and from sentinel
soul to soul
A whisper of day spring passes, and the murmur
of pent unrest.
Linger not, twilight of tutelage! for we fain would
see her throned
In her morrow of majesty peerless—Australia,
our gracious Queen !
From her toil in the leaf-lands glowing, with the
might of her youth enzoned,
Breathing a scent of hillsides mown ; in her eyes
dominion serene.
Throned with the sovereign sisters! what if no lurid
plain,
No homeward roll of the victor wheels set her
defiant there ?
Are there no primal foes laid low under the
ripened grain—
No conquered demons of Loneliness, and
Desert, and fierce Despair ?
In the stern hill-holds, in the silent lands by the
channels of mocking thirst,
They sleep, the leaders who fought and fell, who
labored and dumbly died ;
There are who may win our battles yet, but these
shall not be first,
While the west winds tell of the unknown great
whose graves the blown sands hide.
By the flicker of bivouac fires there is a space for
thought—
Not that we huddle round dying lights or vainly
the issue doubt ;
But nation has sprung from nation, and empire
from empire wrought
Has writ upon Time its story, and the moral
glares starkly out.
Will the mock-stars be ours, my brothers ? Shall
we take for our eidolon theirs ?
Empurpled with power, but forgetting the rags
of the stifled poor ;
Our peace but a standing-at-ease for ever in hollow
squares,
While the flesh of toil is galled with steel of
panoplied war ?
Nay, not as theirs be our Statehood, girt with a
Nessus-robe,
Division of soul and heartfelt wrong under all
armour proud ;
In vain for this had our vestal fires ringed the
dark under-globe;
In vain for this were the wild hills tamed and
the leagues of desert ploughed.
Away with the dragon banners and the motto
dead words unseal!
Have we not living hearts to spell into deeds of
our own dear fame ?
One deathless meaning to make of Nation and
Commonweal,
One path as wide as the waves that eastward
dance with dawn aflame ?
Is there a hue of strife in the dawn-sky or battle
rude?
So be it; shield-borne we shall raise her, front-
ing the alien hordes:
Australia will know no flinching, last born of a
lion brood,
She will call to her sons and they shall fringe
the shore with a surf of swords.
From Tasman’s Isle unto Torres, from Moreton
Bay to the Swan,
From the still-hushed heart of the inmost west
to the sea-vexed river-bars,
From the sands that are still nnconquered to the
plains that our fathers won,
They will fall into square and column under the
white Cross-stars !
Under our own starred blue ; for the old blood-
deepened red
With its emblem of sordid triumph would vex
the untainted skies :
Afar is it sought of the vultures thrice-gorged
with the Nations dead,
And the ravens haunt its shadow with their
evil-boding cries.
To the better Britain that yet may be—brother-
hood long and dear !
While we too share of the glory that shame
cannot all unsay ;
But here, in our capitol stately, knowing nor
pause nor fear,
We shall crown our sovereign Future, and the past
shall not bar the way.
Dec. 26, 1896.
C. KENDALL.
The death of diver Madsen from spinal
compression when down a few fathoms in Sydney
Harbor raises the question of regular medical in-
spection of all divers. Manv persons—instance
sufferers from heart trouble—are totally unfit to
brave the deep, and should be prohibited from
doing so.
Innot Springs, on Nettle Creek, some 25
or 30 miles from Herberton (N.Q.), which have
been famous locally for years, are coming into
favor with whites suffering from rheumatism,
sciatica, &c. The springs are used both for
bathing purposes and internally as mineral waters.
They vary in temperature from 165 to 185 deg.
and a few yards from the hottest is to be found
ice-cold water. A co. is being formed in N.Q. to
acquire and conserve the springs.
“Half the world doesn’t know how the
other half lives.” Does half the world know how
the other half dies ? Printed in small type and
hidden in obscure corners, one meets every day in
the N.S.W. press these pitiful pars (actual clip-
pings on dates given) :
An old swagmin named Peterson was found dead by his
mate on the road two miles from Yass, N.S.W.—3O/12/’96.
A verdict of heat-apoplexy was returned in the ease of
a bushman who was found dead in the scrub near Trundle
N.S.W.— 31/12/’96. ’
An elderly swagman, a stranger in the district, was
seen to stagger and fall last Sunday near Dapto, N.S W
He was carried under the shade of a verandah, where life
was found to be extinct.—l/l/’97.
The body of an unknown swagman was found in a
shearer’s hut at Combaning station, near Temora, N.S.W.
A match-box found on him contained a threepenny-bit
and a Union ticket issued to James Connelly by Wasr-a
Branch A.W.U.— 2/1/97. J i ®
An inquest was held on the body of a swagman found
near Lagawarra, N.S.W. No clue to identity.—3 l/’97.
The body of a man named Dunlop was found near a
gate five miles from Pooncarie, Darling River, NSW
He was a victim of thirst.—4/l/’97.
The body of an unknown swagman was found on Glen-
lyon station, near Broken Hill. A Union ticket issued to
Arthur Hasley found on him was the only clue to his
identity.—s/l/’97.
And that’s how the swagmen die !
Mich Mulleeran’s Humility.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Michael Mulleeran was neither a squatter
nor a cocky; but something between the two.
“ We kem out wid money an’ we’ve made money,”
said Mick. “Leastways the wife has,” he always
carefully added.
Mrs. Frances Mulleeran was a lady and beauti-
ful. Patrician in every movement, and as gentle
as Mick was rough. She handed her cup to her
husband with infinite grace, and smiled with sweet
condescension as Mick grabbed the crockery and
rushed to the sideboard with it. “For ’tis meself
knows how much crarne me darlint loikes,” he
averred.
“ The devil a finger he’d lave her sile, at all, at
all,” declared the gossips of Irishtown.
“ It isn’t for wurruk I married ye, Frances,
darlint,” said Mick.
» * * «
Donations in money or in kind to the
church were always made in the name of Mrs.
Frances Mulleeran, and Mick glorified her name
on every possible occasion.
One Candlemas Day Father M’Cooey read from
the altar a list of contributors: “ John Daniel
O’Ryan, 41b. _ of wax candles ; Mistress Regan,
widow, 21b. imported sperm candles; Jeremiah
Regan, son of above, lib. of sperm candles ; Katie
Regan, daughter of above 21b. best tallow candles
for general use ; Mistress Frances Mulleeran, 31b.
of wax candles ; Mistress Mac ”
“Hould an, yer Riverance,” interrupted Mick
in a loud voice, “Misthress Frances Mulleeran,
foive pound o’ wax candles, iv ye plaze. ”
Upon which correction the priest amended the
item, the congregation looked approval, and Mick
sat up with a self-conscious air of having dared to
do his duty.
The family pew was rented in the name of Mrs.
Frances Mulleeran ; the receipt for the plot of
ground in the cemetery bore the same name, as
also did the title-deeds of the homestead. And
all the stock were branded F.M.
* * * *
The children, two girls, as rough in nature
and appearance as their burly father, regarded
their mother with a mixture of reverence and con-
tempt. At times she shyly strove to teach them
some of her gentle arts, but Mick would brusquely
interfere, with—“ Don’t be throublin’ yerself wid
such ignorant haythens, Frances, darlint ! Lave
’em to Father M'Cooey and the school-inasther.”
So the girls continued to roam the paddocks.
They fought with the school-master and the neigh-
bours’ boys whenever opportunity offered, and
dodged their father’s stockwhip as best they could.
They were big, untidy girls, who jarred on Mrs.
Frances Mulleeran’s susceptibilities ; but it was no
use protesting. Mick always insisted that their
habits were “ good enough for the loikes av thim;”
and forbade reform.
* * * *
Occasionally Mick would paint the town-
ship in varied tints. While the drinking fit was
on him any neighbors who might endeavor to coax
him towards home generally had to fight a drunken
madman or run. So they got into the way of
sending word to Mrs. Frances Mulleeran that
Mick was “ oncontrollable down beyant at the
pub. ”
The gentlewoman would then be driven down to
the township, and would politely ask the publican
if her Mulleeran was within. Sometimes he was
within, sometimes he was without—in the back-
yard, fighting; but, within or without, directly
he heard his wife’s voice he climbed unsteadily, yet
silently, into the buggy, and drove off home. On
one or two occasions Mick’s fellow J’sP. at Irish-
town were regretfully compelled to fine him a
nominal sum for drunkenness and resisting
the police. The fine was paid by a cheque
drawn by Mrs. Frances Mulleeran, and Mick
interviewed the editor of the local paper
with a threat of summary vengeance of a personal
nature and a stoppage of subscription if the words
“by Mrs. Frances Mulleeran ” were not attached
to the usual notification that “ the fine was paid.’'
The words duly appeared.
“ ’Tis herself is the grand manager,” was Mick’s
constant comment on his wife. But the only
managing the lady did was to manage Mick him-
self when drunk. Sober, he was a despot—in
nothing so despotic as in his worship of his angelic
wife.
Where Mick found such a wife nobody could
imagine. “ They kem out together” was all the
gossips could be sure of.
* * • *
Mick’s visits to the township grew more
frequent, and the horse was kept constantly in
the homestead stable ready for Mrs. Frances
Mulleeran to drive down to fetch home her drink-
sodden husband.
But she never complained. On the contrary,
she seemed more contented as Mick sunk lower
and lower. She was 35 and still fascinatingly
beautiful.
Mick’s business “beyant” eventually assumed
such proportions that he engaged an overseer to
look after things on the homestead. The overseer
was handsome, cultured, and compelling. He
found his affinity. After a week’s uninterrupted
spree, Mick crawled home and found his wife had
gone. The overseer had also gone.
Father M‘Gooey sent the children away to a
convent-school, and Mick drank harder than ever.
In less than three months he received legal
notice to quit the homestead of Frances Mulleeran.
Father O’Dale doubted the legality of the pro-
cedure, and suggested that Mick should come with
him to the city to consult a lawyer.
“Look here, yer riverence, isn’t this her own
name on the dokkymint—Mishtress Frances
Mulleeran ? ”
“ Yes ; but, my poor dear man ” began the
priest.
“Then, be jabers!” said Mick, “there’ll be no
damned lawyers interfarin’ wid her intintions, if
Mick Mulleeran knows it.”
And there were not. A week later they found
Mick floating face-down wards among the rushes
by the edge of the wash-pool.
Tas.
JOHN DRAYMAN.
